He had four eggs, a tin of beans, the end of a white loaf, and a packet of bacon gone slightly the wrong side of its date that he held up to the bulb and inspected and pronounced, with the gravity of a vet at a vetting, "fine."

"That's green, that," said Pip, from inside her sleeping bag, just her face out.

"It's smoked."

"It's not smoked. It's *off.*"

"Smoked and off are the same colour, Sallow, you've led a sheltered life." He laid it in the pan and it spat and the whole box filled up with the smell of it, fat and salt and woodsmoke off the stove, and my stomach turned over, and Pip came out of the bag like a creature out of a burrow, because that's the thing about being eighteen and worked sixty hours a week on toast: no one's principles survive bacon.

We ate off three plates between three of us and it didn't matter. The radio — Curtis's, ancient, ran off six batteries it ate like sweets — was on a station out of nowhere playing songs from before any of us were born, and the rain hammered the tin, and Curtis told the story he told once a week about the time a livery's husband had turned up to the yard drunk to fight Caro over a vet's bill and Caro had taken his arm, very gently, and walked him to his car talking the whole time in a voice nobody could hear, and put him in it, and he'd driven off, and paid in full the next morning, and never come back. "Rode him," Curtis said, with his mouth full. "Straight into the box. Didn't raise her voice. I've seen her do it to a stallion. Same face."

"She does it to everyone," I said, before I'd thought about it.

Curtis looked at me. He had a way of looking that took longer than you wanted. "Aye," he said. "She does." And he put more bacon on my plate, which I hadn't asked for, and I ate it, because I always ate it, because some part of me will always be the kid who eats fast in case there isn't more.

---

That was the warmth. I want it down before I get to the rest, because the rest is true as well, and the order people tell things in is a kind of lie. They tell you the warmth so the cold lands harder, or they tell you the cold so the warmth looks like a trap. It was both at once and neither one was a setup. We were three skint kids in a tin box being happy. That happened. Nobody made it happen to use me. It just also happened to be the thing that made me impossible to get out of there, and both of those are facts, and I've stopped trying to make one of them the realer one.

Pip's whole life that month was the mare.

Quillan. Five, chestnut, a face on her like she'd just heard a noise you couldn't, every minute of every day — sharp, electric, the kind of young horse that makes good riders go careful and bad riders go to hospital. Pip had backed her, had done the months of long-reining and lunging and standing-in-the-rain that no one films, had been the one to first sit on her and not come off, and she was producing her up the little classes with a patience that was the best thing about Pip, who was the best of us. She talked about that mare the way you'd talk about a kid you were proud of and frightened for at once.

"She's going to be something," Pip said, that Friday, knees up under the sleeping bag, plate balanced on them. "She jumped the spread today in the school — Dev, you saw — she didn't think about it, she just *went,* she's got more scope than Beau ever had—"

"More than Beau's had a hot dinner," said Curtis.

"—and if I can keep her sound and keep her sweet and get her round a few of the bigger young-horse classes, then—" and she stopped, because the *then* was the dangerous part, the part you don't say out loud in a static in case the cold gets it. The *then* was: a record on the mare, a name on Pip, a front door cracked open into a sport her family had bred into her and could no longer pay for. The *then* was a life. I knew the shape of it because I had one too, a *then,* and mine had four white socks of nothing and a star dragged off-centre and was at that minute standing in a box across the yard with one ear out for the rain.

I didn't say any of that. I said, "She'll be something," and meant it, and Pip went luminous, the way she did, easily, like a kid, and I clocked — I couldn't help it, I clock things, it's the whole of what I am — that Pip had *paid* for that mare. Years of it. Pony Club and the right hat and the lessons her mum had gone without things to afford, the long correct ladder of the horsey poor, every rung of it bought. And I'd been handed the gift for nothing on a riding-school cob too dangerous to charge anyone for, free, an accident of the way I'm made, no more earned than the colour of my eyes.

The unfairness of it ran both ways and I never once landed it on her, and it cost me something not to, and it cost her more to be decent about it the other way, and we never spoke of it, and that was as close as Pip and I ever got to the truth, which is closer than most.

---

Curtis put it the way Curtis put things, which is to say he made it a joke so it wouldn't count.

He was scraping the pan, batteries dying in the radio so the song slowed and deepened like the singer was being dragged under, and he said, not looking at me, "So what's the deal, then. With you."

"What deal."

"Your deal. Here. On paper, like." He turned the pan over, knocked it out on the bin. "I get the tin when she remembers. Pip's mum and dad cover her livery so she's near enough paying to be here, no offence, Sallow—"

"None taken, we're skint, I know we're skint—"

"—and I'm here cause it's a bed and Hereford's worse. But you. You came off a bus from up north for this. So what's it say on your paper."

The rain. The slow drowning song. Pip very still in her bag, watching me, the way she watched a horse she wasn't sure of.

"There's no paper," I said.

I heard it land. I'd not said it out loud before, to anyone, and out loud in the tin box with the two of them looking at me it sounded different than it sounded in my own head, where it had always sounded like *trust,* like *she gave me her word,* like the warmest thing anyone had ever done. Out loud it just sounded like *there's no paper.*

"It's her word," I said. "Board, coaching, and — eventually — a horse to compete. Mine to ride. That's how it's done. That's how she came up. You work and you wait and you earn the ride." I was quoting her. I could hear myself quoting her and I couldn't stop, the way you can't stop a horse that's set its jaw, you just go where it's going. "She coached for Great Britain. People pay a fortune for an hour of what I'm getting for—"

"For free," said Curtis.

"For the *thing itself,*" I said, and even I heard it then, the daft grandeur of it, in a tin box, off a green bacon plate.

Curtis didn't laugh. That was the thing. Curtis laughed at everything — it was his job, he was the one who said the cruel true thing as a joke so it didn't draw blood — and he didn't laugh. He looked at the dead radio for a second and then he said, quiet, in the burr that got broader when he meant it, "Slave with a saddle, that is."

He'd said it before. Day two, he'd said it — *working pupil, that's posh for slave with a saddle* — and I'd laughed, we'd all laughed, it was funny, it was Curtis. He said it now and nobody laughed, and that's how I knew it had stopped being a joke, that somewhere between day two and this Friday it had quietly turned into the truth, and Curtis had carried it the whole way knowing, the way he knew everything, and chosen the moment to set it down where I'd feel it.

"It's not like that," I said.

"No," said Curtis. "Course not." And he put the kettle on, which was his way of taking it back, except he hadn't taken it back, none of us thought he'd taken it back, and Pip was looking at the floor of the caravan like there was something written on it.

It was right then — I'd swear to this, and I've no way to prove it and every reason to have made it up after — that I heard the gravel outside, the particular squelch-and-grit of someone crossing the yard in the wet, and through the weeping window I saw the torch-beam jump, and it was Glenys, going from the feed room to the Hall with a hurricane lamp because half the lights still didn't work, and she didn't stop, didn't look up at the lit caravan window, didn't do a single thing — and I knew, the way you know a horse has heard a thing two fields off by the one ear that's gone, that she'd heard us. That the window was open a crack at the top for the stove, and the words had gone out into the wet, *there's no paper, slave with a saddle,* and Glenys had walked through them in the dark and said nothing, and kept walking, the lamp swinging, and that her saying nothing was a whole sentence I couldn't read yet.

I file things. I filed that.

---

I should tell you about Saracen jumping, because that's the part I can still feel in my hands.

It was the next morning, the Saturday, the rain gone through to a high washed sky and the school still too wet to mean anything but Caro saying *put a fence up for him, just a cross-pole, see what he does* — which was not a thought and not a question, it never was, but for once it was a thing I wanted more than my next breath, so the leg she put on didn't even register as a leg.

I'd jumped him nothing yet. We'd done the groundwork, the long weeks of teaching the right side of his body that the right side didn't have to mean a man hauling on a curb till the world went red. We'd done the flatwork, the going-quiet, the half-stride. I'd got on and stayed and we'd ridden round the school on the buckle with the rain on the tarp and his near ear tipped back to me the whole time, one ear for me, one for the world, which is the only love letter that horse ever wrote anyone and I got every one of them.

But I'd not pointed him at anything.

I built it small. A cross-pole, two foot of nothing, a fence you'd trot a child over. I built it small because of the scar, because of the not-knowing, because somewhere in his past was a fence and a hand and a reason he flinched at poles like they'd bite, and I'd rather have built it on the ground than have him think, even for a stride, that I'd brought him to a place to be hurt.

He saw it from the corner. The off ear went hard forward. The near ear stayed with me. He didn't run out — the left was wide open, the run-out there for the taking, his whole life had taught him to take it — and he didn't. He came down the long side in a canter that got slower the closer it got, not faster, careful and asking, and three strides out the flicker started, and I gave him the whole right side of his mouth and waited the half-stride longer than his fear wanted, *for him,* and he blew out and decided to trust the next ten seconds.

And he jumped it like it was the high wall at Hickstead.

Two foot of cross-pole and he snapped his front legs up to his chin, neat, tucked, careful past all reason, the form of a horse who'd been hit once for touching a pole and had decided, somewhere in the ruin of him, never to touch one again as long as he lived. He didn't jump it big to show off. He jumped it small and frightened and *perfect,* spending something he had almost none of, and he landed soft and his near ear came straight back to me, *was that right, was that all right, did I do it,* and I couldn't say anything for a second.

I'm not going to be clever about this part. I've been clever about everything else and I'll be clever about plenty more, it's how I get through. But not this.

He tried so hard. That's all. He was frightened of the whole world and he tried so hard, for me, for two foot of pole, because I'd asked, and I patted his neck and it was wet under my hand already and I said, plain, out loud, in the empty school with just the two of us and the rain on the roof, "Good. *Good.* You're so good." And I meant it the way you mean the only true thing you've ever said.

That a horse like that existed. That he'd give you that. I didn't know yet what it cost him. I thought it was a gift. It was a gift. It was also him spending the last of something nobody had ever put back, and I'd find that out, and I'd keep asking anyway, which is the whole of what I have to confess.

---

Caro was at the gallery rail. I hadn't heard her come in. She watched the whole of it from the shadows with her hands half-closed over the loose ring, and when I rode him down to a walk and let the reins go long she came down the steps slow — her knees were going, you could see it cost her — and stood at his head, not too close, she knew not to crowd him, and looked at him a long time.

"He's careful," she said.

"He is."

"Frighteningly careful." She put a hand up, slow, and he let her touch his neck, which two months ago would've had her through the wall, and she felt the wet of him and looked at her own wet hand and something crossed her face. "A careful horse with that scope is the rarest thing there is, Devlin. They'll kill themselves being brave, the bold ones, they leave a leg and they're gone. But a careful one—" She stopped. She wiped her hand on her coat. "A careful one with an engine like that."

I thought she was praising him. She was praising him. She was also, in the same breath, with the same warmth, in the same voice, doing the sum, and I know that now and I didn't know it then and the not-knowing-then is the only innocence I've got left to mourn.

"Hartpury's the twenty-fifth," she said. "You'll take him to Hartpury, I think." And she went up the school to the gate without waiting to see what I thought, because it was not a thought and not a question, it never was, and there was already a green-and-white entry form face-down on the kitchen table when I went in for lunch, with my name and his on it in her hand, *D. Okafor-Lyle / Saracen,* the season's first clock started, the bell not rung yet but the hand on it.

I looked at our two names in her writing on the same line and I felt — I'm not going to lie about this either — I felt *kept.* My name and the horse's, together, on a real form, going to a real show. I thought it meant something it didn't mean. I thought a form was a kind of paper.

---

It was late when I walked back up.

The lads were already in the caravan, the window lit yellow, the radio going on its last legs of battery. I'd stayed down to do the late check, run my hands down four legs in the cold for heat and filling, throw a last haynet, stand a minute at Saracen's box with my thumb on the scar while he ate, the way I'd started to without deciding to, the place the book of him was torn out, the page no one could read. He didn't lift his head. He let me stand there. He'd not have let me, in March. The off-hind rested, the white coronet pale in the dark, the near ear out for me even with his nose in the net. I told him about Hartpury. I told him it'd be all right. I told him a lot of things I had no business promising, in the plain voice, the one I only use on him, and then I latched the door — gently, you never bang a door on that horse — and turned to go up the hill.

And I stopped, because of the Hall.

There was one light on in the whole dead house. The kitchen. The warm room, the only room they heated, gold in the window at the cold end of the yard. And Caro was at the table.

I could see her from where I stood, fifty yards off in the dark, and she couldn't see me, and I should've gone up the hill and I didn't, I stood and watched, because of her face.

She had papers in front of her. A lot of them. The chequebook, and brown envelopes with windows in them, the kind that don't bring good news to anyone, and a calculator from a pound shop that didn't belong in that house, and she was going down a column with a pen, and her face—

I'd never seen it. In two months I'd seen her ride a drunk into his car and a stallion into a float and a Sheffield boy into a handshake, all with the one face, the certain one, the one that had no give in it anywhere. And here it was off. She didn't know anyone could see. She'd taken it off the way you take off boots that have hurt all day, and underneath it she was just an old woman alone in a cold house at a table covered in bills she couldn't pay, with a pen that wasn't moving, looking at a number, and she was frightened, and she was tired in a way that went down past tired into something I didn't have a name for at nineteen and have plenty of names for now, and for one second — one — she put her hand over her mouth like a person keeping a sound in.

Then she took it down. And picked up the pen. And went back to the column.

I didn't understand it. That's the truth. I stood in the dark and watched the richest person I'd ever met be more frightened than my mother on a bad morning, and I filed it, because I file everything, but I couldn't read it. I didn't have the figures or the dates or the word *October* yet. I just had a lit window and an old woman's hand over her mouth, and I took it up the hill to the warm tin box and lay in the bunk and couldn't make it mean anything. I could read every horse in that yard down to the flicker under an eye. The thing in the lit window had no body to read. I lay forty yards away, fed and warm and glad, and I never heard a hoof strike the wall once that night, because the horse had gone quiet, and I thought that meant we were winning.


---

# Chapter Six — What Caro Saw

I'd seen the figures on the table the night before, through the lit kitchen window, and the face she'd worn over them — and I'd filed it the way I file everything, in the place where I keep the things I can't read yet. A column of numbers and Caro's pen not moving. Her mouth gone slack the way old people's faces go when they think no one's looking, all the holding-up let off at once. I'd stood in the cold by the muck heap and watched a woman do sums that weren't coming out, and then I'd gone to bed and not thought about it, because the next morning was the morning I was going to ride Saracen properly for the first time, outdoors, over a course, and there is no room in a nineteen-year-old for two big things at once.

So. The numbers waited. They always wait. That's the thing about money, it never goes off; it just sits there going up.

The morning came up clear and cold and still, which it almost never did that spring, a sky washed thin and high and the red earth gone hard with a last frost, and I tacked him in the box in the half-dark with my hands knowing the way now, the noseband, the running martingale that he let me do up without flinging his head — he hadn't, at the start; at the start he'd have had me through the wall for it — and I led him out across the yard and the frost cracked under his big feet and his breath came up white round both our heads, and I want to say it plainly because I've spent ten years learning that this is the part I have to say plainly or I lie about all the rest:

I was happy. He walked out beside me into the cold and he was mine to ride and I was happy clean through.

---

I'd schooled him in the indoor up to then, off the right rein, in the leaking grey light with the tarp cracking overhead, the two of us learning each other where no one watched. The outdoor arena was different. Open on three sides. The Welsh hills laid out blue and bare behind the far rail, a gateway he couldn't see past at the bottom, the whole big morning around him with nothing to hold its edges. A frightened horse reads a space the way you'd read a room you've been hurt in before — where are the exits, where's the wall I can't get behind. I felt him take it in through his back the second his feet hit the sand. The poll came up. The off ear flicked at the gateway and stayed there, hard.

I didn't ask him for anything. That's the whole of it, the only trick I've ever had — I didn't ask. I let him walk the whole track on a loose rein, on the buckle, twice round, three times, while he looked at the gateway and the hills and the far rail and decided, fence by fence, post by post, that none of it was going to come and get him. By the fourth circuit his back had come up under me, soft, swinging, and he blew out and gave me the near ear off the gateway. One for me, one for the job.

I picked up a contact, the right rein too, even and soft, and felt the old flinch start up his neck — and gave with it, the half-stride longer than my nerve wanted, and he waited it out *for me* and came onto the bit round and through, the flinch going off his neck like a wave going back off sand.

We jumped a cross-pole. Then an upright, two foot, nothing. He met it on a forward stride and didn't get bold — he never got bold — he got careful, snapped his front legs up neat to his chin, tucked, apologetic, the form of a horse that's been hit for touching a pole and has decided, privately, never to touch one again. He cleared it by a foot it didn't need and landed soft and waited to be told it was all right.

It was all right. I told him. I told him out loud, *good, good lad, that's it,* the way no one in my life had ever told me anything was all right when I'd done a thing right, the way you give a horse the thing you never got, which is maybe the only honest reason any of us do this at all.

And then we just — went. I put the small fences up to a course in my head and rode them in a flow, a related distance down the long side that walked a forward four and I let him roll down it forward, an oxer off the turn, the upright on the diagonal, round again, and the whole way the near ear stayed tipped back to me and the off ear stayed forward on the next fence and his back swung and his breath came easy and he was, for the first time in the seven years he'd been alive and frightened, *with* somebody. Not enduring a rider. With one.

I won't say it was like flying. People say that about horses and it isn't, it's heavier than that and better. It's like being trusted by something that has every reason not to. There's nothing in the world that touches it. I've chased that ten years and never got it back and I want to be honest that I knew, even then, even at nineteen with the frost still on the rail, that I was inside the best thing that was ever going to happen to me. You don't always know. That time I knew.

---

When I brought him back to a walk and dropped the rein and let him stretch his neck down long, blowing, the steam coming off his near-black neck in the cold like he was a thing on fire that didn't hurt — I looked up and the rail was full of people.

They'd come without being called. That's how I knew it was real, before anyone said a word — Glenys was there with a haynet still in one hand, forgotten, and Pip up on the gate with her chin on the top bar, and Curtis leaning with his arms folded doing the not-impressed face that meant he was very impressed, and two of the livery women who paid to keep their horses here and mostly looked through us like we were part of the muck-heap furniture, and they were all stood along the outdoor rail in the cold watching a horse no one could ride go round like a different animal. Word travels in a yard faster than it has any right to. Somebody'd seen out a window and told somebody and the whole of Tatefield had drifted down to the rail to watch the rogue go quiet.

Pip was the one who said it. "Dev." Just my name, breathed, like it was a swear word in church. Her broad freckled face was doing a thing I didn't have the kindness, then, to look at properly — she rode correct and brave and without one ounce of what I'd just been handed for free, and she was looking at me with her whole heart, glad for me, only glad, and I clocked it and looked away from it because I was nineteen and full of myself and the glad of someone you're not going to choose is a hard thing to hold and I didn't hold it. I just rode past her on the loose rein with the horse blowing and I was, God forgive me, showing off.

Curtis unfolded his arms. "Bloody hell," he said, which from Curtis was a sonnet.

Glenys didn't say anything. She stood with the haynet hanging and watched the horse, not me, the horse, the way she watched all of them, like she was already saying goodbye to him in some part of herself that had said goodbye to a lot of horses over thirty years and knew the shape of it coming. I didn't read that then. I read it later, going back over the morning in my head the way you do, turning it, finding the thing in it you missed while you were busy being happy.

And at the gate, where she'd been the whole time and I hadn't seen her — Caro.

---

I'd been trained, by then, to read her. Three weeks of it. I read her the way I read a horse, by the hands first — the loose signet ring, the half-closed fists she kept so you'd never see a thing fall off her, the way her weight sat. And I'd learned to know when the praise was coming because something in her went still just before it, the stillness of a thing taking aim.

She was very still at the gate.

I rode him over. Walked him right up to her on the buckle, blowing, the off ear coming forward to look at her and the near ear staying back on me, and I was waiting for it, I won't pretend I wasn't, I was nineteen and I'd just done the best thing I'd ever done and I wanted it from her the way you want it from the one person whose word does something to you — and she gave it.

"That was beautifully ridden," Caro said.

And it was the right words and it was warm, real warmth, I'd swear to it then and I'd swear to it now, her eyes were wet at the corners in the cold and her voice when it came out had a crack of something true in it — but I was looking at her face while she said the warm thing, and for half a second, while the mouth said *beautifully ridden,* the eyes did something else. They went off me, and off the warm, and down the horse — down his neck and his shoulder and his big quarters and his clean careful legs — the way you'd look at a thing in a saleyard. The way you read a horse off a lorry. Not at me. Not at the thing that had just happened between a frightened animal and a frightened boy in the cold. At the animal. At the legs. At the engine she'd bought as a yearling and never been able to read the rest of the manual on, and that some Sheffield kid had just, in front of witnesses, started up.

It was there and it was gone. Half a second. A woman doing sums.

I didn't know what it meant. I caught it and filed it with the numbers on the table and the slack mouth in the lamplight, and I had no shape to fit it to — nobody had ever looked at me happy and seen money — so I let the warm part wash it away, because I wanted the warm part, I was starving for it, and a starving thing doesn't inspect the bread. I have turned that half-second over for ten years and I cannot tell you which was real, the wet eyes or the sums, and the not being able to tell is the whole of what she taught me.

"He's a different horse," Caro said. The warm was back, all the way, you'd never have known it had gone anywhere. "I'd given up on that engine, you know. I bought him on his looks and the looks were all he was. And you've —" She stopped. She put her ruined beautiful hand on his neck, the one that had held a thousand frightened mouths soft, and he let her, because I was on him, because he was quiet under me and the quiet held the both of us, and that's a thing I think about too: that she could touch him because of what I'd done to him, that my gentling him was already, that fast, a thing being used by somebody, and I didn't see it, I was glad she could touch him, I was glad I'd made him touchable. "You've found the horse under the rogue," she said. "Do you know what that's worth?"

She said it as good news. She meant it as good news, I think — I'll always think *I think* about Caro, it's the truest grammar I have for her. To her it was the best news in the world, the yard's whole hope coming up green and sound in a frozen arena. She was telling me we'd done something marvellous.

"He's not for sale," I said.

It came out before I'd decided to say it, the Sheffield in me, the same place the *and the wage* had come from on the first day, the part of me that hears the soft thing and clocks the hard thing under it — and Caro laughed, light, easy, the laugh she used like a hand on a rein, and said, "Darling, everything's for sale. That's not a sad thing. That's just the only true thing in this whole business. The art is in *when.*" And she patted his neck once more and stepped back off the rail and looked at me with all the warmth in the world and said, "You've an enormous amount of work to do before anyone need think about any of that. Hartpury first. We'll see if he can do it with a crowd and a bell. One thing at a time."

*Anyone need think about any of that.* I heard *not yet.* I should've heard *when.*

---

The yard broke up off the rail. The livery women drifted back to their own horses with something to talk about; Curtis went to get the next one in; Pip stayed on the gate a minute longer than she needed to and then climbed down and didn't say anything else, just touched Saracen's nose on her way past, soft, the way you'd touch a thing you weren't going to be allowed to keep either. I should've read Pip better that whole season. I read horses better than I read the people who loved me, which is a sentence I could put over the door of my whole life.

Glenys came over while I was loosening the girth, the haynet still in her hand.

She stood at his head and let him breathe at her, and she looked at him a long time, and her walnut face didn't do anything at all, which from Glenys was a face full of weather. She put her free hand flat on his cheek, on the off side, the frightened side, and the horse let her, blowing, the near ear still half-back to me even on the ground, even untacking, even now — and Glenys said, low, more to the horse than to me, "There's clever, isn't he. There's clever, Sass." She always called him Sass. I never did. To me he was only ever Saracen, three whole syllables, the name you give a thing you've decided to take seriously.

Then she looked up at me over his nose, and she opened her mouth.

I saw her open it. I saw her get the breath for it. There was a thing coming, the same thing she'd nearly said the first night on the milk crate — *mind yourself, they'll want a lot off you* — but bigger this time, the whole of it, I could see the whole of it sitting behind her teeth, the true sentence she carried around the yard for thirty years and gave out maybe once a decade because it cost her every time to say it and changed nothing every time she did.

And she didn't say it.

She shut her mouth. She looked at the horse and she looked at me and she did the sum herself, faster than Caro, harder than Caro, the sum of what telling me would cost and what it would buy, which was nothing, which was a boy who wouldn't believe her and would think less of her and would muck out tomorrow either way — and she folded it back up and put it away, and what came out instead was the small worn version, the one she could afford.

"Mind yourself, love," Glenys said. "That's all."

"You keep saying that."

"Aye." She slung the haynet over her shoulder. "And you keep not." And she went off across the frosted cobbles toward the feed room, square and slow and immovable, a woman who'd seen exactly how this went and had decided, decades ago, that seeing it was all she was going to get to do — because she had her own old age and her own thirty years sunk in this drowning place, and you can name the whole con out loud and still hand the kid the fork, because you need him to muck out, because there's nowhere else for any of you to go.

I thought she was just a woman who said *mind yourself* a lot. I ignored it.

---

I took my time untacking him, on purpose, in the last of the light. The others had gone in. The arena had emptied and gone blue with the dusk coming down off the hills and the cold sharpening up again for the night, and it was just me and the horse and the steam still ghosting off his back where the saddle had been, the sweat dried dark on the near-black to a salt edge, the scar on his near shoulder showing pale where the hair had never grown back, the hand's-width of pale that nobody alive could explain — a gate, a kick, a man, a fall, the page torn out of him before Tatefield, the one part of him I could never read because nobody could. My thumb found it the way it always found it, without me deciding, and rested there.

He stood for me. He stood quiet in the dusk with one back foot tipped and the white coronet on the off-hind catching the last of the light, and he was tired in the good way, the worked-and-trusted way, and his near ear was tipped back to me even now, even with nothing left to listen for, just back, just on me, the way it had been the whole bright morning.

I'm not good at saying what came up in me then. I've tried. There aren't the words I'm comfortable with — they're soft words, and I keep the soft words off, mostly, they've never done me any good — but I'll say the one true thing, which is that I leaned my head against his neck where the sweat had dried, against the big warm weight of him in the cold, and I said it out loud, quiet, just for the two of us, the most dangerous sentence I ever said in my life:

"We're going to do something, you and me."

His off ear came forward at a sound in the yard, alert, reading the world. His near ear stayed back. On me. Listening, the way he'd decided he could, because I'd taught him he could, because I was the steadiest thing in the field and I'd given with the right rein the half-second the fear came and he'd trusted me back, half a ton of frightened animal trusting one boy in the dark, and I thought it was the cleanest thing I'd ever done.

Across the yard the Hall kitchen window came up yellow.

I could see her in it, small at that distance, a thin upright shape against the lamplight — Caro, standing, not sitting at the table now, standing by the window with her back half to it, the loose-fisted hand up at her ear. On the telephone. Talking to somebody, in the warm lit room, at the end of the cold day, the night of the morning the rogue went clear. I didn't think anything of it. People make calls. I had a horse's ear tipped back to me and the whole season in front of us and the best thing in the world dried to salt on the side of my face, and I watched my breath and his go up white together into the dark, and I did not wonder, not for one second, who she was ringing, or what she was saying, or that I'd not learn the answer for months — that on the night I was promising the horse the future, she was already, gently, in the warm, beginning to sell it.

He blew out, long and low. I did up the headcollar. The near ear stayed back on me all the way to the box.


---

# Chapter Seven — First Ring

The night before Hartpury I screwed studs into his shoes by torchlight on the caravan step, and I have thought since that if you wanted to understand the whole season you could've stood at the top hedge and watched me do it.

It was a wet April and the going at Hartpury would be holding, soft on top with a grab underneath, the kind of ground that takes a horse's foot and keeps it half a beat too long — and a horse who's already frightened of being held does not need the earth doing it to him as well. So you stud up. You tap the holes clean, you wind in the bigger square ones for mud, off-hind first because that's the foot most horses snatch from you and Saracen would snatch from anyone, and you do it kneeling in the cold with the horse's leg between yours and the stud-spanner going stiff and the torch in your teeth, putting grip into him so he can hold the ground.

I knelt on the wet step and didn't think one thing about it being anything but care. Care was all it was, that night. I want that down before the rest of it.

Curtis leaned in the doorway eating toast and told me I'd lose half of them in the mud and I told him to come and hold the torch then if he was so clever, and he didn't, and Pip came out in everything she owned and held the off-hind up for me when Saracen got tired of standing three-legged for a kid he could've put through the wall, and the horse let her, because I was at his head, because the quiet I'd built held the both of us, and that was the warmest I'd ever been in my life, kneeling in the rain with a half-ton frightened animal letting two skint teenagers screw grip into his feet for a show none of us could really afford the diesel to get to.

We loaded at four. He went up the ramp the second time of asking, which for him was a miracle, and which I'd spent three weeks of mornings teaching him by never once making the box a place he couldn't leave. The lorry was Tatefield's old box that drank diesel like Curtis's radio drank batteries, and Caro had done the sums on whether it was worth the fuel for a 1.20 at Hartpury and decided it was, which I took as faith in me and the horse and which was, I understand now, the first entry in a longer ledger — what the petrol cost against what a placing at Hartpury would put on him. You don't get a price for a horse who's never been in a ring. You get a price for one who's been seen.

I rode shotgun the four motorways with my forehead on the cold glass, M50 onto the M5, the country opening out flat and grey, and behind the cab partition I could hear him shift his weight and set his feet against the corners, and every so often I'd say something to him through the gap, nothing, *all right, we're all right,* and the shifting would settle. Caro drove. Marnie sat between us with Beau's plaiting kit on her knees, not talking, biting the skin at the side of her thumb raw, and I clocked that and didn't know yet what it was. I thought she was carsick.

The clock that day was a simple one and I knew it before the bell ever rang. Caro had said it at the gate three weeks back, light, like a thought — *we'll see if he can do it with a crowd and a bell* — and that was the whole question of the day, the one the course builder hadn't set and didn't need to: not whether the horse could jump, I knew he could jump, I'd felt the scope of him snap up under me on the frozen frost mornings. Whether he could jump *here.* In the noise. Trapped in a place full of strangers and tannoy and a collecting ring like a chute, with everything closing in on the one horse in England who'd been taught that closing-in meant pain with no way out.

## *

Hartpury came up out of the rain as a field of lorries.

That's the first thing about a show that nobody puts in the photos — it's a car park before it's anything else, acres of them, the great gleaming wagons with living quarters and ramps that came down on hydraulics and grooms in matching fleeces, and ours, the old Tatefield box with the rust coming through the green and a wing mirror held on with gaffer tape, nosed in at the cheap end between two outfits that each cost more than the Hall. I clocked it the way I clock everything. The cost of the lorries. The cost of the boots coming down the ramps, the navy ones with the patent tops that go for the price of a month's rent, on the feet of girls my age who'd never screwed a stud in by torchlight in the wet because there was always somebody to do it. I wasn't bitter about it. I want to be clear about that, because the easy thing is to be bitter and the bitter version is a lie. I was *interested.* I read it the way I read a horse, for information, for where the weight sat and where the soft place was, and the information was: this is a world where the money does the standing-still and the working is done out of sight, and I had walked into it through the working door, and most of these people would never know there was a working door because they'd only ever come in the front.

I got him off the box and the ground came up at him wrong straight away — too much sky, too many edges, a tannoy somewhere over his poll going off mid-sentence about a class in ring four — and his head came up and the off ear locked and I felt, through the lead rope, his whole back set against the size of it. I didn't haul on him. I never haul on him. I walked him a slow square at the quiet end of the lorry park, on the buckle, in among our own diesel and rust where it smelled of us, until the strangeness had edges he could find, and Glenys — who'd come up separate in the car with the tack because there wasn't room and somebody had to — leaned on the fender and watched and said, "There's a lot of show in him to come yet, isn't there," which was Glenys for *he's scared* and *good luck* and *I've seen this before* all in three words, and I said, "He'll do it," and she said, "Aye, he'll do it. Question's what it'll take out of him," and went to fetch the saddle.

I heard the *he'll do it.* You hear what you're hungry for.

## *

The collecting ring at Hartpury is a chute, and a chute is the one thing in the world Saracen cannot abide.

It's a fenced corridor of churned ground beside the main ring where you warm up over a practice fence, twenty horses circling in a space built for ten, and the etiquette of it is a whole liturgy of its own — you pass left to left, you call *heads up* before you jump, you wait your turn at the practice oxer while the grooms put the rails up and down — and the politics of it is older and uglier than the etiquette. Because the practice fence belongs to whoever's got the most standing, and standing is money and a known name, and a working pupil on a horse nobody's heard of waits. I knew that before I went in. You learn it in an afternoon. You fetch the poles. You give the fence up. You circle wide and keep your scared horse off the chute of crowd at the rail and you watch the big names get the oxer when they want it, and you say nothing, because you're nineteen and nobody and the round is the only argument you've got.

He was bad in there. I won't pretty it. The rail on the long side was three deep with people, a wall of coats and faces he couldn't see past, and the space funnelled them toward him every time the circle brought him round, and twice he tried to nap off it, to swing left and away, *always left,* away from a pain laid on him before I was born to him — and twice I had to do the thing, off the right rein, give the right hand forward the inch the second the flinch came, *do I have to do this alone,* and be the steadiest thing in a field that was anything but, while a girl on a fifty-thousand-pound mare cut across my line and didn't see me because people like her have never once in their lives had to see people like me.

And then — three strides off the practice oxer, the first time I asked him to actually jump in that boiling chute — he went still.

The stillness is the thing only I read. Everyone else thinks a quiet horse is a calm one. He goes the opposite way from loud. He went still and he stopped blinking and the muscle under the right eye flickered, that small fast tic, there and gone, and to anyone watching it was a horse settling and to me it was the held breath before the bolt, the whole of him asking the only question he ever asks: *do I do this on my own, or are you here.*

I was there. I gave him the half-stride. I sat dead still and let the rein go soft and waited longer than my own nerve wanted, longer than the chute wanted, longer than the impatient queue behind me wanted — and he waited it with me, *for me,* and then he blew out, long and low through his nose, the flutter, the sound of a horse deciding to trust the next ten seconds, and the near ear came off the wall of coats and tipped back. To me. And he jumped the oxer careful and neat, snapped his front legs up to his chin the apologetic way, cleared it by the foot it didn't need, and landed soft and waited to be told it was all right.

I told him it was all right. Out loud, in the chute, in front of all of them, *good lad, good, good lad,* and a couple of faces turned because you don't talk to a horse like that out loud, it's soft, it's not done, and I didn't care. I'd give him the thing I never got. That's the only honest reason any of us do this.

A man was watching from the rail. I clocked him the way you clock a still thing in a moving crowd — middle-aged, a good plain waxed coat, a phone held low and level at the rail, not up taking a selfie like the rest but *level,* steady, the way you hold it when you're filming. He wasn't looking at me. He was looking at the horse. At the careful snap of those front legs, at the scope coming up off the ground. I didn't know him from Adam and I never learned his name, and it didn't matter, because the clip he took at that rail went onto a phone and from that phone onto another and the market that I didn't know was a market started, that morning, to wake up and look at my horse. I felt none of it. I felt the ear tipped back. That was all the world I had.

## *

Marnie went into the small class before us, on Beau, and I watched it from the lorry because I'd learned by then to watch the people who were going to matter to me and I'd already half-clocked that she was going to matter, the way you half-clock weather.

Beau is twelve and a gentleman and the yard's one made horse, a grey schoolmaster gone stiff in the hocks who'd carry a frightened child round a 1.10 in his sleep and had done, for years, for everyone, the way you use up a kind old horse. And Marnie was frightened. You could see it before she even went in — she went white around the mouth, that specific bloodless white that's nothing to do with the cold, and her hands got loud, came up off the wither and started doing things, fiddling, taking and giving and taking, the opposite of quiet, and old Beau felt it and got anxious in his honest old way, because a horse can't help reading you any more than I can help reading them, and the difference between us is I read on purpose and the horse just *catches* it, like a cold.

She had a stop. Not a bad one — Beau wouldn't give you a bad one, he just put the brakes on at a little filler, a planks with an advertising board under it, ears flicking back at her loud hands, four faults and the gut-drop of it, and she got him round the rest of it green and tense and clear of further trouble, and came out, and her grandmother said, "He backed off the planks, you let him," not cruel, just *true,* the worst kind, said the way you'd note the going was soft — and then Caro's eyes went off Marnie entirely and came to find me at the warm-up rail, to find the horse, to check on the asset, and I watched Marnie watch that happen. I watched her grandmother's attention cross her like a searchlight crossing a swimmer and go straight past to the council kid on the rogue.

She didn't say anything to me. She put Beau away herself, did the buckles slow, the way you do something with your hands when you can't do anything with your face, and when she finally looked up at me across the lorry park there was something in it I didn't have the kit to read yet, the thing that's resentment and want and shame all run together so you can't tell which is which — the same currency I'd later learn I had no idea how to count, on a girl who was, though I didn't know it then, the truest mirror of me in the whole valley.

"He's not even scared in there, your one," she said. Meaning Saracen, who was terrified, who had been terrified the whole warm-up, who was only standing quiet now because I'd spent three weeks teaching him a lie that his fear could be borne if he wasn't alone with it.

"He's the most scared horse here," I said. It came out before I'd thought about it, the truth coming up the way it does with me at the wrong moments.

She looked at me like she didn't believe me, or didn't want to, and then she looked at Saracen — really looked, at the white all round his eye even at rest, at the off ear locked on the tannoy, at the near shoulder where the pale scar showed that nobody alive could explain — and something went across her face. I didn't read it then. I read it later, the way I read everything later. She was looking at a scared thing being made to go into the ring anyway, and she knew exactly what that was, because she was one too, and the only difference between her and the horse was that nobody had ever screwed grip into Marnie's feet and stood at her head and given her the half-stride and told her, out loud, that it was all right.

## *

The bell went and I had forty-five seconds and the whole of it came down to nothing then, the way it always does — the lorries and the money and the boots and Marnie's white mouth all fell off the edge of the world and there was just the going under his feet and the off ear and the first fence already coming.

I'll not be clever about the round. There's a way of writing a round that makes it sound like music and it isn't, it's a frightened animal and a kid agreeing, fence by fence, to do a dangerous thing together, and the only true way to say it is plainly.

He spooked at the third. A liverpool, a tray of water under an oxer, blue and wrong and *down,* and horses don't spook at a liverpool for the height, they spook at the ground opening up under the fence, and his head came up and I felt the left run-out load into his shoulder, the swing of it, *away, away* — and the flicker came, three strides out, the stillness, the stopped blink, the tic under the right eye, the question. *Alone, or are you here.*

I was there. I gave the right rein, the inch, the half-stride. I sat still and asked for nothing but the staying, and I felt him decide — felt the back come up under me where it had been about to drop away, felt the blow-out come through his whole frame, *trust the next ten seconds* — and the near ear tipped back, and he stood off the liverpool a stride further than he wanted to and snapped those front legs up to his chin and cleared the blue tray by a margin no judge would ever measure, careful, neat, apologetic, landing soft, and the water never moved.

The rest was joy. I'll allow myself the word the once. The related distance down the long side walked a forward five and I rode it forward and he came down it bold for the first time in his life, *bold,* off the ground, the scope opening up, and the wall — stacked light blocks painted like masonry, a thing he couldn't see past, his worst kind of thing — I rode him at it dead straight off the right rein with my hand forward and the near ear back and he went up over it like the wall had never frightened anything, and we turned for the last, an upright, and he met it on a good stride and jumped it the way he jumped all of them, like a horse apologising in advance for a fault he was never going to make.

The bell. The clock stopped. Clear.

A clear round at a 1.20 at Hartpury on a wet Saturday is the smallest thing in the world. There were forty of them that day. But there was a sound off the rail when we landed over the last, a small ragged clap from the few who'd drifted over to see the near-black horse with the white eye that the word in the collecting ring had got round about, and it went into me hot and low the way Caro's praise went in, before I could vote on it, and I patted his neck so hard my hand stung and I was, God forgive me, grinning all over my stupid face, riding out past the in-gate with a rogue gone clear and the whole season in front of us and the rosette to come.

## *

We were placed. Fourth, as it happened, in the jump-off, which I didn't ride for time because I wasn't risking him flat and fast in a chute his first day — I went steady and double-clear and let the time put us where it put us, and Caro, who would have known to the second what cutting an inside turn would've bought, didn't say a word about it, which I took for trust and was, I think now, the asset being protected: you don't risk a horse who's getting a price, even for a rosette.

Fourth. The cruellest place, she always said about her own fourth, the Olympic one, the one she'd built seventy-one years around never being again — and here was her boy and her engine fourth at Hartpury and her face when she came to the lorry was not the cruel-place face at all, it was *lit,* lit up from somewhere old and starved, and she put her ruined hand on his neck, the hand I'd made it possible for her to put there, and she said, "He went round a 1.20 in a crowd in his first ring and never looked like stopping," and there was a crack of something true in her voice the same as there'd been in the frozen arena, real, I'd swear it, and underneath it, the same as then, the eyes going down his clean careful legs, reading him for what the clip and the clap and the rosette had just added to him.

The cheque, when it came, was thirty-odd pounds, and I worked it out against the diesel and the entry and the nominating and I told Curtis later that we'd lost money coming fourth and he said, "Course you have, that's the genius of it, isn't it, you pay them to take the horse off you eventually and you say thank you," and laughed, and I laughed, and the joke had a blade in it and I felt it go in a quarter-inch and pulled back out before it could mean anything, because I didn't want it to mean anything that day.

I led him back across the lorry park in the last of the wet light with the rosette in my fist, and the field of wagons was emptying, ramps coming up, the great gleaming outfits pulling out for yards I'd never see, and ours sat rusting at the cheap end waiting for us, and I tied him to the side of it loose and long the way he liked, where he could see out and never feel cornered, and I unwound the studs out of his feet kneeling in the churned mud — taking the grip back out of him now the job was done, off-hind first, the spanner stiff in the cold — and then I just stood with him while he came down off it.

He was blowing still, the breath going up in clouds, sweat dried dark to a salt rim on the near-black where the saddle had been, the wet conker shine of him gone matt with the work. I clipped the rosette onto the browband of his bridle the way you do, the red and the white of it daft against his big sensible Roman head, and he turned his nose into my chest and breathed at me, and the near ear, even now, even with the job long done and nothing left to listen for in the empty dusk, stayed tipped back. On me. The off ear swung to a tannoy crackling its last over the empty rings, reading the world the way he always read it — but the near one stayed home.

I put my hand flat on his wet neck. I didn't say anything clever. There wasn't anything clever to say. He'd gone into the worst place in the world for him, the crowd and the chute and the closing-in, and he'd done it because I'd asked him and because I'd taught him the asking was safe, and he'd come out the other side of his own fear and was standing now in the dark letting a council-estate kid lean on him, and the rosette on his head was the price tag I couldn't read yet and the trust under my hand was the only thing I could.

I stood there with him a long time. Across the lorry park, by the dark shape of the box, Marnie stood watching us. Just stood, alone, Beau already loaded behind her, her arms wrapped round herself in last year's good coat, watching the two of us in the wet dusk — the scared horse and the boy he'd gone quiet for — and she didn't come over, and she didn't say anything, and I lifted my hand off his neck to wave her in out of the cold, and she turned and climbed up into the cab and pulled the door to.


---

# Chapter Eight — Quillan

The rosette from Hartpury was still on Saracen's bridle three days later because nobody had the heart to take it off, a cheap red-and-blue thing with a centre that said FIRST in gold letters going to flake already, and it hung in the tack room over his bridle hook gathering the chaff dust, and every time I went in for a girth it caught the corner of my eye and did something warm to me I'm not going to apologise for. I was nineteen. I'd won a thing. The thing had my name on it and a horse no one could ride underneath the name, and I'd carried both home up the M5 in the dark with the radio low and Curtis asleep against the window, and you don't get to feel that twice the same way, so I let it hang there flaking and I looked at it on purpose.

That was the Tuesday. Hartpury was the Saturday gone, Yorkshire was a fortnight out, and in between there was the yard, which is where the season actually lives — not the shows, the shows are the punctuation. The yard is the sentence.

And the yard, that week, was about Quillan.

---

I want to do her properly, because of what came after, because I owe her that much and Pip more.

Quillan was the chestnut mare, five, the one I'd clocked my first day screwing herself into the back of a box, all eye and elbow. Bright bright chestnut, a real copper, with a thin crooked blaze and four white socks that the old men say means you should sell her and the young women say means she's special, and she was both, sellable and special, which is the worst thing a horse can be in a yard like ours. She was the next one. Everybody knew it. You only had to watch her loose-school once. She came at a fence the opposite way to Saracen — where he met it careful, half-asking permission, she met it like she'd invented it, ears pricked sharp as scissors, too much power coming up too fast through a five-year-old's frame that didn't quite know yet what to do with it, and she'd fling herself at the thing with a great green extravagant leap that left a foot to spare on the wrong stride and land bucking out of sheer joy. Electric. That's the only word. You stood at the rail and the hair came up on your arms.

She was Pip's.

Not on paper — nothing was on paper, I'd learned that much by then, it was the one fact of the place I'd properly absorbed — but Pip's the way things are yours when you've made them. Pip had backed her. Pip had sat on her first, that long slow autumn before I came, in the indoor with the tarp going, when the mare was a rising four-year-old who didn't trust a leg near her, and Pip had done it the right way, the slow correct pony-club way, the way you do it when you've paid for the knowledge — lunged her, long-reined her, leaned over her, sat up, walked off, all of it by the book because Pip was the only one of us who'd ever owned the book. And the mare had come good for her. Sharp as she was, electric as she was, Quillan would do anything for Pip and not much for anyone else, would put her copper head down into Pip's chest in the box and stand there blowing while Pip talked rubbish to her, and that's a thing money genuinely can't buy and Pip had it, had the one thing, and I'd watched it across the yard with something I didn't examine too closely because if I'd examined it I'd have had to call it what it was, which was a little bit of wanting it for myself.

"She's going to be the one, Dev," Pip said. We were doing haynets, Tuesday night, after, the two of us in the cold barn with the bale-string biting our fingers. "I know everyone says it, but. She's going to be the one I produce. Properly. Up the grades. And then —" She stopped herself. Pip stopped herself a lot. She'd start a sentence about her future and get embarrassed halfway, like wanting things was a thing you apologised for, which in her family it probably was. "I dunno. Then I'm a producer, aren't I. Then I've done one. You do one and people know your name and they send you the next one and that's. That's the whole game."

"That's the whole game," I said.

"You did one." She grinned at me, broad, freckled, no edge in it at all, which was the thing about Pip, there was never any edge. "You did the impossible one first off, you flash git. I'll do mine the slow way and we'll both be at HOYS one day, dead old, going *do you remember the caravan.*"

"Do you remember the kettle that never worked."

"Do you remember Curtis counting his biscuits." She laughed and the haynet swung and Quillan, three boxes down, heard her laugh and called, a sharp impatient mare-call, *where are you,* and Pip's whole face changed when the mare called, went soft and certain, and she said "Coming, madam," to the dark, and I have thought about that a lot since. The certainty in it. She had no paper either and she was that certain.

---

It happened Thursday.

It happened the way they all happen, the real ones — not with a row, not with anyone raising their voice, this was Tatefield, nobody raised their voice — but charming, and over tea, and so kind in the saying of it that you'd have had to be a hard sort of person to feel the knife go in.

I was there because I was always there by then. I'd become the thing in the kitchen, the extra at the table, fed and useful, and Caro had got into the habit of working me into the day's plans out loud while I ate, which I took, God help me, as inclusion. Pip was there because she'd been called in, and being called in to the Hall kitchen was rare enough for the working pupils that Pip had washed her hands twice and you could see she was nervous and hopeful both, the way you are when you've been summoned and you've decided, on the walk down, that it's going to be good news.

Caro made it good news. That was the artistry of it. She poured the tea herself, into the everyday mugs not the china, which was its own message — *we're all family here* — and she sat Pip down and put her ruined beautiful hand flat on the table near Pip's and she said:

"Quillan's ready for her next bit, I think."

"She is," Pip said, lit up. "She really is, Caro, the way she jumped on Monday—"

"She is. You've done a lovely job with her. *Lovely.*" The compliment first. Always the compliment first; I'd seen her do it to me, I'd see her do it to a horse and a man with a chequebook before the year was out, and it was always the same — she found the soft hungry place and she laid the praise on it like a poultice, and while you were warm from it she did the other thing. "Which is exactly why she's ready for a more experienced jock to take her on now. To make the most of what you've built. You understand."

I watched it land. I'm good at watching things land; it's half of what I am. I watched it go into Pip the way you watch a horse take a needle — the flinch that the body does before the mind's caught up, a small thing, a tightening round the eyes, a hand that came off the warm mug and went into her lap. And then the mind caught up and did the worse thing, which was to *agree.*

"Yeah," Pip said. "No, course. Course. She needs — yeah."

"You've too much else on, darling. Six horses to ride. We can't have you stuck on the one project when you're stretched so thin, it isn't fair on *you.*" That was the masterstroke, I think now. She made it a kindness *to Pip.* She made taking the mare off her a gift, a lightening, a thing done for Pip's own good, and Pip — who'd been raised to be grateful, who'd been raised exactly the way I'd been raised only with better cutlery — Pip said thank you. I heard her. I heard Pip Sallow say *thank you* across that table for the thing being taken off her, and I didn't understand yet that I was hearing my own future in a girl's voice, in a borrowed kitchen, over everyday tea.

"Who's taking her on?" Pip said. Bright. Still bright. The brightness was the part that finished me.

Caro looked at me.

She didn't even say it. She just looked at me, across the table, kind as anything, proud of me, and Pip's eyes followed Caro's and landed on me, and I sat there with a piece of someone else's bread in my hand and I was the answer to the question. I was who was taking her on.

"Oh," Pip said. "Dev. Course. That's — that's brilliant, that makes total sense, Dev's the — yeah."

And she smiled at me. That's the bit. She smiled at me, with her whole broad decent freckled face, because she couldn't not, because she was Pip and there was no edge in her, and the smile had nothing in it but a kind of drowning gladness that at least it was me, at least it was a friend, and I have never in my life wanted less to be smiled at.

---

I rode Quillan the next morning.

I'd love to tell you I refused. I've told it to myself that way some nights — a version where I stand up at the table and say *no, she's Pip's, I won't,* a version where I've got a spine instead of an appetite. But I didn't have a spine yet. I had a gift and an appetite and a place at the table I was terrified of losing, and when Caro said you'll take Quillan on this morning, I think, that wasn't a thought and wasn't a question, I tacked her up.

And she was extraordinary.

I'll be honest about that too, because the dishonesty would be to pretend she wasn't, to pretend it cost me, on the horse, in the moment. It didn't. On the horse it was the opposite of cost. She was the best young thing I'd ever sat on, all that copper power coming up willing through my legs, and she figured me out in about ten minutes — figured out that I'd give her a longer rein and a quieter leg than Pip dared, that I'd let her be as big and brave as she wanted and only catch her on the back side of it, and she went for me like she'd been waiting her whole short life for someone to stop being careful with her. We jumped a course she had no business jumping at five and she ate it. Pinged round it pricked-eared and laughing. I came out of the school grinning, I won't pretend I didn't, I came out of it on the highest sort of high, the *I'm-good-at-this* high, the purest drug in the world and the cheapest, and I dropped the rein and patted her copper neck and looked up—

and Pip was at the rail.

She'd come to watch. Of course she'd come to watch. You'd come to watch the horse you made, even when they've taken her off you, *especially* then, because watching her is the last of having her. She was up on the gate where she'd been to watch Saracen, chin on the top bar, the same spot, and her face wasn't doing the glad thing now because she didn't know I was looking. Her face was just watching her mare go brilliantly for somebody else. And it didn't have an edge in it even then, that's the thing I can't get past, it had no blame in it, no *you bastard,* nothing pointed at me at all — it was just a face watching a door close that it had thought, three days ago, was a door opening.

She saw me see her. And she did the worst possible thing, which was to fix the glad smile back on, fast, and call out "She's flying, Dev! Doesn't she *fly!*" — being generous about it, being good about it, making it easy for me, the way she made everything easy for everyone, the way she'd no doubt make it easy for whoever broke her heart next — and I called something back, "She's a machine, you made her a machine," giving her the credit because the credit was true and because I couldn't think what else to give her, and Pip glowed at that, glowed at the scrap, and I rode the mare she'd lost out of the school past the girl who'd made her and something happened in my chest that I didn't have a name for.

I have a name for it now. But I didn't then, and the not-having-it is the only thing that lets me forgive nineteen at all.

It wasn't guilt. It was colder than guilt and quieter. It was the first time my hands had been the ones that *took* a thing. Not held a frightened horse together — taken. I'd ridden Quillan beautifully and the beauty was the instrument; the better I rode her the more right it looked that she'd been moved up to me, the more sense Caro's kindness made, the more thoroughly Pip got disappeared. My gift had a use I hadn't seen before. It didn't only gentle. It could also be the reason a thing got taken from someone, and the proof that the taking was justified, in the same motion, with the same hands, and I felt it go cold and silent at the bottom of me and I did not look at it.

I went and patted the mare and gave her a Polo. I'm good at not looking at things. I learned it before I learned to ride.

---

I went to Saracen after. I always went to Saracen after, that summer, the way you go and put your hand in cold water.

He was in his box with the door open — open, always, now; I'd won that much, the open door — pulling at his haynet with the rosette-coloured evening coming in through the bars, and when I came in he stopped pulling and turned his head and looked at me with the whole near side of his face, the good side, and the near ear came round and tipped back. Just like that. No asking. He'd got so he did it the second I came through the door, tipped the ear back to me before he'd even decided to, and three months before he'd have had me through the wall for coming in unannounced and now he gave me the ear for free, and I stood at his near shoulder and put my thumb on the scar without deciding to, on the hand's-width of bald pale skin nobody could explain, and I felt the big body let its breath down one notch the way it only did for me.

He trusted me completely. That's the plain fact of it. By that evening in early May he'd handed me the whole of himself, the poll, the right rein, the cornered terror, all of it, *here, you hold it,* and he stood in the open box leaning the weight of his head a degree toward me, the off ear flicking lazy at the swallows in the eaves, the near ear back, listening, and I stood there with my thumb on the scar and for the first time since I'd known him the trust frightened me.

Not for him. I want to be exact, because exactness is the only honesty I've got. It didn't frighten me *for* him, that came later, that came much too late. It frightened me about *myself.* Because I'd just spent the morning finding out what my hands could do besides gentle, and here was the most frightened animal I'd ever met handing me the right to do it to him, tipping the ear back, blowing the long low blow, *do I have to do this alone or are you here,* and the truthful answer, the answer I didn't say out loud because you don't say things out loud to a horse that you can't yet say to yourself, was that I didn't entirely know any more what I'd do with the yes when he gave it.

He gave it anyway. He didn't have my doubts. He just stood there and trusted me, and the swallows went over, and I took my thumb off the scar and did up the haynet I didn't need to do up, the way you do something with your hands when you can't do the thing with your chest.

---

Glenys came past the box on her way down to lock the feed room, her torch not lit yet, the roll-up behind her ear, and she stopped in the doorway and looked at the two of us a moment, me and the horse in the failing light, and she said, not unkindly:

"You rode the mare grand this morning."

"She's a machine."

"She's a sold horse," Glenys said. "That's what she is. They've moved her up to you so she shows better. A youngster going round like that off a jock with a name — they'll have an advert filmed and a price on her by Whit." She wasn't telling me off. That was the worst of it, she never told you off, she just laid the true thing down flat in front of you like a stone on a path and stepped over it and went on. "Pip's a good girl and she did everything right and it counts for nowt, because she'd nobody behind her and no paper in front of her, and that's the whole of how this place works, love, and you've just had your hands in it." She shifted the torch to her other hand. "They'll take what's worth taking. Always have." And then, lower, the worn old refrain she gave me about twice a week and meant every time and which I'd stopped properly hearing: "Mind yourself."

She didn't say anything else. She didn't say the big thing, the thing she had in her she hadn't given me yet, the line that was coming in June that I'd carry the rest of my life. This wasn't that night. This was just the near version, *they'll take what's worth taking,* laid down on the path and stepped over, and she clicked the torch on and went off down the dark yard to lock up the feed where the names of three gone horses were still half-rubbed on the blackboard, and I stood in the box with Saracen's near ear tipped back to me and Pip's *doesn't she fly* in my head and Quillan, three boxes down, calling once into the dusk — *where are you, where are you* — for the only person who'd never once come when she called her again.

---

I found Pip at Quillan's box that night.

I hadn't gone looking. I'd gone out for the cold-water-on-the-face walk you take round a yard before you face the caravan, and I came round by the empty boxes — Quillan had been moved that afternoon, down the row to the smart end, the sale end, the boxes with the working strip lights, where the mare would be plaited and filmed and made to look like money — and there was Pip, standing at the door of the box that wasn't Quillan's any more.

She wasn't crying. I want that on the record because the crying would have been easier, I'd have known what to do with crying, I'd have put my arm round her and said something and felt like a friend. She was just standing at the empty door the way you stand at a door — not leaning on it, not holding it, just standing square in front of it with her hands down at her sides, looking into a box with no horse in it, with the new straw she'd put down that morning not knowing, the haynet she'd filled still hanging full, the headcollar still on its hook with QUILLAN written on the strap in Pip's careful biro because Pip wrote everyone's name on everything, that was Pip, and the mare three boxes away down at the smart end calling and calling and Pip not going.

She heard me come. She didn't turn round. She said, to the empty box, in a voice she'd got completely level:

"It's the right thing for her. She'll go further with you. It's the right thing."

And I stood ten feet off in the dark and I didn't go to her.

I've told myself a dozen reasons over the years. That she'd want to be left. That there was nothing to say. That a hand on her shoulder would've been a lie coming off the hand that rode the mare away. They're all true and none of them is the reason. The reason is that I couldn't go to her, because to go to her I'd have had to stand next to the empty box and be the one who'd emptied it, and I wasn't able, at nineteen, to stand still in front of a thing my own hands had done. So I did the thing I was always going to do, the thing I'd done at the table and on the horse and would go on doing, bigger and bigger, all the way to October — I went quiet, and I let it happen, and I watched.

I watched Pip Sallow stand at the door of a box that didn't have her horse in it, level-voiced, telling the dark it was the right thing, while down the yard the mare she'd made called for her into the cold and got no answer, and I knew, the way you know a thing in the body before the mind will sign for it, that I was looking at something. That this was a small one. That somewhere up ahead there was a big one with my own name on the empty door.

I went up to the caravan and I didn't tell Curtis any of it, and I lay in my clothes in the cold and listened to the country be loud about being quiet, and I thought about the rosette flaking gold in the tack room with my name under the name of a horse I'd been promised and didn't own, on a strap, in someone's careful biro.


---

# Chapter Nine — North

Pip stood at the empty box the whole time we loaded the lorry for Yorkshire, and I let her, because there was nothing in the world I could've said that she wouldn't have heard in my own voice the sound of the one who'd ridden the mare away. So I loaded ramps and shut partitions and tied haynets and pretended I couldn't see her down the line of doors, a girl with her hand flat on a slate where Quillan's name had been chalked and rubbed and would be chalked again with somebody else's horse, and I told myself I had to think about the show. We were three days out. The clock was already on.

Then it was four in the morning and the headlights were swinging the gate posts up out of the dark, and Curtis was driving because Glenys's eyes weren't what they were on the motorway at night, and I was up front with a flask of stewed tea and Saracen behind the bulkhead with his ears, I knew, going at every gear-change, every bump, every blind decision the road made for him. You can feel a frightened horse through a lorry the way you can feel weather coming. He wasn't kicking. He'd given up kicking the first hour. He'd gone to the other thing, the still thing, the thing everyone but me reads as settled.

"He all right back there," Curtis said, not really a question, his face green off the dash.

"He's all right." He wasn't. He was holding it. There's a difference and only one of us in the cab knew it and it wasn't the one driving.

I'd never been north on a motorway in my life. I'd come *down* one, once, on a bus, with a holdall and a betting slip. Now we were going up the spine of the country in the wrong direction for me — the M5 to the M6 to the M1, the names of it like a list of places that had never wanted me — and the diesel gauge dropped the whole way like a thing with a slow leak, and Curtis worked it out loud, the price of it, fifty quid here, sixty there, *and we'll not place high enough to cover the entry, mark me,* and he was right, and it didn't matter, because the yard didn't run on placing high enough. It ran on what placing high enough did to the price of the horse, and none of us said that part. We watched the gauge and the dark and let the country come up at us, and somewhere past Birmingham the sky started going the grey it goes before light, and the hills changed shape, and got harder, and got mine.

---

I knew where we were before the sign told me. You always know your own weather.

The showground sat in a fold of Yorkshire that had been a great house's park once and was sheep and rings and lorry-lines now, and an hour from it, less in a fast car, was a tower block I could've drawn you blindfold, and a riding school on the edge of an estate where a woman called Bev let kids muck out for a leg-up, and a skewbald cob too sharp to be safe and too free to be sold who taught a nine-year-old the only thing he was ever going to be good at. I didn't say any of that to Curtis. I sat in the lorry park while he found us a slot, and I looked at the hills I'd grown up under from the wrong side of them, the show side, the side with money in the lorry park, and I felt — I want to be careful here, because the easy thing to feel would've been shame, and it wasn't shame.

It was the other thing. It was the thing nobody puts on a rosette.

I'd come back. Not crawling, not visiting, not for a funeral. I'd come back *into the ring,* on a horse the size of a barn, in a lorry that wasn't mine with a name that wasn't mine going up a board, and the estate was an hour away and might as well have been on the moon and I could see it from here and it could not see me, and that — God forgive me, because there were better men than me sleeping in that tower block — that was the sweetest geography I have ever stood in. I was a mile up the side of the wound and climbing and the view was lovely.

Then I went and got the horse off the box, because the horse didn't care about any of it, and the horse was the only true thing in the lorry park, and the horse needed me.

He came down the ramp the way he came down every ramp now — thinking about sideways and choosing forward, a half-second of decision you could watch happen in him, the great dark head up, the eye showing white at the corner, and then the drop of the poll, the long low blow out through the nose, *all right then,* and his big careful feet on the metal one at a time. Glenys had her hand on his off cheek, the frightened side, the side he let her have. I had the rope and the half-stride of him that was mine.
