He stood in the strange park with strange horses screaming on every side and a tannoy crackling and a generator going and he shook once, all over, a shudder that ran from his poll to the white coronet on the off-hind, and got rid of the lorry, and looked at me.

I'm not going to be clever about the way he looked at me. He looked at me to find out if it was safe. That's all it ever was. He looked at me the way you look at the one person in a room who knows where the door is.

"We're a long way from home," I told him, which was a lie for me and the truth for him, and I have never been sure which of us I was telling.

---

The course walk at a 1.30 is a different animal to a 1.20, and not because of twenty centimetres.

I walked it in my grey half-chaps among men in boots that cost more than the lorry, jackets off, pacing the lines with the big deliberate steps you learn to make your own — four strides of mine to one of his, that arithmetic, the hidden twelve-foot stride you're really counting — and I read the going first because the going was soft, north-soft, a week of rain in it, and I'd studded him deep that morning, big squares behind, pointed in front, the stud-tap and the spanner cold in my fingers in the dark, screwing grip into a body so it could hold the ground I was about to ask it to launch off. Then I went and walked my lines.

There was a related distance off the top of the ring, fence five to fence six, a vertical to an oxer, and the course builder had set it a half-stride wrong on purpose — it walked a long five or a tight, ugly six, and the men in the good boots stood at it arguing the way they argue, *it's a forward five if you ride it, it's a six if you're a coward,* and I stood at it and didn't argue and didn't count, or counted and then threw the counting away, because the thing I had that they didn't was I wasn't going to decide it on the ground. I was going to land off fence five and ask the horse what he'd brought, and if he'd brought a big stride I'd let it down in five and if he'd come up careful and short I'd sit and fit the six, and I'd know which inside the first stride after the fence because he'd tell me, because he always told me, because he was the only one in this whole expensive field that couldn't lie.

The fences were big. I'll say that plainly, the way I go plain about him. Fence nine was a wall, stacked blocks that looked like the side of a house, and a wall is the worst thing you can build for him, because a wall is a thing he can't see past and his whole fear is being shut in by a thing he can't see past. The builder hadn't built it for Saracen. It landed on him like it was personal. I stood at it a long time. I put my hand on the top block. I thought, *I'll have to be there for that one. I'll have to be more there than I've ever been.*

That's the job. That's the whole job, said small. Be there for the wall.

---

The warm-up nearly finished us before the bell.

It's a chute, the collecting ring, a pen of churned ground with a practice fence in it and twenty horses circling and big names cutting across your line and grooms running poles up and trainers calling *heads up* and *left to left* and a hierarchy you learn fast — the made horses get the fence when they want it, the working pupil waits, fetches the pole he knocked, watches. And in the middle of it Saracen found a wing he couldn't see past, a standing crowd along the rope, a wall of bodies, and he went.

Not loud. He never goes loud first. He went still under me. He stopped blinking. And the muscle came up under his right eye in that small fast flicker that everyone in this ring would've called *he's about to be a sod,* and that I have spent my life calling the last second a horse is still asking you for help — *do I have to do this on my own, or are you here* — and the answer to that question is the only thing I have ever been good at, and I gave it. I didn't haul. You never haul, and on him you never, ever touch the right rein hard, because the right rein is where a man I'll never meet taught him that pressure on that side is pain you can't get away from. I gave the right rein away. I gave it loose, I put my hand forward up his neck, I took the trap off him, and I put my leg on, quiet, *we're going forward, not into anything, just forward, with me,* and I turned him off the crowd to where he could see out, and I waited.

He waited too. Half a stride longer than the fear wanted. For me. He blew out, long and low, and the near ear, the left one, the good-side one, came back to listen for me while the off ear stayed up and out on the world, and that split — one ear for me, one for the job — is the thing I'd ride into a fire for, and I have, more or less, and I'd do it again. He came back to me in a churned pen full of people who'd have sold him to the meat man a month ago, and he popped the practice oxer like he was apologising for the fuss, knees snapped up to his chin, neat, careful, a horse who has decided never again to touch a pole, and we went to the gate.

Glenys was at the gate. She put the rosette-less bridle right, fussed a strap that didn't need it, the way she does when she's frightened for you and won't say it. "Mind the wall," she said.

"I know the wall."

"You don't know nothing," she said, which from Glenys is *I love you,* and she stepped back and the gate opened.

---

I won't give you the whole round, fence by fence. I'll give you the wall and the bell, because those two are the truth of it.

The bell went and the clock came on and I had forty-five seconds to cross the start and then it was just the horse and the going and the track, and we ate the first line easy, three, the oxer over the Liverpool where he spooked down at the water and I had my leg there before he found it, and four, and five — and off five he told me. He landed and he had the big stride in him, the brave one, the one that means he's *up,* he's enjoying it, the fear's parked, and I let it down and we did the long five, the one the men in the good boots called for cowards, and it rode like silk, and I heard somebody at the rail go *oh* the way a crowd does when it sees a thing done right.

Then the wall.

He saw it from three out and the question came up through the reins, and I was there, and he believed me, and he jumped it. I'll not dress that one up. He met it honest and cleared it and the crowd made a sound I didn't hear, because I was already riding for the next, and we came through the timers clear, no faults, inside the time, a clear round in Yorkshire on a condemned horse, and I have never in my life been so simply, stupidly, bottomlessly happy, and I'm not ashamed of it, that one I keep.

The wall in the first round was the easy one. I just didn't know it yet.

---

There was a jump-off and I didn't expect to be in it and we were, six of us back, the course shortened and raised, and now it was against the clock, fewest faults then fastest, and the made horses went and the money went and Tomas Berg's groom — Berg himself wasn't north for a 1.30, he had bigger fish, but his name was already a thing people said at gates — somebody's flash bay went double clear and fast and put up a time that the good boots nodded at.

I had no business beating it. I had a seven-year-old who'd been on a sale lorry six weeks ago and a stride that wins on the inside turn, and I had the one thing the inside turn needs, which is a horse that'll come back to your hand in the air and turn before he's landed because he trusts you that much. I shouldn't have asked him for it. I want to be honest about the shape of the day. To win the jump-off I had to take the inside line to the wall, cut the corner tight, ask him to come off the floor already turning to a thing he couldn't see past, ask him to spend more of the borrowed courage than the first round had spent, ask for the brave thing twice. And he gave it. I turned him inside, tight, and the wall came up off a stride he had to be talked onto, and I talked him onto it, leg and seat and the loose right rein and everything I had, and he flew it, and we took the last on an angle and crossed the timers and the clock stopped on a number, and there was a silence, and then a board changed, and the silence broke, and the number was the fastest of the day.

We'd won.

I'd never won anything. Not a thing. Not a race, not a raffle, not a fight I started. I'd been useful, all my life I'd been useful, I'd held things together so the social worker wouldn't come and ridden the rogues so I'd be fed, but I had never once stood in a place where a thing had been a contest and I'd come out on top of it with people watching and my name — my name, *D. Okafor-Lyle,* the Okafor for a father who'd never sent so much as a card and the Lyle for a mother who I'll get to — my name going up on a board in white plastic letters at the top.

Caro had come north for Beau and Marnie's class and she stood at the rail and I rode out past her with the horse blowing and my face doing something I couldn't stop it doing, and a man I didn't know clapped her on the shoulder and said, *well, Caro, your boy's flying, where'd you find him,* and Caro looked at me, and at the horse, and said, "Ours," and let it sit, warm, in the air. And the man said, "Whose is the horse, though, who owns it," because that's the only question that's ever really asked in that field, and Caro said, "Ours," again, the exact same word, the exact same warmth, and I heard *family,* I heard *us, mine, kept,* I heard the warmest thing anyone had said about me all day, and I have spent ten years learning to hear the other thing that was in it, which was the thing a man says about a horse.

---

The phone went in the lorry park.

I'd got him untacked and washed off and walking, sweat-dark on the near-black neck going darker, the rosette already pushed onto the browband of his bridle hanging on the lorry, and I had the rope in one hand and my mum's name on the screen in the other, and I felt the day tip an inch, the way it always tips when that name comes up, because you never know, with her, which call it is. There were two kinds. There was the bright kind and there was the other kind, and the bright kind was the one that frightened me more, because the bright kind was the one that wanted something and was dressing for it.

"Devlin," she said, and it was the bright one. "Devlin, love, I saw — somebody put it on the Facebook, the horsey lass from Bev's, she put a bit of video, I saw you, I saw my lad win in Yorkshire, oh I'm grand, love, never better, I'm having a lovely week as it goes —"

She called me Devlin and I was nine years old. That's all it takes. One word out of one mouth and the floor of the years drops away and I'm standing in a kitchen that isn't this kitchen making sure the bills are in the right pile so she doesn't have to look at them, being the little adult, being useful, being the reason she's all right, because if I'm the reason she's all right then she'll stay all right, then she'll keep me. I learned to read a person off their breath before I ever sat on a horse. I learned it on her. I learned the whole gift — the listening, the reading the body, the giving away the right rein before the fear bolts — I learned every bit of it as a small boy in Parson Cross reading my own mother to find out which mother had come home today, and now I make my living doing it to half-ton animals and the world calls it a feel and Caro calls it worth money and nobody, not one person in that lorry park, knew they were watching a thing a frightened child had taught himself to survive a woman he loved.

"I won, Mum," I said. "First proper win. He went lovely."

"Course you won. Course you did. You were always — " and her voice did the thing, the breath caught, the bright cracked, and for one second the other mother was on the line, the real one, the one who loved me so hard it shook her hands, and she said, low, no brightness left in it at all, "I'm that proud of you, Devlin. I am. I don't say it. I'm that proud." And then, before I could hold it, before I could keep her in that one true second, the bright came back down over it like a tarp over a roof, *anyway love, I won't keep you, only — only it's been a hard month, the money's been that tight, I wouldn't ask, you know I wouldn't ask, only —*

And there it was. The both-at-once of her. The pride was real. The ask was real. I could not, standing in that lorry park with the best day of my life still wet on the horse's neck, tell you which one she'd rung for, and the not being able to tell is the whole of it, is the thing I came out of that childhood holding, is the eyes I read every person with for the rest of my life. I read horses true and people I read in the dark, by feel, half-wrong, because the first body I ever learned to read taught me that love and the ask come down the same line in the same voice and you will never, ever pull them apart.

"I'll send you something Monday," I said. "When I'm paid." I wasn't paid. There was no paid. I'd send her the only thing I had, which was the bit Glenys slipped me for chips and diesel out of her own tin, and I'd go without, and I'd do it gladly, the way I did everything gladly for the people who needed me, because being needed was the only love I'd ever found that stayed.

"You're a good boy, Devlin," she said, bright again, whole again, gone again. "You always were. Good lad. Go on, then. Go and be famous." And the line went, and I stood with the dead phone and the live horse, and the horse turned his head over the partition and put his great dark face against my chest, and his near ear, the left one, the good-side one, tipped back to me, listening, wanting nothing, asking nothing, the only thing in my whole life that ever came near me and wanted nothing back.

---

I told him what I didn't tell her.

I stood in the dark of the lorry park with the generators going and the other lorries lit up and laughing, the made-horse people drinking the day, and I put my forehead against his and I told the horse, quiet, just the two of us, *I won, Saracen. We won. You did it. You were so brave at the wall, you don't even know.* I told him I was proud of him the way I couldn't get the words for with her on the line. I told him he'd carried me back up the side of my whole life, an hour from the tower block, and put my name on a board, and that nobody could take this one off me. I told him a lot of things you'd laugh at if you saw them written, except you wouldn't, you'd have stood in the dark with me and felt your own throat go, because there is nothing funny about the one thing you love, and he was, I knew it by then and I'd stopped fighting it, he was the one thing I loved.

My thumb found the scar on his near shoulder without my deciding it. The hand's-width of pale, hairless, slick-healed skin that nobody alive could tell me the story of — a kick, a gate, a fall, a man, the one page torn out of him, the one part of him I could never read. I held my thumb on the thing that had hurt him before I ever knew him and I told him nobody would ever do that to him again, not while I had the rope, and I believed it, standing there, with everything in me I believed it.

It was the best day. I've checked. I keep a list, and it's still top.

The near ear stayed tipped back to me, all the way up, while the lorries laughed and the country I came from sat dark on the skyline an hour off, not knowing where I'd got to. He listened to me breathe. He didn't know what a board was, or a price, or a buyer, or a word like *ours* said warm by a woman doing sums. He only knew I was there. He thought that was the whole of it. So did I. We were both wrong, and we had the spring left before we'd find out, and I would not, even now, even knowing, give the spring back.


---

# Chapter Ten — The Family Table

The rosette from Yorkshire was still pinned to the caravan curtain four days later, gone slightly limp at the edges the way they do in damp, and I'd caught myself looking at it the way you'd look at a thing you weren't sure had happened. Red. First. My name on a board an hour from the estate. I'd told the horse about it in the lorry park with my mother's voice still warm and lying in my ear, and the horse had tipped the near ear back and taken it, the way he took everything off me now, and I'd thought *that's the top of the list, that one, I've checked* — and then we'd driven six hours south through the dark and come home to Tatefield, and the rain, and the muck heap, and a note on the kitchen door in Caro's hard upright hand.

*Saturday. Dinner. The Hall — the proper dining room. 7.30. There's a jacket of Hugo's in the boot room if you've nothing. C.*

"Proper dining room," Curtis read over my shoulder, breathing chip-fat. "Bloody hell. She's bringing out the big house."

"Means Julian's coming," said Pip, from her bunk, and didn't say anything else, and I clocked the not-saying.

*

I'd not been in the dining room. Three months at Tatefield and I'd been fed at the scrubbed kitchen table every night I wasn't too tired to climb the hill, but the dining room was one of the dust-sheet rooms, one of the white-shape rooms, and on Saturday at half six Glenys had the sheets off and a fire going in a grate the size of a fireplace in a film, and the cold of two unheated decades coming off the walls faster than one evening's fire could push it back.

The table would've sat eighteen. It was laid for six. There was silver on it I'd only seen behind glass — the cabinet I'd clocked from the lane my first day, a hundred yards off, emptied of its best for one night, knives and forks and a thing I later learned was for fish, all of it polished to a shine that I understood, watching the firelight move in it, was Glenys's afternoon. The bones of money, brought out and laid down and lit, so that a man up from London could see them and write a cheque and not have to look at the tarp.

The jacket of Hugo's fit across the shoulders and hung off everywhere else, and smelled of cedar and a dead man, and I wore it because the alternative was the fleece, and I'd learned that much: there are rooms where the fleece is a sentence you serve all evening.

Julian Wexley-Tate was already by the fire with a glass when I came in, and he turned, and I read him before he said a word, the way I read everything — soft hands, a good coat worn careless because there'd always be another, the weight all back on his heels like a man who's never once had to be ready to move. He looked like Caro with the spine taken out. He looked like Marnie's father, which is to say he looked, when his daughter came in behind me, straight past her at the fire.

"This is the famous Dev," he said. Not to me. To the room. "The Sheffield wonder. Mummy's not stopped writing about you." He put out a hand and the shake was loose and brief, a thing got through. "You've turned our old rogue into a winner, I hear. Clever boy."

"He turned himself, mostly," I said. "I just sat there."

Julian laughed too quickly, the laugh of a man who finds the help charming. "Modest. God, she'll love that." *She* was Caro, who came in then in a dress that had been astonishing once and a brooch that still was, and the room rearranged itself around her without her doing anything, and we sat.

*

I want to be careful here, because it would be easy to make Julian a villain and he wasn't one, quite. He was worse than that. He was bored.

He asked me things all through the soup. Where I'd learned, who'd taught me, what it felt like — *what does it feel like, when they go quiet for you, is it a sort of, what, a sort of telepathy?* — and I gave him the answers I'd learned to give, dry, short, the ones that kept the laugh on my side of the table, and the whole time he asked me he did not ask his daughter one single thing.

Marnie sat across from me in last year's good dress and ate almost nothing and bit the skin at the side of her thumbnail between courses and watched her father be fascinated by the working pupil. I'd seen her white around the mouth on a horse, at Hartpury, the hands going loud. She had the same white around the mouth now and nothing in her hands to make loud, so she made her thumbnail bleed instead, small and neat, under the edge of the table where she thought no one read hands.

I read hands.

"And it's instinct, is it," Julian was saying, leaning in, the wine doing his warmth for him. "It's not something you can be taught. Mummy says you can't teach it."

"You can't teach the front of it," I said. "The feel. You can teach all the rest."

"Extraordinary." He sat back, satisfied, and delivered it like a compliment, like the nicest thing he could think to say, which I think it honestly was: "Well. You've got a feel for them, haven't you. Your sort always have."

The fire ticked. Glenys, going round with the dish, didn't break stride; her hand came in over my shoulder and laid another spoonful on my plate I hadn't asked for, and went on. Caro, at the head of the table, was looking at her son with an expression I couldn't read and didn't try to, because I was busy filing the thing he'd said the way you file a stone you've found in a feed bucket — quietly, to the side, to look at later, because you don't stop the feed to hold it up.

*Your sort.* I'd had it before, of course. You don't grow up the only brown kid on Parson Cross and not have it, in worse words, from worse mouths. But it was new dressed like this, at a table with fish forks, said kindly, said as praise, *your sort always have a feel for them* — them being horses, them being animals, the feel being a thing in the blood and not the head, the same thing they said about Saracen, bred for it, an engine, a thing that did what it did because of what it was and not because anyone had taught it or it had chosen. I sat in a dead man's jacket and ate a spoonful I hadn't asked for and said, "Aye, well. Beats working for a living," and Julian roared, and the danger passed, and that was the cleverage of it, that was the trick I'd already learned without learning it — you make the laugh, you keep the laugh, the laugh costs you a little piece you don't show anyone and it buys you the rest of the night.

Marnie wasn't laughing. Marnie was looking at me across the silver with something that wasn't fascination any more. I think it might've been the first time she liked me.

*

The talk turned to money the way the talk always turned to money in that house, sideways, dressed up, never said. Julian had brought a cheque — you could feel it in the room before it appeared, the way the conversation kept circling back to *the roof, Mummy, you really must do something about the roof,* and Caro saying *yes, darling,* in a voice that meant the cheque would not touch the roof and they both knew it and neither said so. He talked about a horse Caro should sell. He talked about a horse Caro should buy, with money she didn't have, to sell on, as if production were a thing you did with a spare afternoon. He talked about Saracen.

"What's the rogue worth now, then. Now your boy's tamed him."

"It's early," said Caro.

"Mummy. Roughly."

"It's early, Julian." And she said it with a smile and the cold underneath, and he let it go, and I sat there and felt the warm of the win go thin in me, because they were talking about him, they were doing sums about him over the fish forks, and the horse was up the hill in the dark in his bolted box doing the only thing he had to do against the wall, and I'd told him four days ago, *we're going to do something, you and me,* and they were costing it.

I didn't say *he's not for sale.* I'd said it once, to Caro, weeks back — *he's not for sale* — and she'd told me everything was for sale and the art was in when, and I'd been a child to say it. So I learned, sitting there. I learned to eat through it. I ate the fish I'd been given and I made the laugh when it was wanted and I did not say the horse's name, because saying it felt like handing them another thing to weigh.

That's a thing the table taught me, that night, that I didn't have words for till years on. You can be fed at a table and still be one of the things on it.

*

After, Caro and Julian went through to the kitchen with the brandy and the brown envelopes, and I was left in the firelight with the silver and Marnie, and the fire dying, and the cold coming back into the room like a tide that had only ever paused.

She didn't go. I thought she'd go. She got up and went to the window instead, to the long black glass with the yard somewhere out beyond it in the dark, and stood with her back to me and her arms crossed tight, and said, to the glass, not to me:

"He's never asked me what it feels like."

I didn't say anything.

"Twenty-six minutes, you've been in this house, and my father's asked you what it feels like four times." She wasn't crying. Her voice had gone very even, the way a voice goes when you've taken all the up and down out of it on purpose. "I've ridden since I was three. I've got the name. I've got the — I've got everything you'd need, on paper. And no one's ever asked me what it feels like, because everyone already knows. It feels like nothing. There's nothing there. They watch me ride and they can see there's nothing there and they're too polite to say it, all of them, my whole life, and the not saying it is —"

She stopped. Her shoulders went up and held.

"I'm sorry," she said, in the voice that fishes. "God, listen to me. Poor little rich girl. You grew up on a — and I'm crying into the family silver about my *feelings.* You must think I'm —"

"I don't think anything," I said. "Sit down. Your hands are bleeding."

She turned round. She looked at me, and then down at her thumb, where the skin at the side of the nail was raw and she'd got a thread of blood going, and she looked surprised, the way you look when somebody tells you a thing about your own body you've stopped feeling.

"You watch hands," she said.

"I watch everything. It's the only thing I'm any good at."

She came back from the window and sat, not across from me now but along, two chairs down, near, and we sat in the failing firelight and she put her thumb in her mouth like a child and took it out again and folded her hands so I couldn't read them, which I noted, which I let her have.

"I'm frightened of them," she said. Quiet. No fishing in it now. "All of them. Beau. Beau's a saint and a schoolmaster and a — Beau would carry a sack of potatoes round Hickstead, and I'm frightened of *Beau.* I get on and I know he can feel it, I know the second I sit down he knows, and I think — I think they can smell that I don't believe it. That I'm waiting for it to go wrong. And so it goes wrong, because I made the room for it." She looked at me. "How do you not? Make the room for it?"

And here is the thing I have not been able to set down right in ten years, so I'll just set it down plain.

I knew the answer. I knew it exactly. I could have told her, sitting at that table, the truth of it — that the horse reads the gap between what your body says and what your hands do, and you close the gap, you *decide* before the fence and let your hands believe the decision even when the rest of you doesn't, you lie to them with the whole of you so smoothly that the lie becomes the truth and the horse goes on it. She'd asked me the one question that made me look straight at it — *how do you not make the room for it* — and the honest answer was *you don't leave the room for the truth to get in.*

I didn't tell her.

I told myself it was kindness. I told myself she was seventeen and bleeding into her own thumb and the last thing she needed was the working pupil explaining that the family gift was a kind of lie you tell a creature that can't argue. I told myself I didn't have the words for it yet, which was nearly true. But I think — I've had ten years and I think — that some part of me had already started to keep it. The way you keep a thing you've worked out is worth something. I'd watched Caro take Quillan off Pip with a smile, and I'd watched the whole table cost the horse over the fish, and somewhere under all the wine I'd learned the lesson of the house, which is that you don't give the valuable thing away for free at the table. Not even to a girl who's bleeding.

So I said, "You've not got nothing there. You've got too much there. You're all eyes. You ride watching for the disaster instead of riding the horse." Which was true, and useful, and cost me nothing, and was not the answer. "Beau doesn't need you brave. He needs you boring. You're boring at the breakfast table, aren't you. Be that on him."

She laughed — surprised, wet, a real one. "Be boring."

"Bore him to death. He'd love it."

"That's the worst coaching I've ever had and I've had Granny scream at me from a Land Rover."

"I'm not on the payroll. You get what you pay for."

And that landed wrong, in the firelight, the way a true thing dropped careless will, and the laugh went out of both of us. Because I wasn't on the payroll. She knew it. The whole house knew it. The Sheffield wonder who'd turned the rogue, fed at the family table in a dead man's jacket, no wage, no paper, eventually a horse, you have my word — and she was the granddaughter with the name and the fear and the empty hands, and we were, for about four seconds in the cold of that ruined beautiful room, exactly the same frightened animal looking at each other across the silver. Both kept for the wrong reasons. Both waiting to be found out. Neither one able to say so.

"Dev," she said, and stopped, and I thought for a second she was going to ask me the real thing, the thing she'd ask me in July with a horse between us and the season falling in, but she was seventeen and it was May and she didn't have it yet either. "Nothing. Thank you for the worst advice in the world."

*

I found Caro in the kitchen on my way out, alone now, Julian gone up to one of the cold bedrooms with the last of the brandy. The brown envelopes were on the table and the chequebook was open and her reading glasses were down her nose, and she looked, in the moment before she heard me, exactly as I'd seen her that night weeks ago through the gap in the caravan window — the pen not moving, the hand half-up toward her mouth, the frightened private arithmetic of a woman doing sums that don't come out. Then she heard me and it was gone, folded away, the posture back like a coat she'd shrugged on.

"He went well in Yorkshire," she said, not turning. "You did well in Yorkshire." A pause. "I'm proud of you. I don't say it. I'm saying it."

And it went into me, God help me, exactly where it always went, warm and low, before I could vote on it. *I'm proud of you.* Nobody said that to me. My mother said *I'm grand, love* and *you're a good lad* and once, drunk, *you saved my life, you,* which was the worst one. Nobody had ever sat me down sober and said *I'm proud of you* and meant the achievement and not the rescue. I stood in the cold kitchen and I was a little bit hers again, all over again, ninety seconds of it like the gravel on the first day.

I'd had the wine. So I asked.

"Caro." I made my voice flat, the Sheffield in it, my mum's voice when she wanted the rent. "Saracen. When Julian was asking what he's worth. I — when it gets to that, the selling and that. There's the thing you said. Eventually a horse. Is that — is he the —"

I couldn't get the question out clean. It came out sideways, half of it, because asking it straight felt like calling her a liar, and you don't call the woman who just said she's proud of you a liar.

She set the pen down. Click, on the wood, the same small sound as the gold-cracked cup three months back. She took her glasses off and looked at me with the full warmth, the real warmth, the kind you'd swear on a stack of Bibles was real, because it *was* real, that was never the part that was fake.

"Devlin," she said. My name. The one only my mother used. The road stud, going in for grip. "Listen to me. You've had one good win on a horse nobody else could sit on. *One.* You've a season in front of you. When you've a few more of those — when you've shown the world what I already know — we'll see you right. All of you. There'll be a horse, and it'll be a real one, and you'll have earned it, and no one will be able to say you were given anything. That's better than paper. Paper's for people who don't trust each other." She put her hand over mine on the table, the ruined beautiful hand, the loose ring riding on the thin finger. "You have my word. You've always had my word. Don't ask me to insult us both by writing it down."

And I went warm, and I said *no, course, I know, I didn't mean —,* and I took my hand back and said goodnight, and I went out into the dark feeling — and I have to be honest about this, it's the whole point of telling it — feeling *better.* Feeling held. Feeling like I'd been about to do something low, ask for paper, distrust the woman who fed me and praised me and called me Devlin, and she'd lifted me back up out of it with one hand and made me glad I'd not gone through with it.

I know now what she did. She read me, the gap between what my body wanted to ask and what my mouth could bear to say, and she put her conviction in the gap, so smooth the lie became the truth and I went on it, the way a frightened animal goes on it, because it trusts the conviction more than it trusts its own fear.

She rode me. The leg was very good. It had ridden for England.

I'd done the same thing to Marnie an hour earlier across the same silver and not seen it. I'd kept the valuable answer back and given her the cheap one and let her thank me for it. The two of us had sat at that table all night doing the one thing to each other, up and down the line — reading the soft place, putting the leg on, keeping the thing that was worth something — and not one of us had named it, because the table doesn't teach you the name. The table teaches you the move.

*

It was past midnight when I came up the hill, and the wine had me, and the cold had come down hard and clean off the Welsh side, frost starting on the verges, the whole valley gone silver-black and still. Hugo's jacket round me, cedar and the dead, full of a dinner I'd never be able to pay for, my name on a board an hour from the estate and a horse in a bolted box worth more every time I sat on him and less mine for it.

I went the long way, past the top field, because I always did now without admitting why.

Hob was at the rail.

He came over in the dark the way he always came, slow, big-bellied, sway-backed, forty if he was a day, breath steaming, those dinner-plate feet that hadn't worn a shoe in years and never would, the old useless cob bred at Tatefield and never sold and never going to be, kept for nothing, kept for no reason a chequebook would write down. He came to the rail and put his great soft head over it and breathed at me, slow, wanting nothing — not the carrot I didn't have, not a ride, not a result, not a thing I could do or stop being able to do. He just wanted to stand there in the frost and breathe at the boy on the other side of the rail because the boy was there.

I put my hand on his face. He let me. He'd have let anyone. That was the whole of him.

The Hall was lit up behind me, one warm window in all that dark, the silver going back behind glass, the cheque written, the season ahead, the horse I loved getting dearer in the bolted box up the hill. And I stood at the rail with the one creature in that whole valley that nobody needed for anything, and I didn't say it. I had the wine in me and the word right there and I didn't say it out loud, not to Hob, not to the dark, not to anyone.

I just stood there a long time, in the frost, with my hand on a face that wanted nothing off me, until the cold came up through my boots and the light in the Hall went out.


---

# Chapter Eleven — A Clean Vetting

I went past Hob's field most mornings after that and most mornings the old cob didn't bother getting up, just turned an ear at me from the wet grass and went back to the serious business of being kept for nothing, and I'd stand at the rail a minute with my breath smoking and then go down and muck out six boxes, because that was the deal, and I never said the thing I'd not said the night of the dinner, and I'm not going to say it now either. You can have it without me saying it.

The vet's van came up the lane on a Tuesday in the second half of May, and I heard it before I saw it — a diesel rattle with a knock in it that meant a head gasket on the way out, which I knew because half the cars on our estate had sounded like that the year before they went — and I came round the muck heap with the barrow and there it was, a long white Transit gone grey, *PRYCE & SON EQUINE* on the side in lettering that had been good once, the *& SON* peeling worse than the rest, which I filed without knowing yet what to do with it, the way you file everything.

"That'll be Iwan," Glenys said, coming out of the feed room with a haynet over her shoulder. "Beau's got the vetting today. Buyer's vet's not coming till Thursday, so Iwan's doing the pre-poke first, see there's nowt waiting to trip us." She looked at me. "You'll want to watch one. You've never seen a five-stage, have you, a proper one."

"Seen Bev hold a leg up and trot a pony off."

"That's not a vetting, love, that's a prayer." She nodded at the van. "Watch this one. You'll see one for real before the season's out, and it's better you've seen it on a horse where nobody's lying."

I didn't ask her what she meant by that. I should have. But you don't, do you. You file it.

---

He got out of the van the way big men get out of vans they've been in too long — in instalments, a hand on the door, a grunt, one boot then the other onto the gravel, and then a stretch with both fists in the small of his back that you could hear from across the yard. Iwan Pryce. Mid-fifties and gone soft over what had been a big frame, a face broken-veined across the cheeks the way men go who drive a hundred miles a day and finish each one with something amber, kind eyes sunk in tired flesh, and a wax jacket that I'd come to know never once that whole summer looked properly dry, dark across the shoulders like a horse that's been worked, as if the man drove through his own private rain.

"Now then," he said, to the yard in general, to the dog that wasn't there, to me. Soft Welsh, a good deal softer than Glenys's, the consonants worn round. "Got the kettle on, have you. Long road from a colic at Pembridge that took till four." He said it without complaint, the way you state weather. Then he saw Beau looking out over the half-door and his whole face changed, went open and young, and he crossed the yard to him talking low — "there's a boy, there's the old man, what've they got you doing now then, eh" — and put a hand flat on the grey's face, and Beau, who was a gentleman and waited for everything, leaned into it.

I'd been at Tatefield long enough by then to have learned to read who was real in a room by what they did when they thought no one was watching, and I watched Iwan Pryce stand there in the cold scratching an old grey gelding under the cheekbone with his eyes half shut, and I thought: that one's real. Whatever else he is. He likes the horses better than the people, same as Glenys, same as me. That's a thing you can trust.

I'd hold that read all summer. I'd be right about it and it wouldn't matter, which is a lesson I keep being taught and keep not learning.

---

He did Beau in the yard and the school and I followed and fetched what he wanted fetched and watched him work, and I'm going to tell you what a vetting is the way I learned it, by watching a man do it to a horse who had nothing to hide, because Glenys was right that it's better to see it clean first.

Stage one was at rest, in the box, and it took him the longest. He went over the whole horse with his hands the way I'd go over one with my hands — not looking, *feeling,* down the legs, every joint, thumb working the tendons for heat or filling, lifting each foot and reading the sole with his eyes and his knuckles both. He put a stethoscope on the grey's chest and his big head went still and far away, listening, and you could see him counting. He had a thing on a little torch, an ophthalmoscope, and he took Beau into the dark back of the box and shone it into each eye in turn, and the eye lit up gold and Iwan looked into it a long time, into the actual back of the horse, the place a man's not built to see, and said, quiet, "Cataract starting in the off one. Nothing today. Worth a note. He's twelve, he's allowed." He wrote it down.

Stage two was the trot-up, on the hard standing where the concrete was true. I led, Iwan watched. *Let him have his head, don't hold him up, you'll hide it if you hold him up* — and I gave Beau his head and ran and the old horse trotted out honest as daylight, and Iwan read the gait coming and going the way I read a horse's fear, by the whole shape of it moving, looking for the half-beat that isn't there in a sound one. Then the flexions: a hind folded right up tight at the hock and held, longer than felt kind, thirty seconds, forty, the joint pressed shut — "trot him off now, straight off" — and Beau trotted out of the held leg level, no hitch, none of the landing-short that says you've just squeezed an old joint and asked it to lie. "Good lad," Iwan said, and I didn't know if he meant me or the horse and I don't think he did either.

Stage three was under saddle, the heart and lungs put under it, Iwan listening after with the stethoscope on the heaving side, counting how fast the great bellows came back down to quiet. Then stage four, which is nothing, which is the rest — the horse getting his breath back while Iwan drank the tea Pip had brought and told us about the colic at Pembridge and a man near Kington who'd hung himself in his barn in March, said flat, between the colic and the weather. And stage five, the one that's the whole point: you trot him up *again,* cold now, after the work, because the work brings out what the fresh morning horse was strong enough to hide. Beau came back sound a third time, level as a spirit bubble, and that was the horse passed, give or take a cataract starting in an old eye that everyone could live with.

"He'll do for Thursday," Iwan said. "Their vet'll find the eye and write it down, mind, and a buyer who's keen on a 1.40 schoolmaster goes off the idea quick when there's a cataract in the note, however small. We'll see." He smiled, tired. "He's a good old horse. Whoever gets him is getting more than they'll know to be grateful for. If anyone gets him."

I didn't say anything. I was thinking about the grey head gone from the half-door, the gentleman who waited fed last, and I was thinking it the way you think a thing you've decided not to feel yet — and then, because the eye might do for it, the way you put a thing back down half-relieved and half-ashamed of the relief.

---

Then he took the blood.

He did it last, after the trot-up, after the pass, with Beau standing quiet and half asleep in the box, and it was such a small thing, such a nothing — he clipped a patch on the neck, a wipe, found the vein with his thumb the way you'd find a stud hole, and the dark blood came up into the tube, two tubes, three, and he capped them and turned them over a few times against the light and wrote on the labels with a stub of pencil he licked first.

"What's that for, then," I said. "If he's passed."

"Stored." He held one up, the dark glass of it, the blood gone nearly black inside. "Sealed and dated and put away cold. You keep it." He shrugged, settled the tubes into a foam block in his case. "Months, it'll keep, the way they do them now. Reason being" — and here his voice did a thing I'd notice, later, over and over, and not understand the first time, where it went careful and slow and slightly to the side of the plain word — "reason being, see, if there's ever a question after. Say a horse vets sound and then a fortnight on he's not right, and the new owner wonders was he given something on the day. Calmed. Helped along. Made to feel better than he was, just for the few hours it took to trot him up." He clicked the case shut. "Then you've got it, haven't you. There it is. You can send it off and they'll test it and it'll tell you. The horse can't lie to you and neither can his blood. It's there, look, if anyone ever wants to look."

He said it the way you'd explain a thing to a lad who was interested, which I was, because I'm always interested in the part of a thing that's actually true, and what I heard was a nice clean fact about an honest old horse, and I filed it the way I filed everything, neat, no weight on it, a little dark tube of nothing going into a foam block in a tired man's case.

I have gone back to that minute more times than I can count. Iwan Pryce holding up a vial of Beau's blood in the cold yard light and saying *the horse can't lie to you and neither can his blood, it's there if anyone ever wants to look,* and me nineteen and nodding, interested, with no idea at all that I was being shown the gun. Not threatened with it. Shown it. The way you'd show a lad round a kitchen and point out, in passing, where the knives are kept.

Nobody pointed at it. That's the thing about it. Nobody in that whole summer ever once pointed at it. It just sat there on its shelf the entire time, behind us all, cold, dated, true.

---

He stayed for the tea he'd already half drunk, and Glenys came and they did the talk of people who'd known each other thirty years and didn't need many words for it, and I leaned on the feed room door and listened, because that's where the real things get said, in the gaps, while two old hands cradle two cracked mugs.

"How's Marian," Glenys said.

"Ah." Iwan looked into his tea. "You know. Up and down. The Hereford appointment's August now, they put it back again." He turned the mug. "Cost of the diesel alone, mind, getting her there and back. They want you to come for the day and then they tell you to come back another day, and that's another tank of red." He said it lightly. He was a man who said heavy things lightly and you had to be listening for the weight under the light, which I was, because I'd grown up in a flat where my mum said *I'm grand, love, never better* in exactly the voice you say it in when you are the opposite of grand, and I knew that tune, I knew it in my sleep, I could read it on a Welsh vet in a cold yard same as I could read it down a phone line from my mother. The man was drowning. Quietly. Politely. In red diesel and a wife's hospital and a *& SON* that was peeling off a van because there was no son, or the son was gone, or the son had had more sense than to stay in a trade where the big agri-pharma surgeries in town undercut you on every wormer and you drove a hundred miles to a colic at four in the morning for a fee a man could no longer live on.

I read all that off him in the gap between two sips and I thought: poor sod. I felt for him. I genuinely did. That's the part I want down on paper, because it would be easier to tell this if I'd ever once thought Iwan Pryce was a bad man, and I never did, not then and not after. I liked him. I went on liking him the whole way through the thing he did, which is its own kind of horror, the liking, the way it doesn't switch off when it ought to.

"They charge too much, the lot of them," Glenys said. "And then they charge you for telling you they can't help."

"They do." He stood, in instalments, fist in the back. "Right. I'm away. I'll see you Thursday for the buyer's vet, shall I, hold his hand so he doesn't find anything I didn't." He paused at the van door and looked back at me. "You're the Sheffield boy. The one off the rogue." He said *the rogue* the way they all said it, as a fact about a horse, not a question. "Heard about you. They tell me you've got that one going." He nodded down toward the far north corner, where Saracen's box was, the top door open now, had been for weeks, a thing I was proud of out of all proportion. "Good. He's a horse and a half, that. Be a fortune one day if somebody doesn't break him first." He climbed in. "You watch the legs on him. The big jumpers, it's always the legs in the end. The scope's free. It's the legs you pay for."

And the diesel knocked and he rattled off down the lane and I stood there with *be a fortune one day* sitting warm in me, because I heard it as praise of the horse, which it was, and I didn't hear the other thing in it at all, the thing Iwan hadn't even meant, the thing that's true of a fortune, which is that a fortune is a thing that gets taken.

---

I rode Saracen that evening, after, when the others had gone up and the yard had gone that blue colour it goes in May, the first swallows of the year working the muck heap for flies, low and fast. He'd been getting good for weeks, but that evening he was good in the way that frightens you, where the horse is more than you asked for and gives it anyway. I did the work I did every day — the right rein, which was always the work, asking him soft and giving the second he gave, so the asking and the safety came at the same moment — and he put the fear down, blew it out long and low, and stood under me in the blue light not frightened, that minute, with me.

"You're going to be a fortune," I told him, walking him down on a long rein, patting the wet neck. I'd heard Iwan say it and I gave it to the horse like a present. "You're going to be worth a fortune, you, and they're all going to want you, and it's going to be us, you and me, doing it." And the near ear came back and listened to me being soft and stupid and proud, and he believed me, the way he believed me about everything, because I'd spent two months teaching him I was a thing it was safe to believe — and he believed me when I told him he'd be a fortune the same as he believed me when I told him the right rein wouldn't hurt.

He couldn't tell the difference. Neither could I. I didn't know it, and I was happy, and I want that on the record before the record gets any dirtier than it already is.

---

The thing I keep coming back to isn't the school or the swallows. It's the fridge.

Thursday, the buyer's vet did Beau again — found the eye, wrote it down, and the buyer went quiet on it the way they do, no, then a maybe, then nothing, and Caro was cross about it, exactly as Iwan said she'd be — and on the Friday I had to run a thing into Leominster for Glenys, a worming order off Iwan's practice, and I went into the little reception that smelled of disinfectant and wet dog, and through the half-open door to the back I could see the cool room where they kept things, the drugs cabinet locked, a steel fridge with a hand-written sign on it, and on a shelf in there, in a rack with a dozen others, little tubes of dark glass standing up in foam, each one with a strip of label and a date in pencil, the blood of a dozen horses that had vetted clean that month, Beau's somewhere among them, dark, sealed, cold, dated, true.

I saw it for half a second. I was looking for the receptionist. I thought nothing of it at all — a rack of tubes, a vet's fridge, the most ordinary thing in the world, a thing put there for safety, a thing that protects the honest, *it's there if anyone ever wants to look* — and then the receptionist came and I paid for the wormer and drove the red lanes home with the windows down and the May coming green over everything, happy, nineteen, the radio on, a fortune of a horse waiting in a box for me with one ear already turning toward the sound of the diesel coming up the lane.

The tubes stayed where they were. They'd stay there all summer, cold and dated and patient, behind everything that happened, and nobody ever once had to take them down.


---

# Chapter Twelve — A Number on Him

Because I was watching the horse. I was always watching the horse — which is, I've decided since, exactly how the worst things in your life get into the house. Not the front door. They come in through the bit you weren't watching, while you were watching the horse.

It was the Tuesday after Yorkshire, a regional thing at a centre forty minutes off, indoor school, county set, the sort of show where the rosettes are the size of a side plate and the prize for the 1.30m is a saddle pad and a voucher for the saddler nobody can afford. We'd not gone north for it. We'd hacked the lorry up the A road in the morning with the rain easing and the hedges coming green at last, and Saracen had loaded like a horse who'd decided, this season, that the box was a place you got fed at the other end of, which is not nothing for a horse who'd come off a ramp sideways in March with three men on the ropes. He walked up the bars and stood and ate his haynet and looked at me over the breast bar with one ear, and I'd thought, getting the studs in, *eight weeks. That's all it's been. Eight weeks and a box you walk into.*

He won it. I'm not going to be clever about how he won it, because there's no cleverness in it, it was just him being him in a ring that asked him a question he could answer. The course walked a forward seven down the long side and a tight rollback to a square oxer off the wall, and Saracen came round the turn with the off ear pinned on the oxer and the near ear tipped back to me, that split, one ear for the job and one for me, and three strides out I felt the right rein come live under my hand the way it does — not a fight, a question, *is the bad thing here too* — and I gave it to him, soft, all of it, and he believed me, and he stood off it and snapped his front legs up to his chin neat as a man tucking his hands in his pockets, careful, almost saying sorry to the pole he wasn't going to touch, and we landed and he blew out long through his nose and I had to not laugh out loud in front of the county.

Clear. Then a jump-off he had no business winning against a Berg-type kid on a horse that had cost more than the Hall, and Saracen turned inside him at the wall, tight, the kind of turn you can't school, the kind that's just trust spent at speed, and we beat them by a tenth, and the centre clapped the way the county claps, which is politely and with both eyebrows up, because who was this. A black horse nobody'd heard of and a kid in grey half-chaps.

I want it on record that for about ninety seconds, holding a saddle-pad rosette in an indoor school in the middle of nowhere with the rain back on the tin roof, I was as happy as I have ever been before or since. I've checked. I keep the list. It's near the top.

*

It was in the lorry park that the number came, and it came the way money always came at Tatefield — sideways, in someone else's mouth, for free.

I was sponging Saracen's legs off at the side of the box, boots laid out on the ramp to dry, the off-hind with its little white ring of coronet wet and shining where I'd hosed it, and a man came over. I'd clocked him in the collecting ring — you clock them, the dealers, the way you clock the one bloke at a party who's working: good gilet, bad smile, a phone he held like a tool. He stood at a polite distance and looked at Saracen the way Caro had looked at me on the gravel in March. Not at the horse's face. At the whole of him. The stand of him. Down to the off-hind and back up to the shoulder, and his eyes stopped a half-second on the scar, the pale hairless hand's-width on the near side, and moved on, and I felt myself go still the way you do when somebody puts their hand on something of yours in a shop.

"He's a nice type," the man said.

"He's all right," I said.

"Yours?"

"I ride him."

He smiled at that, the bad smile, like I'd told him something. "Wexley-Tate's, then." He looked at Saracen again. "Scopey. Careful with it, and all — I watched the oxer. You don't see careful and scope in the same horse much. Usually careful's a worrier and scope's a fool." He took a card out of the gilet without being asked and held it where I could take it or not. "If she ever wants to move him on, mind. There's people. The right horse at the right age, sound, that turn he did at the wall — you're looking at" — and he said it, the number, flat, the way you'd say the time — "six figures, easy, and that's standing here in a county car park in the rain. Get him round Hickstead and it's a different conversation altogether."

I didn't take the card. I remember that. I kept the sponge in my hand and the cold water running down my wrist and I said, "He's not for sale," and the man laughed, friendly, not unkind, and put the card back in the gilet.

"Course he is," he said. "Everything is. Lovely horse." And he went off to work the rest of the party.

I want to tell you the number didn't go into me. It went into me. It went in the way Caro's *you can feel it* had gone in on the gravel, somewhere warm and low and before I could vote on it, except this one didn't feel warm, it felt like swallowing a coin. Six figures. Easy. I stood there with my hand on the wet near shoulder, on the scar, the place I can never read, and I did a sum I'd never had reason to do in my life. I thought about what that number was in weeks of my mother's money. I thought about it in the farrier's three months owed. I thought about it in the tarp on the indoor roof, in breeze blocks and baler twine, in the dead bulbs in three boxes we mucked out by feel. And then I made myself stop, because the horse had turned his head round to me, hearing me go quiet, and put his nose against my arm, soft, the way he'd started to, eight weeks in, a thing he'd never done for anyone, and I thought, plain, the way I only think about him:

You're not a number. You don't even know there's numbers.

And he didn't. That was the whole of it, standing there in the rain. He didn't know. He thought he'd had a good day and got fed and done the turn at the wall I'd asked him for and been right to trust me about it. He thought we were all right. There wasn't a price anywhere in him.

There was one everywhere else now. I just couldn't see it yet, the way you can't see your own house is on fire from inside the warm.

*

It was the next evening, back at Tatefield, that I felt the click.

I don't have a better word for it. The motif agent in my head, if I had one, would want me to call it something. It wasn't a thing anyone said. It was bodies. It was the yard being a half-degree different around the horse and around me, and I read it the way I read everything, by where the tension had moved to, and I misread what it meant, which is the whole of being nineteen.

Glenys did his feed herself that night. She'd never done his feed; the rogues' feeds were the pupils' job, that was the whole arrangement, but she came down at four with the barrow and did Saracen's bucket with her own hands, soaked sugar beet and the good chaff and a scoop of the balancer we kept for the ones we were actually feeding up, not just keeping alive, and she stood at his door while he ate it with her arms folded over the worn tabard and the unlit roll-up behind her ear, and she didn't say anything daft to him, no *Sass you soft article,* none of the groom's love-talk she gave the others. She just watched him eat. Like you'd watch money. And when I came past with a barrow she looked at me and there was the pity in it again, the look she'd given me on the first day, the look that read me for what was going to be done to me, and she said, "He's doing well on it now, isn't he," and went back up the yard, and I stood there not knowing why my chest had gone tight, because all she'd said was that the horse was doing well.

And Curtis, that night in the caravan, said it out loud, because Curtis says the thing nobody else will and calls it a joke so it doesn't count.

"He's worth a few bob now, your black 'un," he said, not looking up from the three-string guitar. "Heard it off Glenys. Dealer at the centre. Six figures, she said." He grinned the gappy grin. "You want to mind he don't get nicked. People nick a horse like that, you know. Bolt cutters and a trailer in the night. Gone."

"He's not worth a few bob," I said. "He's a seven-year-old that two months ago they were putting on a lorry to a sale he weren't coming back from."

"Aye, and now he's not, is he," said Curtis. "Now he's an asset." He let the word sit there, plucking the one in-tune string, and then he said, gentler than usual, which was worse, "Funny how it works. He were worth nowt when he were dangerous. Worth a fortune now he's safe. And you made him safe." He looked at me then, just a flick, the hard streak surfacing under the ginger and the cold. "You want to think about who that's good for, Sheffield. 'Cause it weren't good for Quillan, were it. Being worth summat."

Pip, in the top bunk, in everything she owned and the hot-water bottle, didn't say anything at all. She'd not said much since they'd taken the mare off her. She turned over to the wall.

I didn't have an answer for Curtis. I had a feeling I didn't have words for, which is different, and it sat in me all night the way the hoof had sounded that first night through the gap at the top of the window — patient, deliberate, one strike and a pause and another, the only thing the box had left to say.

*

But here's the thing about that week, the thing I've turned over for ten years and still can't lay flat.

Caro was never warmer to me than she was that week. And I have to tell it true, because the easy version, the version where she goes cold the second the horse gets a price, where the money turns her to ice — that version is a lie, and it's a lie that lets me off, and I'm done with the ones that let me off.

She came and found me on the Thursday, in the indoor, on her own, no audience, nothing in it for anyone. I was schooling Saracen on the flat, just walk and trot, the dull good work nobody watches, working on the right rein the way I worked on it every day, asking him to soften to it for a stride and giving it back before he had to ask whether the bad thing was coming — building it the only way you can build it, a second at a time, never spending more trust than you've put in. And she stood in the gateway in the leaking north light with her ruined beautiful hands in her coat pockets and watched, and then she came in and stood by the wall, and I thought she'd come to take the school, to tell me what I was doing wrong, the way she did, the imperative softened to invitation. And she didn't.

"Halt him," she said. "Here. Off the rein. Drop your contact."

I did. Saracen stood. He'd learned to stand now, in the school, in the open, which he could only do because there was a door behind him he could see, a way out he didn't have to take. Caro came up his near side, slow, and put her hand flat on his neck, on the scar, exactly where mine goes, and he let her, which he didn't do for many, and she stood there a moment with her hand on the pale slick place where something had happened to him before any of us, and she said, quietly, not to me, half to the horse:

"I bought you for nothing, you know. I bought the engine and never read the rest of it. Everyone told me I was a fool." She moved her hand once, slow, along the neck. "I was a fool. I was just a fool who was right."

And then she turned to me and she did the thing she did, except this time I'd swear on the gold-cracked cup it wasn't a thing, it was real, because there was nobody to do it for, no chequebook in the room, no one watching her be generous. She taught me. She stood in the cold of her dead husband's indoor school under the tarp and the leak and she gave me, for free, the thing people had paid more than my mother earned in a year for an hour of, and she gave it to me the way you'd give a thing to someone you wanted to have it.

"You're riding him like you're frightened of frightening him," she said. "Which is correct, for now, because you are and you should be. But there's a day coming, soon, when you'll have to ask him for more than he wants to give, and the kindness then won't be in not asking. It'll be in how you ask." She walked round to his off side, his bad side, the right side, and she put her hand on the right rein where it hung, and she didn't pull it, she just held the weight of it, light, the way you hold a door for someone, and Saracen turned his eye to her, white in the corner, the question coming up, *is the bad thing here,* and Caro said, soft, "There. You see? He asks. They all ask. The great ones — Hugo used to say it — the great ones aren't the ones who never frighten a horse. There's no such rider. The great ones are the ones the horse believes when they say *not yet.*" She let the rein go gentle back to the buckle. "You can already say *not yet* to him. I've watched you. I don't know where you learned it. I don't think you learned it on a horse." She looked at me then, straight, and there was something in her face I'd not seen, something that might have been tenderness and might have been hunger and I've never been able to tell them apart on her, not then and not since. "You learned it somewhere it cost you. I'm sorry for that. And I'm glad of it, because it's made you the only person alive who can ride this horse, and I'm too honest to pretend I'm not glad. Both. Always both, with me. You'll learn that about me, if you stay."

I'll be straight with you. It was the best coaching I ever had in my life, and it was a true and loving thing, given at cost to a kid who'd never been given anything, and I'd have walked into a wall for her by the end of it. And I have spent ten years knowing that she said it — *the only person alive who can ride this horse* — three days after the number came, and not being able to decide whether she taught me that afternoon because she loved me or because the horse had just become worth six figures with me on him and worth scrap with anyone else, and whether those are even two different things, and whether the warmth was the leg she put on me the way she put it on everything. I can't tell. That's what I came away from Tatefield with, more than anything else. Not the rosettes. The not being able to tell. She gave me, that afternoon, both the kindest thing and the most useful thing, in the same breath, with the same hand, and refused, the way she refused everything, to let me know which it was. Maybe she didn't know either. Maybe nobody does. Caro Wexley-Tate was alive then, with her hand on the horse's scar, telling me I was the only one who could ride him, which I sometimes have to make myself believe was true.

*

I rode him every evening that week, after the others had gone up, in the last of the light, just the two of us and the leak ticking into the bucket somebody'd put under the worst of the roof.

I wasn't building the price tag. That's the thing I'd say to myself if I could go back, except I couldn't have heard it, because from inside it doesn't feel like building anything but trust. It feels like the cleanest thing your hands have ever done. You ask a frightened animal whether the bad thing is coming and you tell him, with everything you've got, *not yet, not while I'm here,* and he believes you, and he gives you the half-stride nobody else gets, and you go over the fence together and land and he blows out long and low through his nose, and you'd swear on anything you like that what's passing between you is the realest thing in the world and has no price on it at all.

It did have a price on it. The better I made him believe me, the more he was worth, and the more he was worth, the surer the day was coming when believing me would be the thing that took him away. I just thought I was loving him. She'd told me herself, with her hand on his scar, and I'd heard a coaching tip.

It was full dark when I untacked him that night. I slid the saddle off his wet back and the steam came up off him in the cold and his coat was coming, the summer coat, the wet-conker colour starting under the winter brown over his quarters, dappling where he was well, where the good feed was doing what good feed does. I put the rug on him and did up the surcingle and he stood for all of it, soft, one hind leg cocked, the off-hind with its little ring of white, and he turned his head round and put his nose against my chest and stood there breathing into my jacket, slow, the near ear tipped back to me and the off ear forward on the dark end of the school where the noise lives, one ear for me and one for the world, the split, the proof.

"Nobody can take this," I told him.

I said it plain because there's no other way I can talk to him. I meant the standing in the dark. I meant the breath in my jacket, the ear tipped back, the not-being-alone of it, the eight weeks of *not yet* that had made a box he'd walk into and a neck I could put my hand on the scar of and a horse who'd turn inside a fancier horse at the wall just because I'd asked. I meant that whatever they did with the rest of it — the rosettes, the diesel, the figures in somebody's mouth — this part was ours, the dark and the breath, and it didn't have a number, and it couldn't be sold because nobody else could even reach it.

I believed it completely. I was nineteen and happier than I have ever been, and I believed it completely.

Across the yard, the office window in the end of the Hall was lit, the one room that gets a bulb, and through it I could see Caro at the desk with the brown envelopes and the pound-shop calculator and the chequebook, her head down, her ruined hand moving, and I thought, the way I'd thought all week, *she loves him, look, she's working half the night for him, for all of us,* and I lifted my own hand off the horse's neck to wave, glad, full of it.

She didn't see me. She was writing something down. I found out later — much later, when it was the kind of late that doesn't help — what she'd been writing that week, in the ledger she kept the real sums in, the one no envelope ever came in for. She'd written the number down. The dealer's number, the six figures, easy. She'd written it under the horse's name in her own hand, the way you write down a thing so you don't lose it, so it's there if anyone ever wants to look. And next to it, because she was honest, because she was always honest about the one thing nobody wanted her to be honest about, she'd written what Hickstead would make it.

The horse breathed into my jacket. The near ear stayed tipped back to me, listening, in the dark, not knowing. And in the lit window Caro put a number on him, in her own beautiful ruined hand, and did not look up.


---

# Chapter Thirteen — The Line

The near ear stayed tipped back to me in the dark a long while that night, after Caro had put the number down in her own hand across the yard, and I went to sleep thinking it meant the horse was mine. I want to be honest that I went to sleep happy. I had no idea I was three days off the worst thing my hands ever did, or that it wouldn't even be to him.

It was the mare. It's always the one you're not watching.

Quillan had a buyer coming on the Saturday. I knew that the way I knew everything that mattered at Tatefield — sideways, off Curtis, who heard it off Glenys, who'd typed nothing and signed nothing but knew the shape of every deal in the valley before it had a name. A man from down Newbury way. He had a daughter doing the junior tour, and the daughter had been promised something special, and Quillan was special, and there was a number, and the number was the kind that fixes a roof.

So that was the clock. Saturday, the man, the daughter, the cheque. And the thing standing between the cheque and the roof was a five-year-old mare who had decided, sometime in the last fortnight, that she would die before she went into water.

*

It had come on her the way these things come on the young ones — out of nowhere and from everywhere. There was a liverpool at the regional show she'd not even jumped, just been led past, a tray of blue under a rail with the sun on it, and something in the flash of it had got into her and stayed. By the Thursday she'd stop at a hose left running across the school door. By the Friday she'd plant and spin at a puddle in the gateway, drop her shoulder and be gone the other way, all that copper power going sideways under you like a door coming off its hinge, and you'd sit it because I'll sit anything, but you weren't riding her any more by then. You were a passenger on a frightened thing.

And there was a water tray in the buyer's course. Of course there was. The man had asked, careful and polite down the phone — Glenys did him in a voice for us, all *the going all right, is it, and she'll pop a little water for us, the daughter does love a water* — and Caro had said yes, naturally, she's bold as you like, water's nothing to her, come Saturday.

Water was not nothing to her. Water was the end of the world.

"She'll not go near it," I said. I was sponging Saracen off at the time, Friday teatime, the light going gold and thin across the quad, and Caro had come down to the rogues' end to find me, which she did when she wanted something, because she never came to the rogues' end for nothing.

"She will," Caro said, "for you."

I knew what she was doing. I'd watched her do it to me on the gravel in March and I'd watched her do it to Pip over tea and I'd watched her do it to a man with a chequebook, and I could name every part of the move while it was happening to me, and it worked anyway, every time, because the place she put her hand was the place I was hungry. *For you.* Two words. They went in low and warm before I could vote on them, the way they always did.

"I don't want to ride her at the water tomorrow cold," I said. "Not first off in front of a buyer. If she stops at it cold he'll see her stop and that's the sale, isn't it. He won't want a mare that stops at water for his daughter."

"No," Caro agreed. "He won't." She looked at me over the wet shine of Saracen's quarters, and she let the silence do the asking, the way she let silence do most of her asking. The summer evening ticked. A swallow went over the muck heap and back.

"So I'll school her at it tonight," I said. "While there's light. Get her over it once. Just once. So she knows tomorrow it can be done."

"Good boy," said Caro, and put her hand flat on Saracen's neck on her way past, on the scar, where it always went, and the horse let her, and she went back up the yard.

*Good boy.* That went in too. It always did. I was nineteen and starving for it, and she fed me a word at a time, and never the whole loaf, and I'd have walked into the water myself for one more.

*

I want to set down exactly what I did, because I've spent ten years pretending the line was somewhere it wasn't, and I'm done with that.

I tacked the mare up myself. I built a little tray in the indoor — a sheet of blue plastic out of the muck shed, a couple of poles laid flat to make a frame, no height at all, just the blue and the shine and the wrongness of it on the ground where ground should be. I put Saracen in first, on the buckle, just stood him in the corner so the mare would have company, because a horse'll do for a friend what it won't do for a man. And I got on Quillan.

She knew before I asked. They always know before you ask; that's the whole of the gift, that you know that they know. I felt it come up through the saddle, that copper electricity going still, and her head came up, and her ear pinned, and I brought her round to the blue at a walk and she saw it and stopped dead twenty feet off with her whole body locked and trembling.

And here it is. Here's the bit.

I knew that stop. I'd never call her stop a sod's stop, the way they called Saracen's stop a sod's stop. Her stop was an honest no. It was a five-year-old animal telling me the truest thing she had to tell, which was *that is going to hurt me and I will not go.* It was the same no Saracen gave off the ramp in March, the same no I'd built my whole self around answering. The flicker came under her eye — she's got a wide kind eye, the mare, white at the corner when she's scared — the little fast tic, there and gone, the held breath, the last second she was still asking me a question.

And the question was the only question any of them ever asks. *Do I have to do this on my own, or are you here.*

In March, in the bolted box, when Saracen asked me that, I'd answered it. I'd given him company in the worst second and taken nothing. That was the cleanest thing my hands ever did and I'd wanted it on the record before the record got dirty, and now here was the record getting dirty, and I knew it while I did it, which is the part I can't lay flat.

Because I didn't answer her question. I used it.

I read the flicker — the last second she was still asking — and instead of telling her the truth, which was *yes, that might hurt, and no, I won't make you,* I told her the lie. I closed the gap between my body and my hands the way I'd learned to do it reading my mother across a kitchen in Parson Cross, so that nothing leaked, so that everything in me said *not yet, not while I'm here, the bad thing isn't coming,* and I rode her at the blue with a leg that didn't argue and a hand that promised, and I asked her to override the truest thing she knew because a man from Newbury was coming in the morning with a cheque that would fix a roof.

She went. God help me, she went. She believed me — she put her trust in the next ten seconds because I'd told her she could, and she gathered herself and lurched over the blue plastic with her knees up round her ears and landed bucking, not from joy this time, from fright spent, and I sat the bucks and brought her round and made much of her, *good girl, good girl,* and did it twice more so she'd believe it tomorrow, and each time the flicker came smaller and her trust came easier, and that was the worst of it. The easier it came, the worse it was.

I didn't feel it as a thought. I want that clear, because it would be tidier if I could say I thought, in that moment, *this is wrong.* I didn't think anything. I felt it in the body, the way I feel everything — a coldness opening up under the warm where the joy lives, my own hand going strange to me on the rein, a held breath I couldn't let go of. There was a stillness in me that matched the stillness she'd had before she broke. I'd gone the thing I read in them just before they lose it. I just didn't lose it. I held it, and used it, and rode the lie home.

And after, untacking her in the dusk, the mare blowing and dark with sweat and trusting me completely now, leaning her wet face into my chest the way they do when you've got them past the worst of themselves — after, I said it to myself, once, plain, in a sentence I've never been able to unsay:

Getting her to trust me and getting her to do the thing that frightens her are the same. There's no line. I just did the second one on purpose, for money that isn't even mine.

Saracen stood in the corner on the buckle and watched the whole of it with his off ear forward on the blue, because even on a loose rein, even with nothing asked of him, he didn't trust water either. He didn't know what he'd watched. He thought we'd had an evening in the school. The near ear was tipped back to me across the whole length of it, listening, the way it always was, and I couldn't look at it.

*

She jumped the water for the man in the morning like she'd been born to it.
