That's the thing nobody tells you about the line, that on the far side of it the work just *works.* The Newbury man came, and his daughter, fifteen and good and frightened in the careful way the well-taught are frightened, and Caro had the silver out of nowhere — not the dinner silver, the charm silver, the *we've nothing to prove* silver — and there was coffee in the good cups in the kitchen and Glenys had Quillan plaited up to the nines with her copper coat shining and QUILLAN coming off the headcollar strap in Pip's careful biro, which nobody thought to change.

I rode her first to show her off. I took her round a little course the daughter would jump after, easy, flowing, and I brought her to the water tray Curtis had built proper in the outdoor, blue and bright and bigger than mine, and three strides out I felt the flicker want to come, the honest no rising in her one more time — *that, I can't, are you here* — and I gave her the lie again, smooth as anything now, smoother than last night, because I'd done it once and the second time is always easier — and she sailed it, ears pricked, like water was her favourite thing in the world.

The man's face changed. I watched it change. You watch a buyer's face the way you watch a horse, and his went from polite to wanting in the space of one water jump, and I'd done that, my hands had done that, and Caro, at the rail, in her mended coat with her ruined beautiful hands folded, watched the man's face change and then turned her head a fraction and looked at me, and there was the look — the one I can never read, the one that's tenderness and hunger in the same face, the one she gave me in the indoor with her hand on Saracen's scar. *Good boy.* She didn't say it. She didn't have to. I'd already heard it.

The daughter got on. Quillan was a saint for her, because I'd asked her to be — because I'd spent the trust the night before and the mare had it left over to spend on a stranger this morning, that's how trust works, you put it in one body and it comes out another. The girl popped her round, popped the water, beamed. The man said *we'll do the vetting, then, shall we,* which is how a man from Newbury says yes. Caro said *of course, Iwan's very good, he's local,* and it was done, the cheque was as good as written, the roof was as good as fixed, and everyone was so pleased with me.

That's the bit. Everyone was so pleased with me. Caro put her hand on my shoulder, which she didn't do, and said, low, so the man wouldn't hear, "I don't know how you do it. I really don't," and it was real, I'd swear it was real, there was nobody to perform it for, and it went into me warm and low exactly where she always put it, and I was so glad, even then, even knowing — I went glad all through, the way you do when someone wants you — and the gladness sat on top of the cold thing in my chest and didn't touch it.

I got paid in being wanted. I always got paid in being wanted. It was the only currency I'd ever learned to take.

*

Pip was there. I'd half forgotten she would be, which tells you something about what I'd become in a fortnight — that I'd forgotten the girl whose mare I was selling.

She'd plaited Quillan that morning. She'd got up in the dark and done the mare's mane in little tight rosebuds the way you do for a sale, the way she'd done it a hundred times dreaming the mare was going up the grades under *her*, and she stood at the edge of it all in her body-warmer worn through at the zip and watched the Newbury man's face change at the water, and watched me do the thing that changed it, and she didn't say anything, because Pip never said anything, that was her whole tragedy, she made herself small and useful and waited to be chosen and wasn't.

But she looked at me. After, when the man had gone in for his coffee and the mare was being walked off and the deal was a deal. She caught my eye across the yard and she held it a second longer than was comfortable, and her kind broad face that hides nothing had something on it I didn't have a word for then. *Recognition.* She'd seen what I'd done. Not the schooling — anyone could school a horse at water. She'd seen the *how* of it, the thing in my hands, the lie I'd told the mare, because Pip rode without the gift and so she could see the gift from outside, the way the deaf see the shape of a lie in a mouth.

She'd watched me make her mare go quiet so it could be taken from her. And she knew the going-quiet for what it was, because it had been done to her too, gently, over tea, *too much on, darling, you understand.* And she looked at me and I understood she'd put it together — that the same hands that gentled the rogues were the hands that did the taking, in the same motion, with the same softness — and that I was, this morning, on the wrong side of the only line that had ever mattered to her.

I should have gone to her. I didn't go to her. I couldn't, because if I'd gone to her she'd have seen me see it too, and I wasn't ready to be seen seeing it, not yet, maybe not ever. So I turned away and made busy with a haynet I didn't need to do, and she turned away too, and that was the conversation we had, the two of us, with our backs, across the yard her mare was being sold out of.

*

They loaded Quillan on the Tuesday after the vetting cleared.

A stranger drove the lorry — the man's own box, smart, dark green, worth more than the Hall, a ramp that came down smooth and level on its hydraulics with none of the gaffer-tape and rust of ours. And Quillan, who three weeks ago wouldn't load for love nor money, walked up the ramp like a lady, because I'd taught her the box was a place you got fed at the other end of, because I was good at it, because the same lie that got her over the water got her up the ramp, and the better I'd made her the easier she went to the man taking her away.

I stood and watched the ramp come up. There's a sound a hydraulic ramp makes coming up that ours never made, a smooth expensive hiss, and the bar clanged across, and through the slats I could see one copper eye and then I couldn't, and the diesel turned over clean, and she was gone up the lane in a smell of money, QUILLAN in Pip's biro going to Newbury where they'd change the headcollar by teatime.

My hands hung at my sides. The hands that had done it. I looked at them like they belonged to someone I was only just getting to know.

And then I did the thing I'd swear I didn't decide to do, the way I never decide to find the scar. I went down the yard to the rogues' end, to the box that wasn't bolted any more, and I let myself in to Saracen.

He turned to me with his whole self, the way he did now, eight weeks on, a thing he'd never done for anyone before me — turned and put his near side to me and dropped his poll a degree and blew out, low, long, deciding to trust the next ten seconds, deciding I was the safe thing in the world. And my thumb went to the scar without me, to the pale slick hand's-width on the near shoulder, the one place I can't read, the page torn out, the thing that happened to him before any of us that nobody alive can name — and I rubbed it the way you'd rub a thing off your hands, the way Glenys rubs at a mark that won't come out, and the horse leaned into it, grateful, because he thought I was loving him.

I was. That's the part I can't get to lie flat even now. I was loving him. And the near ear tipped back to me, trusting, listening, one ear for me and one for the dark end of the school, the split, the proof, the thing I'd ride into a fire for — and for the first time since the bolted box in March, since the very first second I ever answered the question instead of using it, the trust frightened me.

It frightened me because I knew now, in the body, what I was. I knew what these hands did. I'd just watched them do it, twice, for money, and be praised for it, and like it. And here was the one I'd swear I'd never do it to, leaning his whole frightened half-ton of self into the thumb on his scar, believing me completely, the near ear home on me in the gathering dark — and I stood there in the box and could not, for the life of me, find the line between the love and the lever. I went looking for it with everything I had. It wasn't there. It had never been there. There was only how good I was getting at not feeling where it would be.

"I won't do it to you," I told him, plain, because there's no other way I can talk to him. "Not you."

The near ear stayed tipped back. He believed me. That was the worst of it. He believed me, the same as the mare believed me at the water, the same as I believed Caro on the gravel, and not one of us, not one single one, had any paper on it at all.


---

# Chapter Fourteen — Easier

The gelding's name was Foxglove, which tells you everything about the people who owned him, and he wouldn't go past the muck heap.

It started small. It always starts small; that's the trick of it. A livery client's horse, a fat bright bay called Foxglove who was meant to do a 1.10m at a local riding-club affiliated thing on the Saturday and had got it into his head that the world ended at the muck heap, and would nap home off the back of the school every time you tried to take him past it onto the hack track, plant his feet and screw his quarters round and go backwards if you argued, which the daughter who owned him couldn't sit and the mother who paid for him wouldn't watch. So it came to me. Most things did, by June. *Dev'll sort him.* That was the phrase. It had been *the Sheffield boy* in March and *Dev'll sort him* by June, and I'd not noticed the change happen, the way you don't notice the light go in a long evening until you look up and can't read.

It was a Thursday, two days after Quillan went south in a stranger's lorry, and I'd not slept right since. That's the only excuse I've got, and it isn't one, for how little the next part cost me.

I got on Foxglove in the school and warmed him up boring, long and low, nothing to argue with. Then I took him toward the gap in the boards that let onto the hack track, past the muck heap, and ten strides out I felt him start to go — the head come up, the back hollow, the quarters begin their swing to plant and spin — and I read it. That's all it was, at first. I read him. The way the weight came off his right hind and loaded his left so he could pivot on it; the breath held high in the chest; the half-second where his whole body asked the question, *do I have to, is it safe, is the world ending past that brown hill of stink* — and I knew, the way I know, exactly where his trust lived and exactly which hand to put it in.

And I rode him through it.

Not cruelly. I want that understood, because it matters to me still, that I never once was cruel, that cruelty would almost have been cleaner. I rode him through it kind. I closed the gap between what my hands said and what my legs said until there was no daylight in him for the *no* to get through, and I gave him the one thing that lives in the gift, which is the feeling of not being alone with the fear, and he took it, because they always take it, and he walked past the muck heap on a loose rein with his ears swinging and his back come up under me soft as a couch, and the daughter on the gate said, "Oh my *God*," and the mother said, "However did you—," and I said, "He's all right, he just needed company past it," and the thing I felt, the only thing, was that it had taken me about four minutes.

Four minutes. In March it would've taken me a week and half my heart. I'd have lain in the dark working out his particular fear, gone slow, gone soft, met him a hundred times where he was frightened and never once asked him past it until he asked himself. By June I could do it in four minutes off the back of a school, with a bay I didn't love and would never see again, because I'd done it enough times now that my hands knew the route without me, and I rode him past the muck heap and felt nothing where the something used to be, and I didn't even clock the nothing. That's the part. The week before, with Quillan, I'd felt a cold place open where the joy had been, a hand that wouldn't quite go to the rein. With Foxglove, two days later, there was no cold place. There was just a horse going nicely and a mother reaching for her purse and me saying, "No, honestly, it's fine," because you didn't take cash off livery clients, that was the family's, and I'd learned the rules of whose money was whose without ever seeing a penny of any of it.

I rode him past the muck heap three more times to set it, and each time was easier than the last, and the easiness was the whole of it, and I did not feel the easiness as a loss. I felt it as skill. I felt good. I'd got cleverer.

I want to be honest that I felt good.

---

He went round his 1.10m that Saturday clear and got a third, and the family told everyone at the riding club that *the boy at Tatefield* had sorted him, and by the Monday two more horses had been spoken about in my direction — a sharp pony that bolted at one specific gateway, a hunter that wouldn't load — and Caro, hearing it at the kitchen table over the brown envelopes she never opened in front of you, said without looking up, "You're becoming useful," and meant it as the highest thing she had, and it went into me warm the way her things always went in, low and quick before I could vote on it.

Useful. I'd have died for the word. I more or less did.

The thing about being good at it was that being good at it paid — not in money, there was never money, the money was a different country I worked the borders of and never crossed — but in everything that, to me, was better than money, because I'd never had it and didn't trust money anyway. I got the rides. I got the standing. Curtis still mucked nine boxes and so did I but I rode six horses a day now and he rode the cob to the bottom field and back, and we both knew it, and he took the piss about it in the caravan at night — "His Highness, the horse-whisperer of Parson Cross" — and there was a blade in it sometimes and I let the blade go past because he was right and we both knew he was right. Pip rode what was left after I'd had the good ones. She didn't take the piss. She'd watch me bring a horse back into the yard that had gone out an hour before with its head in the air and its eyes white, and she'd watch it come back on the buckle, soft, and she'd say "that's amazing, Dev," and mean it, and there'd be something under the meaning it that I didn't look at too hard because I couldn't have done anything with it, and because Quillan's box was empty at the smart end of the yard and we both knew whose hands had emptied it.

The horror was the ease. I'm telling you that now because I didn't have it then. I thought I was happy. I was happy. That's not a contradiction, it just took me ten years to stop needing it to be one.

---

The letter came on the Wednesday.

I knew it was a letter and not the usual brown weather because Glenys went quiet about it, and Glenys went quiet about almost nothing — she had a comment for the price of feed, the state of the roof, the cataract starting in Beau's off eye, the lot, a running dry Welsh weather-report on the slow sinking of the whole concern — but she came out of the feed room that morning with her face shut and a roll-up behind her ear she wasn't lighting harder than usual, and when I said "all right, Glenys?" she said "go and do your horses, love," which she never said, because doing my horses was the one thing nobody ever had to tell me.

It was on the kitchen table at dinner. I'd stopped, by June, being frightened of the kitchen — I ate at the long scrubbed table most nights now, family table, dead man's chair, the thing I'd mistaken in March for being kept and was still half-mistaking — and it was face up under Caro's hand, which was flat on it, and I read it the way you read anything face up under a hand that's stopped moving. You don't read the words. You read the hand.

The hand wasn't shaking. That was the first thing. Caro's hand never shook; she'd organised seventy-one years around her hand not shaking. It just lay there, flat, on the paper, the signet ring loose on the thin finger, the way she'd had it on the table in March when she put it out to me to shake, and I thought, before I'd read a word, *whatever this is, she's deciding something on top of it.* She talked to me with her hand flat on a thing the way people talk when they've decided before you came in.

"There's a date on it now," she said. She didn't move the hand. "There was always going to be a date. They've put it in writing, which is their version of manners." She turned the letter round on the wood with two fingers and slid it the length of the table to me, and I want you to understand that Caro Wexley-Tate did not show people her post, did not show people anything that fell off her, and that her sliding that letter down the table was either the most honest thing she ever did to me or the most calculated, and I have spent ten years not being able to tell, the same as everything, the same as all of it.

I read it. I'm not going to give you the whole of it, the *whereas* and the *facility* and the *aforementioned*, the legal way money dresses up to come and take a house. I read the figure. I read the date. The figure was the kind of number I'd only ever heard about houses and never about money you owed, a number with a comma in a place that made my stomach go, and the date was the last working day of October, and the two of them sat in the same sentence — this much, by then — like a fence and a clock, like a height and a time allowed, and I did the only thing I knew how to do with a fence and a clock, which was the maths.

I didn't decide to do the maths. The maths did itself. It's the thing I'd been good at before I was ever good at horses, the one school thing, the numbers — I could do striding a line in my head walking it once, sixes and sevens and the half-stride hidden in a related distance, I read the world in it — and the maths did itself across the scrubbed table in the cold kitchen while Caro watched my face do it, and the maths came out one way, the way it could only come out, the way she'd known it would come out before she slid me the paper:

There was one thing on the place worth that number. There was one asset in the whole drowning concern, the silver included, the land included, that could stand against a figure with a comma in that place. And he was in the far north box with the bolted top door, seven years old, near-black, with a scar nobody could explain and a coronet of white on his off-hind and one ear that tipped back to listen for me, and I had spent eight weeks and the best of myself making him worth exactly that much.

I pushed the thought down. I want that on the record too — that I felt it come up, the whole shape of it, complete, the fence and the clock and the one horse that could clear them, and I pushed it back down under the water with both hands and held it there till it stopped moving, and I told myself the thing I told myself all that summer, which was that the win would save him. That if I got him round, got him good, got him to the Grand Prix that paid that figure in one round, the prize money would clear the debt and the horse would be safe, and I'd ride him into October and out the other side and keep him, that the winning was the saving and not the selling, that they were different things and not the same thing wearing the same coat.

"It'll be the Grand Prix, then," I said. My voice came out level. I was proud of how level it came out, which should have told me something.

Caro looked at me for a moment with the white hair pulled hard off the long weathered face, and there was something in it I'd not seen before, a kind of tiredness she didn't let people see, the tiredness of a woman who'd been doing this maths longer than I'd been alive and knew every place it came out wrong.

"It'll be the Grand Prix," she said. "Among other things." And she took the letter back, slid it the length of the table the way you'd take a bridle off a nail, and folded it once and put it inside her cardigan against her chest, where I couldn't see it, where the figure and the date went to live, and she said, "Eat your dinner, Devlin," and I ate it.

I ate the whole of it. Fast, the way I ate, in case there wasn't more. I'd never in my life left food on a plate and I didn't start that night, the night the clock started, the night I did the maths and pushed the answer under the water — I sat in the dead man's chair at the family table and ate every scrap of what they fed me and felt, God forgive me, looked after.

---

Glenys was in the yard when I came out, later than I should have been, doing a last check on Beau's off fore that had filled a little, running it cold with the hose in the dark, the water silver in the one working light, and she didn't look up when I crossed the cobbles to do my own last round, just said into the horse's leg, "You'll have seen the letter."

"I saw it."

"Aye." The hose ran. Beau stood with one hind cocked, half asleep, twelve years old and stiff and worth a price and no fight left in him, the horse she'd sell first. "October," she said. "End of October. A nice long rope, that. Long enough to get good and used to the idea." She turned the hose off and stood up slow, a hand on her own wrecked back, and looked at me across the cob's neck. "You did Foxglove on Thursday. Four minutes, the daughter said. A horse that's napped home off that school all spring." She wasn't smiling. "In March that'd have took you a fortnight. You'd have lost sleep on it."

The look had the pity in it, the same pity she'd had in March top-to-toe in the yard, the look that read me not for what I could do but for what was going to be done to me — except now there was a new thing folded into it, and the new thing was that some of the doing was mine.

"He just needed company," I said. "Past it."

"That what you give them," she said. "Company." She let it sit, not a question, the way the family let things sit. "You're getting clever at it, love. Cleverest I've seen, and the clever ones go furthest and they fall the furthest and all." She picked up the hose and coiled it over her square forearm, neat, a thing her hands did without her. "Mind what clever costs," she said. "That's all I'll say tonight."

That was the whole of it, that night — *mind what clever costs* — and the rest came later, the full thing, the line that did me in. She rationed it on purpose, I've always thought, gave me the warning in instalments she could afford, the way the whole place gave you everything in instalments it could afford, board this week, coaching that week, eventually a horse, your word, mind yourself. She hung the hose on its nail and went up the cobbles to her own caravan by the hedge, her own thirty years sunk in the drowning, and she left me in the yard with the one light and the filled-leg cob and the maths I'd pushed under the water bobbing back up the second I was on my own.

---

I went to him last. I always went to him last, kept him like the good thing at the end of a plate, the thing you make yourself wait for.

I drew the bolt and the top door came open into the dark and he was there, the great near-black shape of him, head low, ears soft, the white of the off-hind coronet just catching the spill of the yard light, and he turned to me and blew out, long and low through his nose, the sound that meant he'd decided to trust the next ten seconds, the sound only I ever got off him, and the near ear came back to me and the off ear stayed out on the dark of the box, one ear for me and one for the world, the split, the proof, and I stood at his door in June with the figure and the date going round in me like water round a drain, and I did the thing I did, which was put my thumb on the scar.

It found it on its own, my thumb. The hand's-width of hairless pale skin on the near shoulder, slick under the winter brown that was nearly gone now, the summer coat coming through underneath the colour of a wet conker, dappling over the quarters where the good feed had done its work. The one place on him I'd never been able to read. The page torn out. A kick, a gate, a fall, a man — nobody alive could tell me, and the not-knowing was the only honest thing left in the whole concern, the one fact nobody could spin or slide down a table or fold into a cardigan, that something had happened to this horse before any of us and none of us would ever undo it or even name it.

He let me. He stood and let my thumb work the scar in the dark the way it had a hundred times, and he dropped his poll a degree, and the near ear stayed home on me, and his trust came off him steady and warm and complete, the whole of him handed over into my hands the way I'd taught him, eight weeks of *not yet, not while I'm here,* eight weeks of teaching him that the bad thing wasn't here when I was, and I stood there and felt all of it pour into me, every coin of it, and for the first time it wasn't only the cleanest thing my hands had ever done.

Because I'd just done the maths. And I knew now what the trust was worth — not to him, he didn't know there was a price anywhere in him, he never would — but in the one country I worked the borders of and never crossed, the country with the commas in the wrong place. I knew, with my thumb on the scar he'd carried from before all of us, the exact figure that this — the dropped poll, the long blow, the near ear come home — would clear by the last working day of October.

I want to tell you I took my hand off him.

I didn't. I let him trust me, and I looked out over the half-door at the one thing in the dark yard that wasn't dark: the office window at the corner of the Hall, where late as it was a bulb was burning yellow behind the curtain, and a thin straight shape was sitting at a desk with the letter, doing the same maths I was doing, getting the same answer.

There was one thing on the place worth what the paper said. I knew which box it stood in. I was standing in it.

So I made myself the one promise I had left, the only one big enough to set against the figure and the date, and I made it out loud, because out loud was the only kind that ever held: "I'll win it. I'll win the thing that pays it, and then there's no reason left to sell you." His near ear came round at the sound of my voice and tipped back, and stayed, and I told myself a horse that listens like that to a man is a horse that man gets to keep. It was the maths I wanted, not the maths the letter did. I knew the difference even then. I said it anyway, in the dark, to the only one in the valley who'd believe me.


---

# Chapter Fifteen — Windsor

The letter with the date on it stayed in my head all the way down the motorway — the figure, and the word *October*, sitting on the kitchen table under the salt cellar where Caro had put it like a thing she didn't want and couldn't move. I'd done my sum about it in the dark of the lorry cab three times before Membury and given it up. There was only one thing in that yard worth what the letter said. I knew which box it stood in. I'd stopped letting myself get to the end of the thought, the way you stop tonguing a tooth that's going to need pulling.

Then the lorry came down off the M4 into Windsor and I forgot the letter for the best part of a day, because I'd never in my life seen money lie about that flat and that easy and that unbothered.

Hartpury had been a field with a burger van. Yorkshire had been a great house's park gone to ruin and let to a show. This was the Queen's back garden with a castle on the skyline like a thing painted on for the brochure, and the lorry park wasn't a lorry park, it was a small county of chrome — sixty-foot rigs with living quarters you could've raised a family in, awnings out, hanging baskets, a bloke in a polo shirt power-washing a ramp that was already cleaner than anything I owned. Tatefield's old box drank diesel like Curtis's radio drank batteries and we nosed her in at the far end with the wing mirror still on its gaffer tape, between an Irish dealer's outfit and something Dutch with a logo on the side, and I got down into the smell of cut grass and Pimm's and someone else's good fortune and thought: *right. So this is where it's been all along.*

"Don't gawp," said Glenys, who'd come down for it because Caro said the horse mattered now and Glenys was the only one who could hold him on the ground in a crowd. She had her good tabard on, which is to say a tabard. "They can smell gawping."

"I'm not gawping."

"You're gawping with your whole back, love. Get the boy off."

*

We'd come up the grades fast — too fast, a horseman would've said, and I half was one and said it to myself and did it anyway, because the letter had a date on it and the grades were money. Saracen had gone from the 1.20 at Hartpury in April to a 1.30 in Yorkshire to a regional win and now here he was entered in something with a 1 and a 3 and a 5 after it, a height the colour of which I'd only ever watched on a phone. Caro had walked the schedule with a pencil and put him in the way you put a young horse in over its head when you can't afford to wait — *he's ready, he's not ready, he's ready enough and we need him seen.* Seen was the word now. Everything was about being seen.

I want to say what he looked like that morning, because it matters to the rest of it.

He'd come into his summer coat at last. Under the winter brown he'd been the colour of old furniture; now he'd come up the dark gloss of a wet conker, near-black until the sun got into him and found the red, and over his quarters, where the good feed had done its work, the dapples had come — those soft rings of light a horse only carries when he's well, when something's gone right in him. He had three black feet and one with a little white ring round the top of the hoof, the coronet on the off-hind, a band of light he wore like he didn't know it was there. The star sat off-true on his face, dragged left, like a thumb had smudged it. And on the near shoulder, where the coat went hairless and pale and slick, the scar nobody could name, the one page of him I couldn't read.

He looked, standing tied long and loose to the side of the box where he could see out and never feel shut in, like a great deal of money.

He didn't know that. That was the thing about him. The whole car park outside the ring was full of bodies that knew exactly what they were worth — knew it in the set of their shoulders, the way they let a groom take the horse, the way they didn't hurry. He stood in the middle of it not knowing there was a number anywhere in him, blowing at the strange grass, ears working, one eye too white in the corner because everything here was new and a new thing was a thing that might trap him. I went up and put my hand flat on his neck under the mane and felt the breath go through him and come back slower.

"It's a big garden," I told him. "That's all it is. Big garden, lot of people, nobody's going to do anything to you."

He dropped his poll a degree. The near ear came back to me. I scratched the place under the mane he liked and didn't think, for about a minute, about the letter or the number or any of it. There's no measuring that minute. I'm not going to try.

*

The first thing the money taught me was that it didn't shout.

I'd thought it would. I'd come from a place where the people with a bit of it made sure you knew — gold on the wrist, the right trainers, the loud car. Here the loudest people were the ones from where I was from: a dealer's lad in a sponsor's gilet shouting odds about a horse, a county mum in a Barbour that still had the creases telling everyone what the lessons cost. The actual money was quiet. It wore old kit, properly old, the boots cracked from twenty seasons and not from poverty, the wax coat gone the colour of a hedge. It didn't queue. It didn't carry its own anything. It didn't look at the prices on the trade stands because there weren't any — you asked, and the asking was the price. It stood at the collecting-ring rail with a coffee somebody had fetched it and watched the horses go by the way Caro watched me eat that first night — not at the faces, at the whole animal, the stand of it, down to the feet — and I realised, watching them watch, that I'd been reading horses my whole life and so had they, only they did it with a chequebook in the inside pocket, and that gap was the whole gap between me and this place, and it was about the size of the castle.

"That one's bought four horses this season," Glenys said in my ear, nodding at a small grey-haired woman in a anorak that had seen better decades. "Spent more than this yard's worth on the last one. Look at her. Looks like she's come to read the meter."

"How do you know what she spent?"

"Because the man she bought it off told the man who tells me everything. There's nothing private here, love, only quiet." She took the roll-up from behind her ear, looked at it, put it back. "Quiet's not the same as private. You'll learn the difference if you stop here long enough, which I hope to God you don't."

*

Tomas Berg was warming up when we got to the collecting ring, and I knew who he was before anyone said, the way you know which dog in the park is the one to watch.

He was tall and going grey at the temples in a way that looked deliberate, machine-fit, no spare anything, sitting a big bay so quiet you'd have thought the horse was asleep except the horse was jumping a metre forty practice oxer off a stride that came up under it like the ground had been built to measure. Everything about him was the right weight. The right boots, broken in over years and not by a groom. The right gloves. A martingale set so exactly you knew he'd checked it himself. He didn't speak to the people raising the practice fence except to say what he wanted in two words, and they did it, and there was no hierarchy fight about it, no waiting, because Tomas Berg got the fence when he wanted the fence and everybody including the fence knew it.

I watched him put the bay over the oxer three times and then walk it off on a loose rein, done, no fuss, no last greedy jump, the horse cool, the man cooler. And I felt a thing I didn't have a name for yet and have since decided was the closest I've ever come to looking at the road I didn't take.

Because he had it. The same thing I had. You could see it — the timing, the stillness, the horse believing him. He had the gift, only he'd had it *cleanly*, the way you'd have a tool, and you could tell by looking at him that he'd never once lain awake over what a clear round cost the thing that jumped it. He looked very free. He looked, if I'm honest, like the happiest man at Windsor.

He saw me looking. He nodded — not down, level, one rider to another, which was more than the well-bred had managed all morning, and he came over on the bay and stopped and looked at Saracen, properly, the whole of him.

"Holsteiner cross," he said. Not a question.

"That's him."

"He is frightened of the right rein." He said it flat, no cruelty in it, no kindness either, the way you'd read a temperature off a thermometer. "I watched him in the warm-up at the regional. He naps left. You ride him very" — he searched for the English — "patient. Most would have him in draw reins. You don't." He looked at my hands, the bare reins, no gadget on them. "Good. The draw reins fix it for a month and break it for life." Then he looked back at the horse and was quiet a second, and I'll give him this, the next thing he said had the only warmth in it I heard from him all day. "He has a lot of horse in him. Be careful what they make you do with it."

And he turned the bay and went, and didn't ask my name, and I didn't get his, and I stood there with the most useful thing anyone had said to me about my own horse ringing in my ears, given to me free by a man who felt nothing, while the people who loved me said *seen* and meant *sold*.

*

I walked the course on foot in my grey half-chaps among a hundred pairs of boots that cost what I'd earn in a year if I earned, and I counted strides in big deliberate paces and read the light and the going and didn't let one inch of my face show that I was frightened.

It was a proper track. A course-builder who'd built clever, not just big — a line off the turn that walked a forward five and would ride a quiet six if you let the horse think too long; a square oxer over a water tray, a liverpool, where a green horse would spook *down* at the blue and leave the back rail; a wall near the end, light blocks stacked like the side of a house, which was the worst kind of thing in the world for Saracen because it was a thing he couldn't see past, and a wall he can't see past is a wall that might be a trap, and a trap is the thing he'd been built by somebody's hands to be most afraid of.

I walked the wall twice. I stood in front of it where he'd meet it and looked at what he'd see, which was nothing, which was the problem. Then I went and studded him up.

I did it by the box, off the lid of the tin, on the going I'd felt walking it — not soft, Windsor in May, but a grab to it under the firm top, enough that he'd want grip to turn and hold and launch off. Big enough studs to bite, off-hind first, because that's the foot most horses snatch and his off-hind was the white one and the one I always did first like a small private superstition I'd stopped questioning. Screwing the grip in. Giving him the ground so he could hold it. I'd said to myself once, kneeling by his leg in the Hereford dark before Hartpury, that it was only care, and I'd wanted that down on the record before anything else got onto it, and I'll keep my word to my younger self: that night it had been only care. This wasn't that night. I knelt at Windsor in the smell of cut grass and screwed the grip into a body so it could hold the ground I was about to ask it to launch off in front of people who'd come to read its price, and somewhere in the turning of the spanner I knew there was a word for that other than care, and I did the other thing I'd got good at lately, which was not think it.

*

The bell went and I crossed the start line and the clock came on, and for the next ninety seconds there was no letter and no number and no castle and no Tomas Berg, there was only the body under me and the questions it asked.

He asked the first one in the collecting-ring chute before I'd even started — the crowd three-deep along the rope, a wing he couldn't see past, his worst place — and went the way he goes, still, the blink stopping, the small fast tic of muscle under the right eye, the last second he's still asking, *do I do this on my own or are you here.* And I gave him the right rein, soft, gave it away before he could fear it, turned his face off the crowd to the open and waited the half-stride longer than his fear wanted, the half-stride only I ever get, and he blew out, that long low flutter down his nose, and the near ear came back to me and the off ear went forward on the world, and we went in.

I'm not going to be clever about the round. He was huge. He met the forward five off the turn and I sat up and let him have the quiet six and he waited for me, he *waited*, this horse who'd been taught his whole life that waiting got him hurt, and came off a stride that was mine to give him. At the liverpool he saw the blue and dropped a wing of an ear at it and I closed my leg and said *not that, that's just water, I've got you* with my hands and my seat and the whole of me, and he believed me, the same as he believed me when I told him the right rein wouldn't hurt, and he snapped his front legs up neat to his chin the careful apologetic way he does, like a horse who's been hit for touching poles and decided never again, and left it up by a foot it didn't need.

And then the wall. I turned him to it knowing he couldn't see past it and feeling the whole of him ask the question one more time, *that, I can't see past that, are you here,* and I gave him the half-stride and rode him at the thing he was most afraid of in all the world on nothing but the lie I'd spent three months teaching him, which was that his fear could be borne if he wasn't alone with it. And he jumped it. He spent the trust I'd built and went up over a wall he couldn't see the far side of for no reason on God's earth except that I'd asked him to, and we landed clean on the other side of the thing he was most afraid of, and the bell — there was no bell, there was nothing, just the last fence and the line and the clock stopping.

Clear.

Clear round at Windsor, 1.35, on a condemned horse, in front of all of it.

He came back to a walk blowing, sweat-dark down the neck, the dapples gone with the wet, his sides going like a bellows, and the near ear stayed home on me, tipped back, listening, the off ear out on the world, the split, the proof. And I leaned down on his neck where the crowd couldn't read my face and I said into him, plain, the only true thing: "Good. You're so good. I've got you."

We placed third in the jump-off. I didn't ride him fast. I never rode him fast for a place. I told myself it was the horse and it was, partly, but it was also that fast meant risking him and risking him meant maybe a fall and a fall was — well. He was worth too much to fall now. There it was again. The thought I kept not finishing, finishing itself in my hands on the reins, telling them to be careful with the asset. I rode a clean steady double clear and let Berg's empty bay turn inside me and win it and I was glad to be third because third kept him whole, and I hated that I was glad for that reason, and I patted the wet neck and we walked out third while the loudspeaker said my name wrong.

*

The man came to the stable in the long gold light of the evening, when the crowds had thinned and the trade stands were packing up and the lorry park had started its slow smell of horses being washed off and fed.

I clocked him from a way off the way I'd learned to clock the quiet money — by what he didn't do. He didn't hurry. He came down the line of boxes alone, no entourage, in clothes that were the most expensive thing I'd seen all day precisely because you couldn't have told what they cost, soft and dark and exactly right, a closely kept beard, a stillness on him like a man who has never once had to wait for anything and so has stopped being in any kind of hurry about anything at all. He stopped two boxes up to look at a horse that wasn't ours, and the way he stood to look at it told me the last thing: he could ride. You can see it in the hips, in how a person stands near a horse, whether the body knows or whether it's only ever watched. His body knew.

He came to Saracen's box and stopped, and didn't reach in, didn't make the *kiss-kiss* noise the amateurs make, didn't do one single thing to a frightened horse that a frightened horse would have to brace against. He just stood, hands easy at his sides, and looked.

I came round from the muck barrow with my sleeves wet to the elbow and my hands stinking of the day, and he turned and inclined his head, the smallest, most exact courtesy, the kind that costs you nothing if you're him and costs you your whole footing if you're me.

"Mr Okafor-Lyle," he said. He had my name right, both halves, the hyphen and all, which nobody at Windsor had managed including the loudspeaker. "Raef Al-Mazroui. Forgive me coming at your supper hour. I wanted to see the horse, and I find one sees a horse better in the evening, when no one is performing." His English was formal and unhurried and entirely without an accent you could have made a joke of, and I'd had a joke loaded, the gear I go to, and I put it down. "You rode him very well today."

"He jumped well." I wiped a hand on my fleece and didn't offer it, because it stank, and he understood that too, and didn't offer his, and somehow the not-shaking was its own small civility passed between us.

"He jumped well because you made it possible for him to jump well," Raef said. "That is not the same thing, and most people in that ring today do not know it is not the same thing." He looked back at the horse. Saracen had come to the door, ears working, that too-much-white in the corner of the eye, deciding about him, and Raef stood very still and let himself be decided about, which I have never once seen a buyer do, and which told me he was the most dangerous man I'd met all day. "How old?"

"Seven."

"Seven." A pause, the kind a horseman leaves. "Holsteiner. The sire I think I know." He named him. He was right. "Bought as a yearling on his looks and never made, yes? I saw three like him today wearing rugs that cost more than the horse, and they will none of them ever do what this one did at the wall." He said the next thing gently, almost to the horse and not to me. "He frightened himself at that wall. I watched. And he went anyway."

"He trusts me," I said, before I could weigh it.

"Yes," said Raef. "I know. I watched that too." And he smiled, and it was a real smile, warm, nothing false in it, and that was the worst of it, because he meant it, and it was a compliment, and it was also the most accurate thing anyone had said about me in months, and I couldn't tell, I still can't, whether he was admiring the horse or reading the price tag I'd hung round my own neck without knowing.

He stayed a minute more. He asked one or two things, easy, no offer in them, nothing you could've held him to — how the horse loaded, was he sound, who did his feet. Buyer's questions worn so smooth you'd swear they were just conversation. I answered them and heard myself answering them and somewhere under the answering a cold thing started up in me, quiet, the same cold that had started in my hands when I told them to be careful with the asset, and I knew the word for it the way I'd known the word by the studs and didn't think it.

Then he stepped back from the door, and looked at the horse one last time, the whole of him — and I watched his eyes go, in order, exactly where mine would have gone if I'd been the one buying: to the eye that held too much white, reading the fear in it; to the enormous shoulder, reading the scope; and then, last, the way mine always went, his eyes found the scar. The hand's-width of pale slick hairless skin on the near shoulder, the one page of him nobody could read. Raef's eyes paused on it half a second, the same half-second the dealer's had, the same half-second mine still did, and I knew he'd seen it and weighed it and put it where it went in the sum, and I wanted to step between him and it the way you'd cover a friend's record from a stranger.

He didn't ask about the scar. The ones who know don't ask. He just looked at it, and looked at the eye again, and then he looked at me, with that real warmth, the warmth that was the cruelty, and he said it.

"He's frightened," Raef said. "You've made him brave." He let that sit, the way Caro let things sit, only without the leg on, just true. "That's worth a great deal."

And he inclined his head again, that exact small courtesy, and turned and walked off up the line of boxes into the gold light, unhurried, a man who had all the time there was, and Saracen put his head over the door and watched him go with the near ear tipped back toward me and the off ear forward on the leaving stranger, one ear for me and one for the world, not knowing one single thing about what he'd just been told he was worth.

I heard a compliment. I stood there in the dusk with my sleeves wet and my heart full and I heard a man say I'd made my frightened horse brave, and I held it the way I held the gold-cracked cup, two-handed, glad.

*Worth a great deal.* He'd said it kindly, the way you'd tell a man his work was good. He'd read the eye and the shoulder and the scar and the boy in the wet fleece, and he'd priced the lot, accurately, to my face, with real warmth, which is the only way the honest ones ever do it. He'd come to Windsor and found the one thing in that whole field of money that money hadn't made, and he'd told me, kindly, what it cost.


---

# Chapter Sixteen — What He's Worth

He'd said it at Windsor, with his hand not on the horse but near it, the way you hold your hand near a fire you're deciding about — *he's frightened, you've made him brave, that's worth a great deal* — and then he'd walked off across the good grass in shoes that had never been near a yard, and I'd carried it home in the lorry like a thing I hadn't paid for and wasn't sure I was allowed. Ten days later his car came down the Tatefield drive, and I understood that a man like that doesn't say a thing like that and then leave it.

The car came slow over the ruts, which is how you know money, in the end — not the car, though the car was quiet and dark and cost more than the Hall, but the *slow*, the way it picked its way down the drive like it had all afternoon and the drive was the one being tested. Our drive. The potholes you learned by April or lost a wheel to. He came down it at walking pace with no one in the world telling him to hurry, and I stood in the yard with a barrow of soiled bedding and watched the whole arriving of it, and I thought, the way I think when I'm out of my depth and won't show it: *here's the appraiser, then. Come to read the rest of the manual.*

Caro was already on the gravel. She'd been watching the lane the way she'd watched it the day I came, which I knew now meant she'd been watching it a while.

*

His name was Raef Al-Mazroui and I'd had it off Caro the night before — *a serious man, Devlin, a serious buyer, you'll be charming and you'll let me do the talking* — and I'd nodded and not promised the second part. He got out of the car the way he did everything, without effort and without hurry, a man in his early forties with a close-kept beard and clothes that didn't announce themselves and a stillness on him I clocked before anything else, because stillness is the thing money buys that you can't fake. He shook Caro's hand and called her madam, which she liked, and then he looked past her at the yard — the tarp, the swelling doors, the dead bulbs, the silver-and-no-money of it all laid bare in the flat June light — and his face didn't do the thing visitors' faces do, the quick flinch and cover. He just took it in, all of it, gently, the way you'd take in a horse's old scars before you decided what you thought. He missed nothing and judged none of it out loud, and that was somehow worse than a flinch, because a flinch you can dislike a man for.

Then he looked at me, over the barrow, and inclined his head a degree, the way you'd greet someone you already knew the value of.

"The young man off the rogue," he said. "I watched you both at Windsor. I came to watch you again, if I may."

*If I may.* In my own yard, with my own barrow, the only person at Tatefield without a wage. He asked my permission like it was mine to give, and I knew it was a courtesy and not a fact, and I also knew it was the first time anyone in that whole world had pretended otherwise, and the pretending went into me somewhere I didn't want it to.

"He's not had his work," I said. "I'll bring him out."

*

What I'll say about Raef Al-Mazroui, before the rest of it, is that he could ride.

I didn't know it till he asked, very courteous, whether he might sit on one of the others — not Saracen, he was too good a horseman to ask that of a stranger's hands, he asked for Beau, the made one, the schoolmaster, the safe ask — and Caro said of course and I legged him up, and the second he took the reins I felt the yard go quiet in me the way it goes quiet when something true happens. Because he had it. Not the gift — I don't think you can buy the gift, I've come to believe you have to be damaged into it — but the craft, the real thing, the soft following hand and the still seat and the leg that says a thing once and means it. He took Beau round on the buckle first, just walking, letting the old horse find out about him, and Beau found out about him and dropped his head and blew, and I stood at the gate and thought, against my own interest, *that's lovely. That's a man who'd never break a mouth before lunch.* He picked the old grey up and put him over a couple of fences and the fences came up under Beau easy and kind, and Raef sat them like he was being carried and asked nothing he didn't need, and when he was done he loosened the reins and patted the grey neck twice, real pats, not the showman's slap, and brought him back to the gate.

"He's a good horse," Raef said, swinging down. "He's done his work and he's tired and somebody loved him properly once." He ran his hand down the old foreleg without being asked, found the heat that's always in a twelve-year-old's knees, straightened up. "You'll sell him before the others. He's the one with a price and no fight left in him." He said it without any cruelty in it at all, which is how I learned that the worst things in that world get said kindly, by men with soft hands, who are right.

Caro's face didn't move. "Beau's not the conversation," she said, smooth.

"No," said Raef. "He isn't." And he looked across the yard to where Glenys was bringing Saracen out, and the conversation, the real one, walked toward us on four black legs with a hand's-width of pale scar on the near shoulder and an eye that held too much white, and the whole afternoon, I understood, had only ever been about him.

*

I rode Saracen for them in the outdoor school.

I'd like to tell you I didn't try. I'd like to tell you I just let the horse be a horse and showed the man nothing, kept something back, protected the one thing I had. But I'm done with the lying, so: I rode him as well as I have ever ridden anything. I gave him the warm-up he needed, the long low loosening, off the right rein, never asking the bad question till he'd told me the answer himself; and then I picked him up and put him over the course Caro had set, and he went — he went *with* me, the near ear tipped back the whole way and the off ear out on the world, the split, the proof, and at the oxer where the light came under it wrong he felt the right rein go live and asked me, *is the bad thing here too*, and I gave it to him soft, all of it, *not yet, not while I'm here*, and he believed me, and he stood off it and snapped his front legs neat to his chin and was careful, so careful, careful the way only a horse who's been hurt is careful, and we landed and he blew out long and low and I had to not smile, because there was a man at the rail with a chequebook and a soft following hand, watching the exact thing that made the horse worth selling, and I had just done it for him on purpose.

When I came back to the gate Raef wasn't looking at the horse.

He was looking at me. He'd watched the horse jump and then he'd watched *me*, the way I'd watched him on Beau, reading the craft and not the result, and his stillness had a thing in it now that hadn't been there before, a focus, and I knew the look because Caro had given me a version of it on the gravel in March, and the dealer had given me a colder version of it in the lorry park, and I'd hated it both times because it was the look that prices you. Except Raef's look didn't price the horse.

It priced me.

"Now I have seen it twice," he said. "Good. The first time you wonder if it was the day. The second time you know."

*

Caro took him in for tea. The good cups, the gold-cracked one for him, the cold grand kitchen warmed by nothing but the range — she did the whole performance of Tatefield, the faded silver of it, the *we live in the kitchen as though it were a choice*, and Raef received it with perfect manners and was not for one second fooled, and they talked around the horse for half an hour, the price-dance, the *early days* and the *we'll see how Hickstead goes* and all the soft language people use to not say a number across a table. I stood at the range with my hands round a mug because Glenys had told me to be near and quiet, and I read the two of them the way I read a horse, by where the tension lived, and the tension didn't live in the price. They both knew the price. The price was a settled thing they were being polite about. The tension lived somewhere else, and I couldn't find it, till Raef found it for me.

He set the gold cup down — gentle, the way you'd put down a thing you knew was cracked — and he looked at Caro and said, "May I speak to the boy alone, madam. Five minutes. I have a question that isn't about the horse."

And Caro's thumbnail went to the seam of her glove.

I'd not seen her do it before. She did it the way Marnie bites her thumb, the way Pip apologises — a tell that gets in under the manner before the manner can stop it. Her face stayed exactly where it was, warm and dry and certain. But the thumbnail worked at the worn seam of her glove, once, twice, and I read it, and I thought, *she's frightened*, and I didn't understand of what.

"Of course," she said. "Devlin. Walk Mr Al-Mazroui to his car."

*

We walked out into the yard and the light had gone long and gold the way it does on a June evening when the rain's finally given up, and Raef put his hands in his pockets and looked at the boxes and didn't go to his car. He'd no intention of going to his car. He stood in the middle of the yard I'd mucked out that morning and he said, without preamble, courteous and exact:

"How much are they paying you?"

I said nothing. He nodded, like the nothing was the answer.

"I'll tell you what I think," he said, "and you may correct me. I think you are not paid. You have board, and your food at that table, and coaching that is — I will grant her — genuinely excellent, and a promise. A horse, *eventually*, when you have proved yourself, on her word, with nothing written."

His eyes went to Saracen's box and came back, unhurried.

"I think you made that horse, with your hands, over months, out of a thing that was worth less than nothing. The value of him now is, in a real and measurable way, *you*. And you will not see a penny of it when he sells, because there is no paper anywhere that says you should." He wasn't cruel. That was the unbearable part of him. He laid it out the way you'd lay out a horse's bad leg, gently, so the owner could see the truth and not be shamed by it. "I have watched a great many yards run on a great many boys exactly like you. I am usually wrong about the boy. I do not think I am wrong about you."

"You don't know me," I said. It came out Sheffield, flat, the gear I go to.

"No," he said. "But I know what you're worth. Which is more than anyone here has told you." And he let that sit in the gold light, and I felt it land the way the dealer's number had landed, except the dealer had priced the horse and this man was pricing *me*, and the awful thing, the thing I've never been able to put down, is that it was the kindest thing anyone had said to me in that valley. Because it was true and he'd done the sum and he wasn't ashamed of me for the answer.

*

Then he made the offer.

He made it the way he did everything, without hurry, looking at me and not at the horse, and what he offered was the whole of the thing I had not let myself want.

A job. A real one. Wages — he said the figure, and it was a year of my mother's money in a month, and I kept my face still the way Caro had kept hers and I don't know if I managed it. A contract, written, *because that is how I do things, you understand, paper is not an insult, paper is a respect, it says I will not be able to change my mind about you on a Tuesday and call it the world.* A place in his programme — he was building a string in the Gulf, serious horses, the best, and he wanted them ridden by the best, and he had decided, watching me twice, that I was somewhere in the best, or would be, and that the smart move was to have me before the rest of the world worked it out. He'd fly me out to see it. He'd put me up. He'd train me with people I'd only read about. He would, he said, and here he slowed right down so I'd hear it, *keep* me — that was the word he used, *keep*, as casual as anything, not knowing it was the word the whole of me had been hungry for since I was nine years old — keep me as a professional whose work had a value he would pay, for as long as the work was good, and treat me, in the meantime, like a man and not a debt.

And I want to be honest about what was wrong with it, because nothing was wrong with it, and that was the terror of it.

There was no leg in it. That's how I knew. Every offer I'd had at Tatefield came with a leg on — the praise that found the soft place, the *only person alive who can ride this horse*, the *we don't insult each other with paper*, the warmth applied exactly where I was hungry so that I'd give more than I'd meant to. Raef put no leg on me at all. He laid the thing out flat, all of it, the number and the contract and the *keep*, and then he stopped, and waited, and let me look at it with my own eyes, and asked nothing. He didn't need to find my hungry place. He'd costed it correctly and he was simply offering the right price for it, which is the one thing in that whole world nobody else would do.

He saw the gift for exactly what it was. That's the thing I keep coming back to. The dealer saw a horse worth six figures. Caro saw the only hands that made the horse worth it and loved me and used me in the same unsorted breath. But Raef saw the *gift*, the thing itself, the closing of the gap between a frightened body and a man's hands so the body would override its own fear — he saw that I could do it and what it was and what it was worth on an open market, and he wanted to buy it clean, with paper, at the price, like a grown man buying a grown man's labour. No mystery. No *your sort always have a feel for them*. No *eventually*. He didn't think the gift was magic and he didn't think it was love. He thought it was a skill, an expensive one, and he was right, and being seen that clearly by a man who'd then *pay* for it should have felt like rescue.

It felt like rescue. I'm not going to pretend it didn't. For about four seconds, standing in the gold light with the swallows working the muck heap behind me, I felt the door come open that I'd never let myself walk to, and the wages and the paper and the *keep* all standing on the other side of it like a life, and I thought, plain, *I could go. I could just go.*

*

And then I didn't.

I'd like to give you a reason that sounds like a reason. The truth doesn't sound like one. The truth is that to take Raef's contract I'd have had to pick up, with my own hands, the one fact I could not survive picking up: that the handshake had never been love. That when Caro held her dry hard hand out across her dead husband's table and said *we do our word*, and I'd gone glad all through and taken it, I'd taken a deal and called it being wanted. To sign Raef's paper I'd have had to agree with it. I'd have had to look back at the stew pushed at me, *eat, you're skin and bone*, and the cup mended with gold, and the *Devlin* that made me nine, and call the whole of it a transaction. And I couldn't. I'd rather have stayed and been bled white than learn that the only love I'd ever walked into had a balance sheet under it.

So I read my own hungry place — the *keep*, the wages, the door — and I rode myself past it the way I rode the rogues, soft, *not yet*, and gave it a kinder name.

"I'm loyal," I said. "She took me in when nobody else would. I'm not for sale either."

Raef looked at me for a long moment, and there was no triumph in it and no disappointment in it, only a kind of patience that I've thought about every year since, because it was the patience of a man who has all the time and all the money and knows exactly how a thing like this ends.

"Loyalty," he said, gently, "is what they call it when they want you to work for free and feel honoured. I'm not insulting you. I admire it. It's cost more boys than you their whole lives." He took out his car keys, finally, and turned them once in his fingers. The hands were easy. Everything in him was easy; that was the thing I couldn't push against. "It is the thing you feel toward the person who has convinced you that being needed is the same as being loved. They are not the same. Yours is the rarest gift I have seen in a paddock or out of one. You should be paid for it. You will not be, here. And when she sells the horse" — he said it as a settled thing, the way he'd said Beau, the way he said everything that was true — "they'll do it so kindly you'll thank them for it. I'd rather you came to me before that than after. But you'll come after. They almost always come after."

*

He left the door open. That was the last of it, and it was the cleverest thing, except it wasn't cleverness, it was just money being patient. He didn't push. He didn't take the offer off the table. He got into his quiet dark car and he said, through the open window, with the same courtesy he'd come down the drive with:

"The offer doesn't expire. I want the horse and I'll have the horse — you know that, I think, better than she does — but I want the rider more, and the rider I'm willing to wait for." He paused. "Think about who, in all of this, has told you the truth. That's all. Think about who told you the truth and what it cost them to keep it from you."

And he took the car back up the drive at walking pace, picking out the potholes, in no hurry at all, and I stood in the yard he'd just shown me the whole shape of, with the gold gone grey now and the swallows gone in, and I told myself I'd done a true thing, a loyal thing, the right thing, and I almost believed it, the way I almost believed everything that season, which is to say I believed it the way you believe a horse believes you — completely, and only because nobody's shown him the rest of the manual yet.

Glenys was at Saracen's door. She'd brought him in while we talked, and she'd heard none of it and all of it, the way she heard everything, and she stood with her square back to me and her hand flat on the horse's frightened cheek, the off side, the bad side, and he let her. She didn't turn round when the car's tail-lights went over the cattle grid and out. She just kept her hand on the bad cheek a moment longer, the way you'd keep a hand on a thing you couldn't keep, and then she dropped it and picked up the empty feed bucket off the cobbles.

She didn't say the line. The line was still weeks off — saved, hoarded, the one true sentence she'd allow herself, because she'd watched too many boys to spend it early. She gave me the worn thing instead, the thing she said in place of the thing she meant, the pity from the first day folded into it.

"Mind yourself, love."

And she went up the yard to start the feeds, and I stood there in the dark by the box I'd been told the first night was not mine to open, with a horse inside it who turned his head to the sound of me and tipped his near ear back, listening, trusting me completely, not knowing one single thing about the offer I'd just turned down on his account and mine, or what a man with a chequebook had just told me was true.


---

# Chapter Seventeen — No Paper

Glenys watched the car go all the way to the cattle grid before she moved, and then she took the roll-up from behind her ear and held it, and didn't light it, and I stood there next to her in the yard Raef Al-Mazroui's tyres had just printed and unprinted, and I told myself the word I'd been telling myself all afternoon, which was *loyal.* I was being loyal. I'd turned down a wage and a contract and a roof in the Gulf with my name on the lease, and I'd done it for the people who took me in, and that was loyalty, and loyalty was a thing you could be proud of, and I held the word the way you hold a stud you've dropped in the straw — fingers closing on it before you've properly found it, just glad it isn't lost.

Glenys put the roll-up back. "You said no, then," she said.

"I said no."

"Aye." She looked at the gate the car had gone through, not at me. "Daft," she said, but soft, the way she said most of her hard things, so you couldn't be sure she meant you to keep it. And she went up the yard to the feed room, and that was all she'd let herself say, and I let it lie, because I had decided, somewhere in the last hour, that today was going to be the day I sorted the other thing. The thing under all of it. The thing I'd been not-asking since March, since a hand came across a scrubbed table and I took it before she could change her mind.

If Raef could put it on paper, I could ask Caro to.

That was the logic, and I want it written down plainly because it's the most hopeful thing I did all season and the most stupid, and I can't have one without the other. A man had offered me a contract that morning. It had made the empty space where my own contract should have been suddenly the loudest thing in the valley, louder than the tarp. I had thought, walking the yard after his car left, *I'll just ask her. I'll ask her when. That's all. When does the horse become mine. Where's it written. What does the word mean.* I had thought it would be a small conversation between people who trusted each other, which is what the handshake was supposed to make us. I went and found her in the kitchen, where we lived, the warmest room, and I asked.

*

She was at the long table with the everyday mugs and a heel of bread and the brown window-envelopes stacked under the salt, and she was doing the thing she did at that hour, which was looking at the post without opening it, the way you look at weather you can't change. When I came in she moved the envelopes off the table and into the chair beside her, casual, the way you'd move your feet for someone, and that was the first thing she did, before either of us had said a word — she put the money out of the room.

"You'll have a tea," she said. Not a question. She was already up.

"I wanted to ask you something."

"Ask it over a tea. Everything goes down better over a tea, your Glenys would tell you, if your Glenys ever made one for anybody but a horse." She filled the kettle. Her back was to me, the ramrod back, the white hair scraped to the bone, and I had the strangest feeling, standing in that cold kitchen with my question in my mouth, that I'd lost the round before the bell, that she'd read the whole of it off me the second I came through the door — the car, the offer, the no, the thing the no had loosened in me — and was already three strides ahead, walking the line, counting it out in those big deliberate steps. *He's coming to ask about the horse. There's a half-stride in it. I'll fit one in.*

I'd seen her do it to a horse. I want that understood before I tell you the rest. I'd watched her, that very week, stand at Saracen's off side and hold the weight of the right rein without pulling it, light, the way you hold a door, until the horse turned his eye to her and asked his question and she answered it before he had to bear it — *not yet* — and the horse softened. I knew the move. It was my move. It was the only thing I was good at in the world. And I stood in her kitchen and watched her warm the pot and I knew, the way you know a fence is going to come up wrong three strides out and there's nothing in your body quick enough to fix it, that she was about to do it to me.

She did it to me.

"Raef Al-Mazroui's a serious man," she said, setting the mug down in front of me, sitting, folding her ruined hands around her own. "I'm glad you met him. It does you good to be looked at by a man like that. He looks at a thing properly. Not many do." She let that sit. Caro never rushed. She had the timing of someone who'd jumped against the clock at the top of the world and learned that the clock isn't the enemy, the panic is. "He'll have offered you something."

"Wages," I said. "A contract."

"Of course he did." Her face didn't move. "He'd be a fool not to. You're the best young horseman I've seen since I stopped being able to call myself that without people being kind to me about it, and he saw it in an afternoon, which is to his credit, and he'd take you off me tomorrow if you let him, which is also to his credit, because that's what serious people do, they see a thing's worth and they go and get it." She turned the mug a quarter-turn on the wood. "And you said no."

"How do you—"

"Because you're sitting in my kitchen with a face like a boy who's done a brave thing and wants someone to tell him it was brave." She said it without one ounce of cruelty in it, which was worse than cruelty, because it was true, and we both heard that it was true, and she let me sit in it a second before she gave me the thing I'd come up the yard for, the warm thing, laid on the place that was hungry for it. "It was brave," she said. "And it was loyal. And I'll not pretend I'm not glad, because I'd not have known how to keep this place upright if you'd gone, and that's the truth, and you'd a right to go, and you stayed. I know what that cost you. I do know."

I'd come to ask about paper. I want you to see how far we'd already drifted from the paper.

*

"I wanted to ask," I said — and I made myself say it, I'd come to say it — "about the horse. About when. You said eventually there'd be a horse. You've said it since March. And I've not asked, because asking felt like — I don't know. Like I didn't trust you." I looked at the mug. "And now there's a man wants to write me a contract and I find I don't even know what I've got here. On paper. Whether the thing you said in March is a thing I could — " I ran out. The Sheffield in me, that'll say *and the wage?* across a table on the first day, deserted me clean. "Whether Saracen's the horse you meant," I said, low. "And whether it's written anywhere that he is."

She let me get all of it out. That was part of it, the letting. She didn't interrupt and she didn't flinch and she didn't cool, the same as the first day when I'd asked about the wage, and when I'd finished she reached across the table and put one of her ruined hands flat over the back of mine, light, the weight of it and no more, the way she'd held the right rein, and she left it there.

"Devlin," she said.

She'd called me that on the gravel in March, the name only my mother used, the name that put me nine years old. She'd not used it much since. She used it now, and it did the thing it was built to do, it went in under the ribs before I could vote, and I understood, even as it worked, that she'd kept it in her pocket for exactly this, the way I kept the half-stride for the worst fence, and the understanding didn't stop it working. Nothing stopped it working. That's the part I can't get anyone to believe who hasn't had it done to them. Knowing the move is being made doesn't unmake it. I have watched a man see my hands going soft on a frightened horse and say *that's it, that's the trick, I see what you're doing* — and the horse softens anyway, because the horse isn't in on the conversation, the horse is just a body being told the bad thing isn't coming. I was a body being told the bad thing wasn't coming. I knew it. I softened anyway.

"We don't put a thing like that on paper," she said. "Not here. Not Hugo's people, not me, not ever. And I'll tell you why, and it's not the reason you're braced for." Her thumb moved once across the back of my hand, slow, along the bones. "Paper's for people who've already decided to disappoint each other. You write it down so that when the love runs out there's a thing left to hold the other one to. That's all a contract is, when you scrape the gilt off it — it's two people agreeing in advance about how they're going to fail. And I'll not insult you by writing one. I'll not insult *us.* You've my word. You've always had my word. When you've shown the world what I already know — and you're close, you're so close, you've no idea how close — there'll be a horse, and you'll know it's yours the way you know it now, in your hands, where the things that matter actually live, and not on a sheet a solicitor in Hereford keeps in a drawer." She took her hand back, gently, the contact returned to the buckle before I had to ask whether it was leaving. "You came here for a life. Not a job. A man like Raef offers a job. I offer the other thing. You knew that this morning, when you said no to him. You knew which one was worth more. Don't let one afternoon of being flattered make you forget what you already know."

And there it was. She'd taken the no I'd said to Raef — the brave loyal stupid no — and she'd made it the proof I didn't need the paper. *You said no to the contract because you already trusted the word. Now you want the word in writing? Then you didn't trust it. Choose.* She'd built the line so that asking for paper meant calling her a liar, and there was no fence in it I could see, only the long soft turn she'd ridden me into, and at the end of it the answer to my question was that the question was an insult, and I was the one who'd be ashamed for having asked.

I left the kitchen with no paper and with my chest warm and full and a thing in it that I knew, even then, walking out into the yard, was not the same as having an answer. I'd been ridden. The leg was very good. I'd say it had been the best of her if I hadn't already given that to the indoor school, to the afternoon with her hand on his scar. She had two bests in her, the loving one and the one that took, and the terrible thing was you couldn't tell them apart from inside, and you couldn't tell them apart from outside either, and I have come to think she couldn't tell them apart herself, which is either the kindest thing I can say about her or the worst, and I've never been able to decide which, the same as everything else.

*

Glenys was in the feed room when I came past, soaking sugar beet, the steam off the bucket and the molasses-and-mouse smell of the place, the strip light buzzing, the yellowed scoops on their nails. She didn't look up.

"You ask her, then," she said.

"How does everyone in this valley know what I'm about to do before I do it."

"'Cause it's the same thing, love. It's always the same thing. They've all asked, in the end. Every lad and lass that's come through that caravan, sooner or later they get a face on them like you've got on you now and they go up to that kitchen to ask whose the horse is, or where's the money, or what's it all been *for* — and they come back down" — she stirred the beet, slow — "with a warm chest and no paper and the certain feeling they've been the daft one for asking. And then they muck out. Same as you're about to."

I stood in the doorway. Outside, Saracen's head was over his door, the near-black of him gone wet-conker now in the May light, one ear on me, the other on the swallows working the muck heap. He'd learned to put his head over the door. In March there'd been a bolt on it and a horse behind it that struck the wall all night, and now there was this, a horse looking out at the weather, and I'd done that, with my hands, a second at a time, never spending more than I'd put in, and I was proud of it, and I want that down too, because the pride is part of the trap, the pride is the bait in the trap.

"Glenys," I said. "What is it you're not saying."

She stopped stirring.

I'd known her two months.
