I'd learned that she said one true thing and let it lie and never said it twice, that her love came in single sentences with long gaps after them, that she'd watched the place sell off everything she helped build and stayed and would stay, and that the staying was the truest thing about her and the most damning. She talked to the horses tenderer than to anyone. She'd given me *mind yourself, love,* worn down to nothing through over-use, the way you wear a warning down to a noise. But I had never once asked her the straight thing and held her eye while I did it, and I did it now, in the feed-room door, with the kettle for the bran going behind her and the beet steaming and a horse looking out at swallows, and she set the spoon down on the bench, and she gave it to me. The whole of it. Once.

"They'll have the horse off you, love," she said. "And they'll have you and all. And they'll do it kind, so you'll thank them."

The strip light buzzed. The beet steamed. I didn't say anything.

"That's the trick of this place, and it's not Caro's trick, she didn't invent it, it's older than her, it's older than the Hall." Her voice didn't rise. That was the worst of it, that she said the most terrible thing anyone said to me all season in the flat tone you'd use to tell a child the tap freezes. "The cruel ones, you can leave. The cruel ones do you a mercy, they make it easy. It's the kind ones that finish you. They feed you and teach you and call you family and cry real tears the day they sell what you made, and you'll stand there holding the headcollar while the new owner's man backs the lorry up, and you'll have helped, because they'll have made it so the helping felt like love. And you'll go home that night and think, *well, they were good to me.* And they were. That's not a lie. That's the trap. Both at once. You'll never get them to be the villain so you can hate them clean and go." She turned the spoon in the beet. "I'm in it myself, mind. Stood here soaking his feed, fattening the thing they'll sell, and I'll lead it to the box at the end and cry over it and soak the next one's in the morning. So don't take it off me as if I'm clean of it. I'm telling you because I'm in it. So that when it happens — and it's already happening, love, it happened the day a dealer said a number in a car park — you'll at least have heard it said out loud the once, by somebody, and you can't tell yourself afterward that nobody told you."

She picked the spoon back up.

"There," she said. "Now you've heard it. I'll not say it again. Go and muck out, the swelling door's dropped again and I've put a bale under it." And she handed me the fork, the actual fork, off the nail by the door, into my actual hand, and turned back to the beet, and that was the warning over, and the work starting in the same motion, and I understood that she'd meant me to feel that too — that the hand that gave me the truth was the same hand that gave me the fork, that there was no clean place to stand, not even beside the one person who'd told me the truth, because the one person who'd told me the truth was holding the bucket of the thing being fattened for sale, and so was I.

*

I mucked out. The swelling door had dropped on its hinge and I shouldered it and Glenys's bale held it and I did Saracen's box around him while he stood loose and long, the way he stood now, the way he hadn't been able to stand in March, and I forked the dirty straw and barrowed it to the heap and laid the clean back down and banked it up the walls high the way he liked it, walls of it, so that even in the dark even with a door at his back he'd feel held and not cornered, which is a thing you can do for a frightened horse, you can build him walls that comfort instead of trap, it's all in whether he can see the way out, it's all in whether you leave him a door — and I did this work that I loved with Glenys's sentence going round in me like a stone in a boot, *they'll do it kind, so you'll thank them,* and I did not stop and pack my holdall and walk the mile uphill to the crossroads and wait for the bus.

I want to be honest about why, because it's the centre of the whole thing and I've dressed it up enough ways over ten years to know which is the true one.

It wasn't that I didn't believe her. I believed her completely. She was the only one who'd never once lied to me and she'd just told me the exact shape of what was going to happen and I knew, the way I know a horse is about to stop, that she was right. And it wasn't even the loan-maths, though that was in it — I knew the only thing in the yard worth enough to save the place was the horse I loved, and I'd done the sum, and I'd pushed it down. It wasn't the maths.

It was that leaving meant being a thing nobody needed. And I had spent my whole life learning that being needed was the only love that ever stayed. My mother loved me in bursts and the bursts came when I was useful, when I was holding the flat together, when I was the little adult who made the gas card last — and I'd built my entire self on the one solid fact I'd ever found, which was *be needed and maybe they'll keep you.* Glenys had offered me the truth and the door in the same breath, the same as she'd handed me the truth and the fork. I took the fork. I'd rather be used than unwanted. I knew that about myself, standing in clean straw with a stone in my boot, and I hated it, and I banked the walls up high and gave the horse his door and did not pack my bag.

I saw the whole con that May, in a feed-room doorway, more clearly than anyone. And I stayed. You can't muck the wound out with a fork.

*

I went up to Hob's field on the way to the caravan. I'd started doing that on the bad days, the days a number got said or a thing got named — standing at the top rail with the old useless cob and letting him not need anything off me.

He came over in the dusk. Forty if he was a day, dinner-plate feet gone unshod for years, sway-backed and grey round the muzzle, the son of a hunter Hugo'd ridden, bred at Tatefield and never sold and never going to be, kept for no reason on earth except that he was Hob and this was his field and nobody had the heart. He came over wanting nothing. That was his whole gift. There was nothing anyone needed off him and never would be, and so nobody would ever sell him, and so he was safe. The safest horse in the valley was the one worth nothing. And I stood there in the failing light wanting, more than the Grand Prix, more than my own name on a horse, more than the contract I'd turned down that morning, to be the horse nobody needed. I didn't say it. You don't say a thing like that out loud. You'd have to do something about it.

*

It was full dark when I came down, and I went to Saracen's box, the way I went every night now, last thing, before the caravan. He was lying down. He'd only just started lying down at night, in May, the surest sign there is that a horse has stopped keeping watch, that he's decided the dark is safe enough to be defenceless in — a prey animal lies flat out only where it trusts the world won't come for it while it's down. I crouched at the door and he didn't get up, which two months ago would have been unthinkable, a man crouched at his door in the dark and him not surging to his feet. He just turned his head, and there was the eye with its corner of white catching the yard light, and there was the breath, slow, and the near ear came round and tipped back to me, listening, the off ear loose, and I crouched in the cold straw-smelling dark with Glenys's sentence still in me and I said the thing I had no right to say and knew I had no right to say even as it left me.

"I won't let them," I told him.

He blew out, long and low, the sound he makes when he's decided to trust the next ten seconds, and put his big head flat back down in the bank of straw I'd built him high so he'd never feel cornered, and shut his eyes, and trusted me, completely, the way only he did, on the strength of a promise that was not mine to make, in a yard where the man who'd lead him to the lorry had already soaked his beet, in a season with a date on it, to a boy who that very afternoon had been ridden past his own question and hadn't even managed to keep the paper, never mind the horse.

I never did get to keep that promise. I keep it now, though, in the only way that's left, which is to say it out loud to whoever'll listen, the once, the way Glenys said hers — that I told a sleeping horse I wouldn't let them, and meant it, and was already too late, and that the ear stayed tipped back to me in the dark the whole time I was lying to us both.


---

# Chapter Eighteen — Hickstead

Three weeks after I made a sleeping horse a promise I had no right to make, I loaded him onto the box at four in the morning and drove him to the one place in the country where everybody who could afford to take him from me would be standing in a single field at the same time. And I did it gladly. That was the kind of clever I'd got.

Hickstead. You say the word a certain way in this sport, the way the religious say a place they've never been. The All England. The Derby Bank, that grass cliff men have galloped down for seventy years and a fair number have not got up from. I'd had pictures of it the way I'd had pictures of Tatefield, and the same thing happened the same way: the pictures had lied by leaving out the size of it. We came over the Sussex Downs in the grey before dawn, the lorry drinking diesel the way it always did, the gaffer-taped wing mirror shuddering, and there it was below us in the mist — rings inside rings, the great permanent stands, the white boards, a course already built in the main arena under the floodlights they'd left on for the grooms, fences the height of a kitchen worktop standing in dewed grass that someone had been paid more than my mother earned in a month to keep that exact green.

"Posh field," said Curtis, who'd driven the last leg. He'd got out to look and was hugging himself in the cold. "Posh field, this."

"It's the poshest field," I said.

"It's a field, though. In't it. At the bottom of it. Under all the — " he waved a hand at the stands, the banners, the catering lorries the size of houses — "it's grass and a horse and a stick to jump over, same as Sheffield."

It wasn't, and we both knew it wasn't, and that was Curtis's whole method — say the true thing as a joke so it didn't count, then say the other true thing right after it as if the first had cancelled it. I unhooked the ramp. Behind it Saracen had gone quiet the way he went quiet when he was frightened and had decided not to fight yet, which is a different quiet from the good one and you have to have lived with a horse to tell them apart. I could tell them apart. I knew his frightened-still from his settled-still the way you know your own mother's two voices on a phone.

*

The thing nobody tells you about Hickstead, or about any of it once you're inside it, is how much of the day is the field of cars.

The lorry park at the All England is its own country, and you read a man's whole life off the box he keeps his horses in before you ever see him ride. We nosed in at the cheap end, the way we always did, between an outfit with the flags of three nations down its flank and a thing the size of a touring bus with living quarters and a hydraulic ramp that came down on its own with a sigh, and out of that one stepped a groom in a polo shirt with the yard's name embroidered over the heart, and behind her, unhurried, came Tomas Berg.

I'd met him at Windsor. He looked at me now the way he'd looked at me then, which I'd had to get used to because it wasn't like the way the rest of them looked, the well-bred ones, who looked at my boots and my hire-purchase half-chaps and the rust coming through the green of our box and arranged their faces. Tomas didn't arrange his face. He looked at the horse coming down our ramp and then he looked at me, the rider, two facts of equal interest, and he nodded, once, the nod you give a man you've decided is in the same trade as you.

"The black horse," he said. Swedish, the consonants set down very clean and level, like a man putting cups on saucers. "Windsor. You went clear and I had the back rail of the double. You rode the line in seven where everyone walked eight. That was correct. I walked eight and I was wrong." He said it the way you'd report the weather. No ego in it anywhere, which is rarer in that warm-up ring than money, and money was everywhere. "He is a careful horse."

"He is."

"Careful is worth more than brave." Tomas looked at Saracen a moment longer, at the snap-up tuck of him, the too-much white in the corner of the eye, the cathedral shoulder. "You can buy brave," he said. "I have bought brave. It comes off them in a big field with a crowd. Careful, you cannot buy. Someone has to make it." His gaze came back to me, level, with nothing in it I could push against. "Who made it?"

"He made it," I said. "I just stopped letting people make it worse."

Tomas thought about that with the seriousness he'd give a line in a jump-off. "That is the same thing," he said, "said modestly," and he turned and went to his bus, and I stood there in the cold with the truest sentence anyone had said to me about my own gift hanging in the diesel air, said by a man who felt nothing about it at all. *That is the same thing.* Stopping people making it worse, and making it. He'd seen in one Windsor afternoon what it had taken me till a feed-room doorway in May to see, and it hadn't cost him a thing to see it, and it wasn't going to cost him a thing to beat me with it either, and that was Tomas. He was what I'd be if I ever managed to want nothing. I didn't know yet whether I envied him or whether I'd rather have drowned, and I've had ten years and I still go back and forth.

*

The going was good but it had rained in the night, soft on the top with the summer ground hard as a road underneath, the worst combination there is — grab and then nothing, so a horse thinks he's got a foot and hasn't. I studded him for it on the lorry ramp in the first proper light, kneeling at his legs with the stud tap and the little tin, road studs in front for the hard floor under the grass and big squares behind for the grab on top, off-hind first, because that's the foot a horse snatches when he goes to land off something big and you want grip in it before he asks the question.

I gave him the ground. So he could hold. The word that had started living next to *grip* in my head since Windsor came up again at the foot of the poshest field in England, and I did the thing I'd got good at, which was screw the off-hind tight and not look at it. He stood for the studding now, all four feet, where in March he'd have had me through the wall for touching that foot. I knew what that was worth in this field, to the men with the embroidered grooms. I tried, kneeling there, not to know what it was worth to him.

*

You walk a Hickstead course the way pilgrims do anything, slow, hats off, in a long straggling line of the people who're about to ride it, and you count the strides between the fences in big deliberate steps, four of yours to one of his, and you read where the designer's been clever — where he's built a distance a half-stride wrong so the brave ride deep and the careful chip in, where the light comes off a white wall into a horse's eye on the turn, where the going changes by the water and a galloping stride goes suddenly to mud.

The man had built clever, not big. The fences were 1.40m, which is its own country up from 1.30m — a country where scope stops being a thing you have and starts being a thing you spend — but he hadn't won the day on height. He'd won it on a related distance into the far corner, six strides off a square oxer to an upright stood tight against the boards on the bend, with a wall before it. A wall, and then a turn the horse couldn't see round, and then a fence almost on the rail, in the one corner of the arena where the stands came down close and the crowd stood three deep and a frightened horse would feel the world close to a chute.

I walked it twice. The first time as a rider. The second time as Saracen, which is a thing I'd taught myself to do and never told anyone, because it sounds like the worst kind of horse-book nonsense and it isn't nonsense, it's just attention — I walked the line again at his eye-height in my head, off the wall I couldn't see past, into a corner I couldn't see round, with people I couldn't see beyond standing where the open should be. And I stood at that upright on the bend with my heart doing a thing it had no business doing on foot in an empty ring, and I understood the day's question and it was a question built, by accident, exactly for the shape of one horse's particular terror, and that horse was mine, and we were going to have to answer it in front of everyone.

*

Marnie had Beau in a smaller class in the second ring and I went over before mine because I'd started doing that, watching her, and I couldn't have told you whether it was kindness or the thing I did instead of going to her, which was watch.

She went white round the mouth at the in-gate the way she always did, and her hands came up an inch and the old grey felt it and stiffened and you could see the whole round go small and careful and frightened in both of them, the horse minding the girl who was minding the horse, a closed loop of nerves with no door in it. Beau got round. He always got round, the gentleman, twelve years old and stiff in the mornings now and going in the off eye and worth, to anyone with a chequebook, exactly the steady honest 1.40m schoolmaster he was and not a thing he wasn't. They had a pole at the wall — Marnie backed off it, the way she backed off everything, and Beau backed off her backing off, and the front rail came down with the soft heartbreaking *knock* of a pole that rocks in the cup and decides, late, to fall.

She came out with her chin up and her eyes wet and her grandmother at the rail saying, not unkindly, in the precise cold patrician way that was worse than unkind: "You let him back off it, darling. He'd have left it up if you'd believed it."

"I know," Marnie said.

"You always know after."

Marnie looked at me across the collecting ring then, one look, and there was the whole of her in it, and I have wished since that I'd gone to her instead of going to walk my horse. But Caro's eyes had already crossed her granddaughter and gone past, the way water goes past a stone, and landed on me, on us, on the black horse coming up the lane to the warm-up, and I felt the attention land and I'm ashamed to say I felt it as warmth. The asset got the look. The granddaughter got the verdict. I'd seen Caro do that to Marnie every show all season and I'd told myself it wasn't my business, which is a thing you tell yourself about the cruelty you're benefiting from.

*

The warm-up at the All England is theatre with a real chance of injury, twenty horses circling a space built for ten, the practice oxer raised and lowered by grooms, the etiquette — heads up, pass left to left — laid over an open hierarchy where the names get the fence when they want it and the kid from Parson Cross fetches the pole when it falls.

It was the chute. It was always going to be the chute. The fenced lane up to the main arena ran between two stands and the crowd hung over the rail on both sides, a corridor of standing people, the exact shape of the thing he could not bear, and three off the practice fence I felt him go.

He went still under me. That's how he goes — not loud, the opposite of loud, he stops. The canter stayed but the horse left it, and the blinking stopped, and the muscle under his right eye did the small fast thing it does, there and gone, the tic that everyone in that ring would have read as a horse settling, going quiet, getting professional, and that I read as the only thing it ever was, which is a horse asking a question with the whole of his body in the half-second before he can't ask it any more. *Do I have to do this on my own. Or are you here.*

I gave the right rein away. Soft, the whole of it, the way you set down something you don't want to drop — I let the rein go to nothing on the side where the pain lived, where a man before me had hauled and held until pressure on the right meant a thing with no way out, and I sat very still and I waited the half-stride longer than his fear wanted me to wait, longer than was safe, long enough that the groom raising the practice oxer glanced at us because we were drifting toward his crowd, and I did not pick up the right rein, and Saracen, three strides from a fenced lane full of standing strangers, blew out through his nose, long and low, and gave me the half-stride. He always gave me the half-stride. Nobody else got it. Everybody else got the run-out, the slam to the left, the head in the air, the half-ton of him going sideways toward the rail and a steward's clipboard. I got the breath and the drop of the poll a single degree and the near ear coming round, tipping back, finding me, holding on me, one ear for me and one for the world, and we jumped the practice oxer clean off it, careful, the front legs snapping up neat to his chin the way he did it, apologetic, like a horse that had been hit for touching poles and had decided, with me, just this once, to risk being brave.

"Good lad," I said. Not loud. Just for him. "Good lad. That's the worst of it done."

It wasn't. The worst of it was in the ring.

*

The bell went and I knew the day's real clock before it rang, which I always did with him — not whether he could jump the height, he could jump a house, but whether he could jump *here*, in this, off that wall, into that corner, past those people. I crossed the start and the clock came on and we were going.

The fences came up the way they do when the partnership's right, which is to say they barely came up at all — the horse arrives and the fence is behind you and you're already riding the next, the careful neat snap of him under me again and again, the near ear flicking back between each one for the word and the off ear forward on the world. We were clear to the back of the course and I could feel the crowd come with us the way they do when a horse they've been told is a rogue goes round like a prayer.

Then the wall, and the dogleg off it, and the thing I'd walked twice on foot with my heart going.

I'm not going to make a set-piece of it. There'll be a day to do that and it isn't this one. I'll tell you what happened, which is that he met the wall green — jumped it big and frightened and landed steep, turning already toward the corner he couldn't see, the right rein going live under my hand and the muscle under his eye doing the small fast thing, and for one stride I felt the whole story of his life rise up in him, the left already in his quarters, the bolt loading. And I gave him the rein back and waited the half-stride and showed him the door, the shorthand of it now, the move I'd made a hundred times in the dark, *not that way, here, I'm here* — and he came back off it. Not clean. He came back late and chipped in a short one and rubbed the upright hard enough that the crowd gasped, and it rocked in the cup, and held. A rub, not a pole. We finished clear by the thickness of a coat of paint and the width of the half-stride only I ever get, and I rode the last line home with my heart going and the horse blowing and the thing not quite spent, held back, kept, because some part of me that I didn't look at was saving him, the way you save a horse you've started to suspect you're going to need to spend later.

That was the round. It was good. It frightened me how nearly it wasn't.

*

Caro was at the rail when I came out, and Raef was four people along from her in the members' enclosure, and I read them both before I'd got my breath.

Caro's hands were folded on the top rail and her face had the look it had worn at the gate the first day Saracen went round, the look I'd learned to call the wrong name for a long time — not the face of a woman watching a kindness, the face of a woman watching a sum come right. She said the loving thing. She always said the loving thing, that was the genius of her. "He's the bravest horse on this field and you're the only reason," she said, and her voice was thick, actually thick, real water standing in the old hard eyes, and it was true, all of it was true, that's what I could never get anyone to understand, that the tears were real and the appraisal was real and they lived in the same face at the same second and you could not get a blade between them. Both. Always both, with her. I'd given up trying to separate.

And four people along, Raef Al-Mazroui nodded.

He didn't clap. He didn't call out. He'd ridden, you could see he'd ridden, in the stillness of him, and a man who's ridden knows the rider coming off a round like that wants quiet and his horse's neck under his hand. Raef gave me the quiet and the one nod, and the nod was the worst thing in the field, because the nod was a man marking a thing nearly proved. Not *well done.* *Almost. Once more in the clear and I'll know.* The rub had spared me a fraction; he wanted to see it without the rub. Every brave stride had been a number going up, and the number wasn't finished going up, and I understood, getting the nod, that I'd be asked to finish it.

Tomas beat us in the jump-off, cleanly, leaving a stride out at the double that I sat up and fitted in, because I would not ask Saracen to gallop flat at speed into the kind of thing that had nearly had him in the first round — I would not spend him like that, not for a placing. Tomas would and did, and it cost his horse nothing, because his horse had never been afraid of anything in its life. We were second. The yard would eat for a fortnight off second at Hickstead, off what second at Hickstead did to the horse's price, which is the only economy that place runs on, and none of us ever said the part out loud.

*

She found me at the box.

I had the studs out of three feet and was on the off-hind, kneeling in the trampled grass of the lorry park with the tap and the tin, taking the grip back out of him now the job was done, unscrewing the thing I'd put into him to make him hold, and Caro stood over me with the rosette already in her hand because she'd taken it off the bridle, and she said it the way she said her hardest things, lightly, after the loving thing, when you were still warm from the loving thing and your guard was down on the floor where it had fallen.

"Raef would like to vet him."

I stayed on the off-hind. I felt the stud come loose and put it in the tin. I did not look up, because if I looked up she'd read whatever was on my face and ride it.

"Vet him," I said.

"It's how it's done, darling. A man doesn't buy a horse like this off a video and a handshake." There was no irony in it. She didn't hear what she'd said. A man doesn't buy a horse off a handshake — and I had given her my whole self off a handshake in a cold kitchen in March, and she said the sentence with her clear cold loving face and did not hear the bottom drop out of it, and I knelt at the foot of my horse and heard it for both of us. "They've a vet they use," she went on. "I've said the practice in Leominster, Iwan can do it, he knows the horse, it's better the buyer's man and our man are — it's better there's no surprises. The fortnight after next. I've put a date in." She paused. The pause was the leg going on. "You knew this was coming, Devlin. You're not a child. We can't keep a horse like this. We of all people can't. *You* of all people understand that, you've seen the books, you've seen me at that table with the brown envelopes. He's worth more than the roof. He's worth more than the *yard.* And he's worth that because of what you did with him, which is the thing I want you to hold onto when it's hard, that you *made* this, you made a horse worth saving everything we've got, and there'll be — " and here it came, the soft turn, the door shown where the wall was — "there'll be another. There's always another. You'll have your horse. You have my word. This isn't the one, that's all. This was the one that pays for all the others. Including yours."

I screwed the off-hind stud-hole stopper in so the dirt wouldn't pack it. I stood up. I made myself look at her then because not looking would have told her more than looking.

"He's frightened of vettings," I said. "Strange men, the trot-up, the flexions, a stranger taking a contact on the right rein and not knowing what it means. He'll go the way he went off the wall today. He'll try to leave."

"Then you'll be there," Caro said, "won't you," and she put the rosette into my hand and closed my fingers over it with both of her ruined ones, the loose ring riding on the thin finger, warm, the loving thing, the realest love I had in the world standing there in a field, and I could not for the life of me tell you, then or now, whether she'd said *then you'll be there* because she trusted me to gentle the horse through it kindly, or because she knew that I was the only one who could get the frightened horse to override its fear long enough to pass a stranger's vet and complete her sale, and the unbearable thing, the thing I carry, is that it was the same sentence either way. Care and use in one breath, out of one mouth, and not a blade between them. The hand that holds a frightened mouth soft and the hand that holds it soft so it'll sell. I'd been doing it to horses all season. I just hadn't believed it could be done to me by someone whose tears were real.

*

I got him loaded second time of asking, which for him at the end of a day like that was a thing close to a miracle, and stood the studs-out feet of him in the bedding and gave him his net and watched him pull at it, blowing, sweat-dark down the near-black neck gone the colour of a wet conker in the last of the Sussex light, the white coronet on the off-hind the one bright thing on him in the dim of the box. The rosette was on the bridle hung over the partition. Second at Hickstead. The board would say it, the clip would travel, the price would move.

He put his head over the partition to me the way he did, last thing, and the near ear came round and tipped back, listening, on me, only on me, the way it had been all day at every fence and every fright, one ear for me and one for the world, the proof of the thing I'd built, the proof of the lie I'd taught him in a chute in April that his fear could be borne if he wasn't alone with it — and I put my hand on the wet neck and my thumb found the scar without me deciding it would, the hand's-width of pale that no one alive could explain, and I stood there and didn't say anything, because I'd run out of things I had the right to say.

Across the lorry park, between our rusting box and a touring bus with the flags of three nations down its flank, I saw them.

Caro and Raef, by his enormous gleaming lorry, in the floodlit dusk. I couldn't hear a word. I was too far, and the generators were going, and the world was full of the noise of a horse show packing up to go home. But I didn't need to hear it. I read it the way I read everything, off the bodies, the way I'd been reading bodies since I was a child reading my mother's, when love and the ask came down the same line in the same voice and you learned to tell the weather off a person before they spoke. Raef stood the way a man stands who has decided. Caro stood the way she stood at the kitchen table over the brown envelopes when she thought no one could see, except now her shoulders had come down off her ears, the fear gone off her for the first time all season, because the thing in the box behind me had taken it off her.

And then Raef put out his hand. And Caro took it.

I watched it across a hundred yards of trodden grass, the handshake, the deal that needs no paper between people who can afford solicitors and have them anyway, and I felt Saracen's breath warm on the back of my neck and the near ear stayed tipped back to me, home, listening, not knowing one single thing about what two people were settling with their hands in a floodlit field, and I stood between the horse who trusted me and the women shaking hands to sell him, and I did not call out, and I did not walk over, and I did not pack my bag, and I put my hand up over my shoulder onto his wet face in the dark and let him lean the weight of his great frightened head into it, and I watched my whole life get decided by two people I couldn't hear, the way it always had been, off a handshake, the once that mattered, with no paper anywhere near it at all.


---

# Chapter Nineteen — Clear

There is a thing a hand does when the deal is already done and the shake is only the lid going on. I'd seen Caro's hand do it across the lorry park the evening before, and Raef's close over it slow and certain, and a navy-fleeced groom stand a polite yard back with a new headcollar over his arm, soft leather and brass, the colour of a thing bought that week. I'd told myself I couldn't read it. I can read hands before I can read faces; I'd learned it on my mother across a kitchen in Parson Cross, you read the hands to know whether the night's going to be the good kind or the other kind. I read it. Then I told myself the words were what mattered, and I hadn't heard the words, so I didn't know anything. That was the lie I went to sleep on.

I lay in my clothes in the caravan and listened to Saracen blow himself down two hundred yards off in the dark — that long ragged flutter winding down to nothing he always made after a big track — and I thought, *tomorrow he jumps the biggest he's ever jumped, and I'll ride the round of my life, and that'll settle it.* As if a round could settle a thing handshakes had already settled. As if going clear were an argument I could win.

*

Hickstead in the last week of June is the loudest quiet the sport's got. You walk the course in the morning before the crowd's properly in and there's just the larks going up off the Sussex grass and the great green bank sitting there at the top of the arena like a thing left over from a bigger sport, and the white rails, and the flags hanging dead in the early air, and you pace the lines with your jacket off, four big steps to a stride, counting, the way I'd watched the professionals count in March and not known what they were doing and now did in my sleep.

I walked it with Glenys, which I shouldn't have — grooms don't walk the course, riders walk the course — but she came in through the gap with her wellingtons and her three jumpers and nobody told a sixty-year-old Welshwoman where she could and couldn't put her feet, and we paced the long related distance down to the wall and she stood and looked at the wall a long time, the wall built up high and solid out of those lightweight blocks that look like the side of a house and fall like nothing if a horse so much as breathes the truth at them.

"His worst sort of thing, that," she said.

"I know what it is."

"A wall on a turn off a corner." She looked at the line into it, the way you came at it blind round the dogleg with the bank behind, no run at it, no time to see it coming. "Built mean, this one. Course-builder's earning his money." She put the unlit roll-up between her lips and took it out again. "How's he in himself?"

"Good," I said, and meant it. "He loaded second time of asking yesterday. He's eating up. He stood at the rail this morning watching the lorries and he didn't lather." That was the thing I had, the thing I always had instead of the things everyone else had — not whether the horse could jump it, he could jump the moon, but whether he could jump it *here*, in the chute, in the crowd, with the bank behind him he couldn't see past and the wall on the turn he couldn't read until he was on it. The height was never the question. The height was the easy bit.

Glenys nodded at the wall, slow. "You'll mind him at that."

"I'll mind him at that."

"Aye," she said. "Mind yourself and all." And she walked off back toward the lines with her square strong back rolling, and didn't say the other thing, the thing she'd said to me once, the once, weeks back in the dark by his box — *they'll have the horse off you, love, and they'll have you and all, and they'll do it kind so you'll thank them* — she'd said it once and she didn't say it twice, she'd told me she wouldn't and she was a woman who did what she said, but she'd said it once and it lived in me now permanent, like the hoof on the wall the first night, and I heard it again walking out of that arena even though her mouth never moved.

*

The class was the big one, the feature of the day, 1.40m with a jump-off, a course that walked clever and rode mean, and there were forty in it and most of them had cost more than the Hall and were ridden by people who'd never once in their lives eaten fast in case there wasn't more. Tomas Berg was in it, third to go, on a great machine of a bay that jumped like it was bored. He went clear inside the time without ever appearing to hurry, the way he did everything, and he came out past me at the in-gate with the reins in one hand and nodded — "The treble walks short, the second element. Sit up or you'll have it" — and gave me the truth about the line for free, no ego in it, the way he gave me everything, a colleague's courtesy with nothing underneath it, and went off to ice a horse that didn't need icing.

We were drawn twenty-eighth. You spend the whole morning being twenty-eighth. You watch twenty-seven rounds and you walk the horse and you wash your hands of the watching because the watching does you no good, and you put the studs in.

I studded him on the ramp because the lorry park ground was too churned to kneel on. Big squares behind for the holding going, the grass cut up by then into that toffee they get by mid-afternoon, off-hind first because the off-hind's the foot a horse snatches when you go near it and you do the worst foot first while he's still got the patience. He cocked it for me and let me run them in, and I knelt at his hind end with the stud-tap in the cold and the show going on over the hedge, the tannoy, the applause, the bell, and I did his feet and gave him the ground to hold on the way I'd done it every show since April, and I'd like to tell you I thought about nothing but grip. I'd like that on the record the way I wanted the first time on the record, the Hartpury night, when it was care and only care. But this time there was a thing in me kneeling at his hind end that I made myself not look at, the way you don't look at the thing in the corner of a room at night so it'll stay a coat and not a man, and the thing was the new headcollar over the navy groom's arm, soft and brass and the colour of a thing bought that week. I screwed the studs in. I gave him grip. I did not let myself finish the other thought, which was that I was tightening the shoes on a horse so he could jump well enough to be taken.

He turned his head round and put his nose on the back of my collar while I did the last foot, the way he'd started to, and breathed there, slow, warm through the fleece, and I stood up and put my hand flat on his near shoulder, on the scar, the pale slick place I can never read, the page torn out of him, and I said, plain, the only way I can talk to him:

"Just the one round. Then the dark and the haynet. I promise."

I meant it as a kindness. I didn't know yet it was a lie, and not the good kind I tell him at a fence — *not yet, not while I'm here* — but the other kind, the kind grown-ups tell when the thing's already decided and the shake's just the lid going on.

*

The bell went.

I want to be careful here because this is the round I rode the best I ever rode anything, and the temptation is to be clever about it, to make it a thing of strides and inside lines, and it wasn't that, or it was that and that wasn't the half of it. It was the two of us, and it was the best we ever were, and that is exactly the trouble with it. I've gone over it ten years and the cruelty of it is precise: I rode the round of my life, and the round of my life was the thing that finished him.

Forty-five seconds to start. I picked the canter up and let him look at the first while we circled, the great green bank standing behind it, and I felt him take the whole arena in through his skin the way he did, the crowd three and four deep down both long sides, the flags lifting now in the afternoon wind, and I felt the question start in him before we'd even crossed the line — not panic, just the asking, the body asking *is this the kind of place where the bad thing happens* — and I gave him the answer I always gave him, the soft long *not yet* down the rein, and he dropped his poll a degree and blew out, and the near ear came back to me and the off ear went out on the world, and we crossed the start.

First fence, a big ascending oxer off the bank, and he stood off it and snapped his front legs up neat to his chin, careful, that apologetic careful of a horse that's been hit for touching poles and decided never again, and we landed and I heard the crowd take a breath because they hadn't seen him before, this black horse out of nowhere being neat at the top of a fence that size, and we ran down to the treble.

The treble walked short in the second element, Tomas had said, sit up or you'll have it, and I sat up, and Saracen came back to me a hair quicker than his fear wanted because I asked and he believed me, and we jumped in and out and out of the treble clean, the near ear flicking back to me between each, *here? here? are you here?*, and me giving it back, *here, I'm here, go*, the whole conversation in a stride and a half, the realest talk I've ever had with anything, the talk that has no words and never lies.

And then the dogleg, and the wall.

You came at it round the bend with the bank at your back, no run, the wall built up high and mean on the turn so the horse couldn't see it square until he was nearly on it, and three strides out I felt the right rein come live under my hand the way it does, the old terror, the curb that somebody held on him before any of us, *the bad thing is on the right and there's no escape from the bad thing* — and the wall coming, the wall he couldn't see past, the cornered thing and the right-rein thing both at once, the two oldest fears in his body arriving together in three strides at speed — and he went still.

He went still and he stopped blinking and the muscle under his right eye flickered, that small fast tic, there and gone, and everyone in that arena who knew anything thought, *he's going to be a sod, he's going to run out left, watch* — and I knew what it was, because I'm the only one who's ever known what it is. It's the last second he's still asking. It's the held breath before the bolt. It's the whole horse asking me with his whole body the one question: *do I have to do this on my own, or are you here.*

And I gave him the right rein. I gave it to him soft, all of it, opened my fingers and gave him the bad side back like it was nothing, like there was no curb anywhere in the world, like the wall was a gate I was holding open for him, and I waited — I waited the half-stride longer than his fear wanted, the half-stride only I ever get, the half-stride everyone else's horse never gives them because they snatch at the right rein at exactly the second you have to give it away — and I felt him decide. I felt him spend it. I felt him take whatever he had left of trusting a person and lay it down on the floor of that arena and come off the ground at a wall he couldn't see past, going up blind, because I'd told him to and he'd decided, this once, with me, that the asking was safe.

He cleared it by the foot it didn't need. Careful, neat, the legs snapped up apologetic to his chin. The crowd came up off the rails with a noise like the sea.

Two more fences. I don't remember them the way I remember the wall. I remember the bell going at the finish and the clock stopping and the board going up.

Clear.

Clear round at Hickstead on a horse three months off a lorry to a sale he was never coming back from, and the great green bank behind us and the crowd on its feet, and I sat down into the saddle and put my hand on his wet neck and he blew out long and low and ragged, and the near ear stayed tipped back to me, *was that all right, was I good, are we all right*, and I said it down to him under the noise, plain — "You were perfect. You were perfect" — and for one second, riding him out the gate with the crowd still going, I was as happy as I have ever been, and it is the last time in this whole story that the happiness came clean, and I didn't know it, which is the only mercy in it.

*

It was at the in-gate, between the round and the jump-off, that I learned.

Not in an office. That's the thing I've come to hate about how it happened — that it happened in the warm, in the middle of the best thing, with the crowd noise not even faded yet. I came off the course and a steward took my number and I was loosening the girl-girth a hole and patting him and looking for Glenys, and the navy groom was there. The one with the new headcollar. He'd come round to our end. He had a tape measure in his hand — a tailor's tape, soft, the kind you measure for a rug with — and he was standing at Saracen's quarters, not touching, polite, and he said to me, courteous, with an accent I couldn't place and no malice in it at all, the way you'd ask a porter:

"His back, please — wither to dock. Mr Al-Mazroui asks. For the travel rugs and the stable rugs. We have his measurements from the vetting Tuesday but the wither I would do again to be sure."

I looked at him. I had the reins in one hand and the horse blowing under me and the rosette not even on the bridle yet.

"The vetting," I said.

"Tuesday past," he said, and something went over his face when he saw mine, a softening, an ah, the look of a man who's realised he's the one carrying the news. He was kind about it. That's the thing. He was kind about it the way Iwan was kind, the way they were all kind. "He passed very well," he said gently. "Mr Al-Mazroui is delighted. He flies him out after your big show in October — he is very pleased the horse jumps your Birmingham first, he wishes to see him in the indoor before. I am sorry. I thought you would know. I thought" — and he stopped, and folded the soft tape in half, and didn't finish, because the kind ones never finish, they leave the worst of it for you to do yourself.

I sat there on a horse I'd just been perfect on and did the worst of it myself.

The vetting was Tuesday. They'd vetted him while I was mucking out. Iwan in the yard with his soft cracked hands and his wax jacket dark across the shoulders, drawing the blood, storing the truth, and nobody told me because I wasn't anybody who needed telling, I wasn't on the paper anywhere, there was no paper, there'd never been any paper, board and coaching and *eventually a horse, you have my word, don't ask me to insult us both by writing it down* — and the horse I'd made, the horse I'd given the right rein back to a thousand times in the dark, the horse who'd just laid his last trust down on the floor of Hickstead because I asked him to, was sold. Done. The shake was the lid going on. October. He jumps Birmingham first because the new owner wants to watch his new horse in the indoor before they fly him to the Gulf, and the reason the new owner wants the horse to jump Birmingham, the reason the sale even wants me near him in October, is that a horse that wins under lights at HOYS is worth more flying out than a horse that doesn't — and I, the only person alive who can ride him, am the last bit of value they need to put on him before they hand him over.

I got it all in about four seconds, sitting on the horse, with the crowd noise just dying behind me and the rosette not yet on the bridle. I read it the way I read everything, fast, by feel, half in the dark. I just read it on a steward's clipboard and a groom's tape measure instead of a mouth, but I read it.

"Thank you," I said to the groom. I don't know why I said thank you. Pip said thank you when they took the mare. My mother said thank you to people who took things from us my whole childhood. It's what we say, where I'm from, when the people taking the thing are being lovely about it. *They'll do it kind, so you'll thank them.* I'd thanked him before the thought finished forming.

*

And then I had to ride the jump-off.

That's the part. That's the part nobody who hasn't done it understands. I had four minutes and a clear round behind me and the yard needed the placing and the prize money — the loan was due in October, the figure ruinous, the date fixed, and a placing at Hickstead in the feature class was real money and it raised the price of the horse another notch besides, the horse that was already sold, so that winning the jump-off would put more money in the cheque that was buying him away from me — and Caro was at the rail, and Raef was in the members', and the bell was going to go, and I had to take that horse back into that arena and ride brilliantly, ride better, ask him for the brave thing again, the inside line, the wall a second time, because that's what the round was for now, that's what I was for, and the better I went the more certainly I'd confirm the sale I'd learned about ninety seconds ago.

I'd thought I understood the inversion. I'd known it the way you know a thing in your head — that the cleaner he went the surer he was sold, that I was building the price tag every evening in the dark and calling it love.

I learned it that afternoon in my body, which is the only place I ever really learn anything. I learned it the way the horse learns the right rein. Hope and dread didn't take turns any more. They came together, in the same three strides, at the same wall, the way his two oldest fears came together — and I had to ride through it the way I'd just made him ride through his, give it all away and wait the half-stride longer than the fear wants and go.

The bell went.

I took him back in and I rode the jump-off like the round of my life, because there was no other way left to ride it, and somewhere in me a colder thing took over, the thing Tomas Berg has where his wound used to be, and it rode the horse beautifully and felt nothing it could afford to feel. Inside line to the second fence, leave the stride out down the long side, and the wall on the turn again, blind, mean, and I came at it and felt the right rein go live and the still and the stopped blink and the flicker, *do I do this on my own or are you here*, and I gave it to him, I gave him the bad side back, and I want to tell you my hand was steady and it was, it was perfectly steady, and that's the thing I can't lay flat after ten years, that my hand was steady — that I asked a frightened animal to spend the last of his borrowed courage at speed at a wall he couldn't see past, *for a placing in a cheque that was buying him away from the only person who'd ever told him the truth*, and my hand did not shake, because I'd got that good at it, because the flinch had gone out of me somewhere back down the season and I hadn't even noticed the day it left.

He cleared it. Of course he cleared it. He believed me. He always believed me. That was never the part that was going to fail.

*

We won.

The board went up and the crowd came up and the announcer said the name of a yard most of them had never heard of and a rider in grey half-chaps, and I rode him round on a loose rein with the rosette being pinned to the bridle, the red rosette, the winner's, the size of a side plate, and Caro was at the rail with her ruined beautiful hands together and her face doing the thing — and I read it, I made myself read it, I owed myself that much — and her face had on it, at the same time, in the same instant, with nothing to separate them, real joy and real relief and real love for me, the boy who'd just won her the biggest class of her year and saved her one more month of the roof and the silver, and under it, behind it, woven through it so you could not pull the two apart any more than you can pull the love from the ask in my mother's voice down a phone, the other thing: the owner's relief, the asset confirmed, the price gone up with the placing, the deal she'd shaken on yesterday made good in front of the man who'd buy it. Both. Always both. She'd told me that herself with her hand on his scar. *Both. Always both, with me.* I'd heard a coaching tip.

Raef in the members' stand inclined his head to me, once, with real warmth and total accuracy, the most honest man in the building, the only one who'd ever offered me a wage and a contract and a future and called it by its right name — and I understood, getting the head-incline, that his warmth was real too, that he wasn't a villain, that he'd just done the one thing none of the people who loved me would do, which was tell me to my face exactly what I was worth, and pay it, and now he'd told me to my face exactly what the horse was worth, and paid that too. The accuracy was the cruelty. There was no cruelty in him anywhere except the accuracy.

And Glenys, at the rail, who'd warned me once and only once, took the unlit roll-up from behind her ear and held it in her fingers and looked at it, and didn't light it, and looked away from me, out across the arena at the great green bank, at nothing, the way you look away from a thing you told a child not to walk into and then watched them walk into because there was nothing else for them to do.

I rode him out of the gate. The crowd noise fell off behind us into the ordinary sound of a showground in the afternoon. And in the collecting ring, quiet now, the navy groom with the soft new headcollar over his arm came and stood at the rail, not pushing, polite, the way Raef's people did everything, and waited.

He'd come to learn the horse. That's all. He'd come to start learning the horse, the way you start learning a thing that's going to be yours.

I got off. My legs weren't right under me. I ran the stirrups up and loosened the girth the rest of the way and put my hand flat on the wet near shoulder, on the scar, the place I can never read, and Saracen turned his head round to me and put his nose against my chest and blew out, long and low and done, the big ragged flutter winding down, sweat-dark on his neck and the steam coming up off him in the cooling air, the off-hind cocked and resting with its little ring of white round the coronet, and the near ear — the near ear stayed tipped back to me, all the way, listening, one ear for me and one for the world, the split, the proof, *was that all right, were we all right, are we all right* — and I stood there with my hand on him and the red rosette on his bridle and the new owner's man waiting quiet at the rail with the new headcollar, and the horse did not know.

That was the whole of it. He did not know. He thought he'd had the best day of his life and got it right and been right to trust me, and he had, all of that was true, and none of it was the thing that was happening. He'd just spent the last of himself being perfect, and being perfect was the thing that handed him over, and he stood there breathing into my jacket with the near ear tipped back to me and not one single number anywhere in him, and I had built every coin of the price that was taking him, *not yet* by *not yet*, in the dark, with my own hands, and called it love, and it was love, and that was the worst of it, that it was love, and I put my hand on the wet neck and I didn't say anything to him at all, because for the first time since the bolted box in March there was nothing true left that I could say.

I didn't know yet that I'd never ride him in a class again — except the once that mattered. I only knew the near ear was still tipped back to me, and the man at the rail was waiting, and the horse was blowing himself quiet, and trusting me, and not knowing.


---

# Chapter Twenty — The Vetting

The horse did not know, and a fortnight later the horse still did not know, and that was the only part of it that hadn't changed.

He came up to the gate of the school field with the near ear already tipping back to me before I'd whistled, the way he did now, the off-hind cocked when he stood, the little ring of white at the coronet bright in the July grass — he'd come up the colour of a wet conker by then, the summer coat in proper, dappling over the quarters where Glenys had been feeding him like money — and he put his nose against my chest and blew out, that long low flutter, the one that means *the next ten seconds are all right because you're in them.* Same as Hickstead. Same as the dark box in March. He'd had two weeks of turnout and good doing and a man who came to him morning and night and told him the truth, and as far as Saracen was concerned the world had finally, after seven years, turned out to be the kind of place he'd been hoping it was.

I knew different. I'd known different since the navy groom and his tape measure. But knowing a thing in your head and carrying it down a field toward a horse who doesn't know it are two different jobs, and the second one is heavier, and I did it every day that fortnight, twice, and didn't tell anyone, including him.

What I had not known, on the morning Iwan's grey Transit came knocking up the lane with the practice name peeling off the side, was the date.

*

Caro told me at the kitchen table, over the everyday mugs, which is how I should've heard it coming — everyday mugs, not the gold-cracked china, *we're all family here.*

"Thursday," she said. "Iwan'll do the vetting Thursday. He's coming up tomorrow to have a look at him first, settle him to it, so Thursday isn't his first time being prodded by a stranger in a hurry." She had the brown envelopes by her elbow, the way she always had them by her elbow now, unopened, the loan letter in among them somewhere, *end of October, the figure ruinous, the date fixed.* She didn't open them. She talked over the top of them. "You'll have him in. He likes you. He doesn't like being stood on a hard surface and trotted at — what horse does — but he'll do it for you and not for Glenys and not for me, so it's you that has him on the day. You understand that."

"I understand it."

"It's a five-stage." She said it lightly, a thing you'd say about the weather. But I'd been at Tatefield four months by then and I'd learned to watch her hands while her mouth talked, the way I watch a horse's quarters and not its face, and her thumbnail had gone to the loose signet ring and was turning it, round and round, on the finger gone too thin to hold it. "Stages one to five. He'll listen to the heart. He'll look in his eyes in the dark stable. He'll trot him up, flex the legs, ride him hard, rest him, trot him up again to see if the hard work brings out anything the morning hid. And he'll" — the ring went round — "take a bit of blood. They keep it. It's all very thorough. Raef's having his own man there as well, which is his right, he's buying."

I said nothing. I was reading the ring going round.

"It's the one thing left," Caro said, and she did look up then, and her face was open in a way it almost never was, almost frightened, and I couldn't tell if she was letting me see it or couldn't help it. "He passes the vet, the sale completes, the yard sees October. He doesn't pass the vet —" She stopped. She put the mug down, click, the everyday mug on the scrubbed wood, no gold in it. "He passes," she said. "He'll pass. He's the soundest horse in this yard. There's not a thing wrong with his legs. You of all people know there's not a thing wrong with him."

There wasn't a thing wrong with his legs. That was true. There was a thing wrong with the rest of him, and it wasn't a thing a vet flexes a hock for, and it wasn't a thing the soundest pair of forelegs in Herefordshire could carry him past on a hard yard with a stranger's hands on his bad side and his back to a wall he couldn't see over.

"He'll pass," I said.

I believed it less than she did. I'd been in the box no one opened. I knew exactly the kind of day a vetting was, for a horse whose fear lived in his poll and his right eye and the memory of being cornered with no way out — a stranger trotting him at on a hard surface, in hand, the lead rope on the wrong side, the flexion tests, the strange man's thumb running down the off-hind, the dark stable for the eyes, the small enclosed places, the no-run-at-it, the no-explaining-it-to-him. A vetting is a morning of asking a frightened horse to stand quiet and override every single thing his body is telling him while a man he's never met decides what he's worth. It's the whole con of the season, done in two hours, with a clipboard.

It is, near enough, exactly the thing I do.

It just doesn't usually have a number written on the end of it for somebody else to keep.

*

Iwan came up the Wednesday in the grey Transit, the *& SON* peeling worse than the rest of it, and got out in instalments the way he did, big and gone soft, the wax jacket dark across the shoulders as if he drove through his own private rain even in July. He had the kind sunk eyes and the cracked hands and he shook mine, which he didn't have to, a vet doesn't shake the working pupil's hand, and he said, "You're the Sheffield boy. The one off the rogue," as though we'd not met three times before, which was how tired he was, and we walked up to Saracen's box together not saying much.

Saracen put his head over the door and read Iwan the way he read everyone, and pinned for half a second, the ears going flat-back and hard to the poll, and then — because Iwan stood still and didn't reach and let himself be decided about, which most people can't manage and which is the only thing that's ever worked on this horse — the ears came up off the poll, and Saracen blew at him, wary, and Iwan said, "There's a big handsome thing, isn't it. There's a lot of horse in there," and didn't touch him, and Saracen let him not touch him, which is the most a stranger ever got.

"He's better than he was in March," I said.

"He's a different animal than the one I saw in March." Iwan looked at him a long time, the slow professional look, the eye going down the neck and the shoulder, pausing where everyone's eye paused, on the pale slick hand's-width of scar on the near shoulder, the page torn out of him, the one part nobody can read. "Somebody before you held him too hard on the right," Iwan said, mild. "You can see it in how he carries the poll. He'll throw his head off the right rein the rest of his life, a bit, even off you. It's in there now. It doesn't come out." He said it without judgement, a man stating a fact about a leg. Then: "He's worth a deal of money, this horse. You made him worth it." He turned to look at me with the sunk kind eyes. "It's the legs they pay for. But it's the head that's worth keeping, and the head's the part you can't sell, see. The head goes with whoever he trusts." He looked back at the horse. "And he trusts you. Which is going to be a problem for somebody, one of these days, but not my problem, thank God."

I should have heard that for what it was. I heard it as a kindness. I went on liking him. I went on liking him the whole way through what came next, which is the part I've never been able to lay flat — that he was kind, and gentle with the horse, and the gentlest thing in the yard, and I liked him, and he did it anyway, and so did I.

He had a look at the legs. He ran his thumb down each one the way I do, by feel, for heat, for filling, for the half-beat that isn't there, and found nothing, because there was nothing. He stood the horse up square and walked round him. And then he didn't go to the van for his bag. He stood with his back to the box and looked out across the yard at the muck heap steaming gently and the tarp snapping on the indoor roof, and he got a packet of mints out of his pocket and offered me one and took one himself, and there was a thing coming, I could feel it coming the way you feel the right rein go live three strides out, and I waited for it the way you wait, and gave nothing away.

"The thing about a horse like this," Iwan said, to the muck heap, not to me, "is he'll pass anything in the world on his legs. There's not a vet in three counties would mark him. It's only" — the mint clicked against his teeth — "it's only that a vetting's a long old morning, isn't it. Trotting up on the hard, in hand. The flexions. A strange man's hands all down the wrong side of him, and the dark stable for the eyes, and the wee enclosed places, and the man's buyer standing there watching, wanting to see him sweet. And a horse like this, on the wrong morning —" He let it hang.

"On the wrong morning he runs out the gate sideways," I said, "and they decide there's a screw loose in him, and the sale dies."

"Aye." He looked at me then, sidelong, almost grateful that I'd said it so he didn't have to. "There's nothing wrong with the screw. You and I both know there's nothing wrong with the screw. It's only fear, and fear isn't a fault, except it is, isn't it, on the morning a man's writing the cheque. A buyer can't tell the difference between a horse that's unsound and a horse that's frightened. Looks the same trotting up. Same hitch. And you can't put *he's frightened* on a vet cert." He turned the mint over. "So what we do, with the difficult ones — what's done, with the difficult ones — is we just help them along a bit. Settle him. Take the edge off the morning, just the once, just enough that he can stand still long enough for the man to see what's actually in there, which is a sound brave horse and nothing else. We're not hiding anything. There's nothing to hide. We're only" — and here his voice did the thing it does, the careful passive going soft and slow and trailing, a man not-quite-saying — "we're only making sure the truth of him gets a fair hearing on the day."

I understood every word of it. I understood it the way I understand a horse — not the words, the body, the thing the words are walking around. He never named it. He didn't name the thing he'd do, and I want that down: he never once said the word, not then and not Thursday, and neither did I, not then and not ever, and that not-naming was the whole transaction, it was the handshake, it was Caro's *don't insult us both by writing it down* with a needle in it instead of a horse.

What he was telling me, in his soft Welsh trailing grammar with a mint clicking against his teeth, was that he would make the horse lie.

Not hurt him. That's the thing I had to get my head round, standing there, and it's the thing that made it possible to do what I did, which was nothing. He wasn't going to hurt Saracen. He was going to do, with chemistry, in a morning, the exact thing I'd spent four months doing with my hands and my voice and the half-stride and the right rein given away soft — he was going to reach into a frightened animal and make the fear sit down so the body would read calm and sound and sweet to a man with a cheque, would *go quiet* the way I made him go quiet, except mine was a lie the horse believed and chose, and Iwan's was a lie poured into him that he didn't get a vote on. Same lie. Same body made to read a thing that wasn't the truth of it, on a morning when a man was deciding what it was worth.

The only difference was the consent, and I'd already worked out, weeks back, on a chestnut mare at a water tray, that there was no line there either. That the horse never really gets a vote. That I'd just got so good at the asking that it felt like the horse was choosing, and the not-feeling-where-the-line-would-be was the whole of my gift, the thing I'd been praised into perfecting since I was a kid on a free cob in Sheffield.

"And the blood?" I said. Because I knew about the blood. I'd watched him draw it in March, find the vein with his thumb the way you'd find a stud hole, and I'd heard him say it, kind, proud almost: *the horse can't lie to you and neither can his blood, it's there if anyone ever wants to look.*

Something went over Iwan's face. Shame, I think, though it never reached anything that stopped his hands. The blood was the part he couldn't soften, because the blood was the part that would still be true on a shelf in his fridge in October, in November, the one honest body left in the whole arrangement, sealed and dated, telling the thing the horse couldn't tell and the cheque couldn't cover.

"The blood's drawn at the fifth stage," he said, quiet. "After. It's stored. Held. It's — it can be tested, if anyone ever has cause. Months on, even. The buyer's man'll see it drawn, fair and square, into the tube, sealed in front of him." He looked at the muck heap. "Nobody ever has cause," he said. "In near thirty years I've drawn a thousand of them and I've never once known a stored sample tested. It just sits there. It's a — it's a thing we do so the sport can tell itself it's clean. It mostly never gets looked at."

*Mostly.* He'd said *mostly.*

"And if it did get looked at," I said.

He didn't answer that. He put the mint packet back in his pocket and pushed off the wall and went to the van for his bag at last, and over his shoulder, not to me really, to the yard, to the July afternoon, he said: "Marian's appointment's been put back again. Hereford. August now, they're saying. There's a thing wrong they won't put a name to till they've looked, and they keep not looking. So." He got the bag. "We all of us drive a hundred miles a day past the thing we should stop the van for, Sheffield boy. That's the job. You'll find out."

He was telling me why. Drowning man, hurt horse, and a why he'd never have said to a vet board and said easy to a council-estate lad who'd understand it, because I did understand it, the money that's a different country, the appointment put back, the *& SON* with no son, the kindness done to one creature paid for by a coldness done to another, *we all of us drive past the thing we should stop for.* I understood it completely. That was the trouble. I've never once been able to make Iwan a villain, and I've tried, because it would be such a relief.

*

Here is the part I'd rather skip.

I could have stopped it. I want to be careful not to make that bigger than it was — a working pupil who tells a buyer's man the horse he's vetting has been *settled* is a working pupil who never gets another ride from anyone again, and knows it. It wasn't a clean choice between right and ruin. That's not the excuse; that's just the weather.

But I could have said something. To the navy groom on Thursday, polite, by the rail — *you might want your man to come back another morning, when nobody's helped him along.* Three words and a look and the whole thing falls down. Of everyone at that vetting I was the only one who could read, in the horse's body, the difference between a sound horse standing quiet and a frightened horse made to stand quiet. I was the only one who'd know.

And the reason I didn't — I've had ten years and I can give it to you straight, without the weather around it — the reason I didn't is that the sale completing was the only thing keeping my own ride alive.

Because that was the deal Caro had got, the thing I'd half-heard and not let myself add up. Raef bought the horse, but the horse jumped HOYS first, the 1.60 Grand Prix at Birmingham in October, under lights, with me on him — because a horse that wins a Grand Prix flies out worth more than a horse that doesn't, so the new owner wanted his investment shown off and finished by the one rider who could finish it before delivery. The sale and my Grand Prix were the same animal. If the vetting failed, the sale died. If the sale died, there was no October ride, no HOYS, no 1.60, no Grand Prix — the thing I'd walked downhill in the rain toward when I was nineteen, the thing the whole of me had wanted since before I had words for wanting, the *be somebody, be on the horse instead of holding it.* The vetting passing was the only road left to the only thing I'd ever asked the world for.

So Iwan would settle the horse, and the horse would go quiet — chemical-quiet, not trust-quiet, the perversion of the only clean thing my hands ever did — and the buyer's man would see a sweet brave sound animal and write it up, and the sale would complete, and the loan would be paid, and Saracen would be sold, and I would get to ride him in the Grand Prix on the way to handing him over, and the price of all of it, the entry fee, the thing I paid to keep my own shot alive, was that I kept my mouth shut.

That's all silence is. It's the cheapest currency there is. It doesn't even feel like spending. You just don't make a sound, and the world arranges itself in your favour, and you get to tell yourself you didn't *do* anything, you only failed to do a thing, and there's no court anywhere can see the difference, the same as there's no paper anywhere on what Caro promised me, the unwritten and the unsaid, the two cheapest, strongest leashes in the world.

I didn't decide it in a thunderclap. That's the other thing. I'd half hoped, telling this, that there'd be a moment I could point at, a fork, a night I lay awake and chose the bad road — because at least a choice like that is a *something*, at least the man who makes it is awake. But it wasn't like that. It was more like the flinch going out of me had gone — the way it had gone on a chestnut mare and then on a nappy gelding called Foxglove I'd ridden past the muck heap in four minutes and felt nothing and clocked the nothing too late — it was just that by July there was a thing I'd have flinched at in March, and the flinch wasn't there to do, and so I did the thing, which was nothing, and felt nothing where the something used to be, and went and got the horse in for the man with the needle.

*

Thursday was a clean bright morning, the kind a horse should be sound on, and he was.

I had him in early and did him over till he shone, the summer coat coming up dappled, and I plaited him, which you don't have to for a vetting but I did, because — I don't know why, because he looked his best plaited, because if a thing's going to be taken from you you want it taken at its best, the way Pip plaited Quillan in little tight rosebuds the morning the mare was shown and sold. I plaited him and I picked his feet and I ran my hand down each clean cold honest leg, and the buyer's man came, navy fleece, polite, the soft tape gone now, and Raef's own vet, and Iwan, and Caro in her mended coat standing very straight by the mounting block with her hands half-closed over the loose ring so nothing would fall off her.

And before all that, early, before any of them, in the half-light, Iwan had come to the box, just him, and gone in to Saracen with the soft cracked hands, and I'd held the horse, and the horse had let him, because the horse trusted me and I was holding the rope and telling him the thing I told him, *not yet, not while I'm here,* the long soft lie down the lead that he believed and chose — and Iwan had helped him along, just the once, just enough, and I held the rope while it happened and said the true-feeling thing into the near ear and watched the horse go quiet under my hands and under the chemistry both at once, the two quiets pouring into the same animal, mine and the needle's, and I could not, in the horse's body, tell you where one ended and the other began, and I have never been able to since.

He vetted sound. He vetted sweet. He stood for the flexions, and trotted up clean and even on the hard yard with no hitch, and stood in the dark stable for the light in his eyes, and stayed quiet for the strange hands all down his wrong side, the right side, the bad side, the side a man held too hard before any of us — stayed quiet for all of it, the cornered places, the no-run-at-it, the things that would have had him sideways out the gate in March — and the buyer's man nodded, and Raef's vet nodded, and Caro's hands came down off the ring an inch, and Iwan, kind Iwan, gentle Iwan, took the blood at the last stage, found the vein with his thumb the way you'd find a stud hole, drew it slow into the tube, and sealed it in front of the buyer's man, fair and square, the one honest thing in the whole bright morning, the sealed and dated truth of what we'd done, and labelled it, and put it in the cool box for the van.

And the sale completed. There, on the yard, on a clean July morning, on a handshake — there was a contract for the horse, paper for the horse, of course there was, you don't move a horse like that on a handshake, Caro'd said so herself; the paper was always there when the thing being moved was worth a number — and the only handshake with no paper under it anywhere in the world was still the one I'd taken in a cold kitchen in March, *board and coaching and eventually a horse, you have my word.*

*

I drove down to Leominster with Iwan in the grey Transit that afternoon, because the cool box of bloods had to go to the practice fridge and Caro wanted a wormer picking up and there was no diesel to spare for two trips, so I went along, and we didn't talk much, and the diesel knocked, and the *& SON* went by in shop windows reversed.

The practice was a cold breeze-block room behind the surgery with a fridge that hummed and a shelf of stored samples in it, rows of little tubes in their racks, dated in Iwan's careful hand, dozens of them, the bodies of dozens of horses sealed and held, the truth of every sale he'd ever passed sitting there in the cold, *it's there if anyone ever wants to look, nobody ever has cause, mostly.* He put Saracen's tube in the rack with the rest of them, and wrote the date, and shut the door, and the fridge hummed, and that was that.

I stood and looked at it through the glass before he shut it. I don't know why. I knew what it was. I'd helped make it. It was the one thing in the entire summer that couldn't be charmed or folded into a cardigan or shaken on or left unsaid — Saracen's blood, drawn honest, telling the exact thing his trotting-up wouldn't, the thing I'd watched poured into him at first light with my own hand on the rope, sitting in a rack in a humming fridge in a cold room in Leominster, dated, sealed, the loaded thing, the one true witness, and it would never have to say a word, that was the worst of it, it would just sit there being true while everything got away with everything, the way the stored blood mostly does.

It was the body that never lies, made to lie, and the proof of the making sealed up cold where it would never be looked at.

I looked at it.

And then I looked away — and that was the choice, that was the whole of it, the entire transaction of my own ruin done in one small movement of the eyes, no thunderclap, no fork in the road, just a boy in a cold room turning his eyes off the one true thing because the one true thing, looked at, would have cost him the only thing he'd ever wanted — and Iwan said, "Right, the wormer's out front," and I went out front, and we drove back up the lane in the knocking van, past everything we should have stopped for, and the horse, back at Tatefield, the chemical wearing off him now and the real horse coming back up into his eyes, stood at his door and watched the lane for the van, frightened again, looking for me, the near ear already tipping back before I was anywhere he could see.


---

# Chapter Twenty-One — Make Him Go Quiet
