I'd looked away from a fridge in Leominster on the Thursday and I was still looking away from it days later, which is the thing nobody tells you about looking away — you don't do it once and have it done. You do it again at the kettle and again on the muck heap and again every time the practice van's diesel knocks past the bottom gate, the little syllable of looking away, all day, like a stone you keep finding back in your pocket after you've thrown it in the river.

So when Marnie came across the yard on the Thursday evening with her hat already on and her chin set, I was glad of her, in the way you're glad of anything that gives your eyes somewhere else to be.

She had Beau on the end of a rope. He came after her at the dignified amble of a horse who has done everything twice and is owed a quiet retirement nobody in this valley could afford to give him — grey gone almost white round the eyes, the dapples faded out of him, sixteen-two of patience on four stiffening legs. Twelve years old and going on a hundred. He stopped when she stopped, the way the made ones do, parked himself square without being asked, and dropped his bottom lip, and looked at nothing.

"Will you watch me," Marnie said. Not a question, the way her grandmother did it, except in Marnie's mouth the trick didn't work, because you could hear the asking come through the not-asking, the seam showing.

The qualifier was eleven days off. That was the clock, for both of us, ticking under everything that week — Royal International, the back end of July, the class that turned a horse from a good horse into a Grand Prix horse or sent it home a good horse forever. I was riding Saracen in it because of a thing I wasn't thinking about. Marnie was riding Beau in it because Caro had entered her, which is a different sentence from *Marnie wanted to ride in it*, and we both knew that too.

"Course," I said. "Let me get the school sheet down."

"It's down. I put it down." She'd been planning this. White round the mouth already, and she hadn't got on yet.

*

The indoor was the warm dead light of late evening, the leak in the north corner gone to a steady drip into the bucket Glenys kept under it, the sound of it the only clock in there. Saracen was three boxes down the covered row, where I could hear him moving when he moved, which was a thing I'd started needing to be able to do, the way you keep a hand on a child in a crowd. He'd had his evening feed. He was all right. I could hear he was all right.

Marnie led Beau in and shut the door and stood by his shoulder doing up the throatlash she'd already done up.

"He stopped at the wall at Hickstead," she said. "You saw."

"He backed off it. You let him."

"I know I let him." She said it fast, before I could be gentle about it, which I wasn't going to be, because she'd had a lifetime of gentle and it had taught her nothing except that the gentleness meant she was hopeless. "Granny said the same. *You let him back off it.* As if I did it on purpose. As if there's a version of me in there who's deciding."

She got her foot in the iron and went up, neat enough, she could do the parts that were technique, she'd been taught by the best for seventeen years and it was all in there, the leg, the hand, the still upper body — and the second her seat hit the saddle the whole of it went wrong, the way it always went wrong, because the technique sat on top of the fear like a sheet over furniture and you could see the shape of the fear through it, every line.

Beau felt it come down through the saddle and lifted his head an inch, just an inch, and sighed.

"There," she said. "He's done it already and we haven't moved."

"He's not done anything. He sighed. He's an old man, he sighs."

"He felt me."

"Course he felt you," I said. "They feel everything. That's not the problem."

She walked him on round the school, sitting too forward, hands too busy, the contact going and coming, going and coming, and Beau did what a schoolmaster does, which is carry the rider anyway, forgive it, fill in — but I watched his ear, the near one, because I always watch the ear, and it wasn't tipping back to her to listen. It was just up, neutral, the ear of a horse who has stopped expecting the conversation to be worth having and is getting on with his job alone. He'd carried a hundred frightened children round a hundred schools. He carried her the same way. Kindly, and by himself.

She brought him back to me at the rail, both of them blowing slightly, neither of them having done anything.

"All right," she said. She'd come to the part she'd come for. I felt her arrive at it the way you feel a horse arrive at a fence it doesn't want — the little gather, the breath held. "I want you to tell me how you do it."

"How I do what."

"Don't." Sharp. "Please don't make me say it stupid. You know what you do. You get on Saracen — *Saracen*, who put two of Granny's pupils in hospital, who they were going to send to the meat man — and he goes quiet. He goes *quiet*, Dev. Under you. I've watched you do it all season. Everyone's watched. Granny talks about it at dinner like it's weather. And I can't" — her voice went, came back — "I can't make a twelve-year-old schoolmaster who's never refused anything in his life believe I'm worth jumping a wall for. So tell me how you do it. Teach me. Or — " and here it came, the naked thing, the bravest thing anyone said to me the whole rotten season — "or do it for me. Make him go quiet for me. So I can get round the qualifier and Granny can look at me once the way she looks at you."

The bucket dripped in the north corner.

I'm not going to be clever about this part either. I've been clever about a lot of it because clever is the gear I go to when I don't want to feel where I am, and you'll have noticed, ten years of it, the jokes coming in like outriders to keep you off the thing in the middle. But there wasn't a joke for this. A seventeen-year-old girl in last year's good coat had got onto the kindest horse in the yard and asked me, with her whole chest, to do to him the thing I'd spent the back half of the summer learning the name of, so that her grandmother would love her, and she'd asked it as a kindness, the way you'd ask someone to teach you to swim.

"Marnie." I didn't know what came after it. I said her name to buy a stride. "It doesn't — it's not a thing I do *to* him."

"Then what is it."

And that was the question. That was the whole book of the summer in three words out of a frightened girl, and I'd been ducking it on the muck heap and at the kettle and in front of a fridge in Leominster for a week, and here it was with a face on it, with Beau's grey patient face behind it and her white one in front, and there was nowhere left to put my eyes.

*

I tried to tell her the true thing. I'll give myself that much. For about a minute I tried.

"You watch the body," I said. "Not the — forget the schooling for a second. You watch where the worry lives. Every horse keeps its worry somewhere. Saracen keeps his on the right side, in his eye and his poll, because something happened to him on the right side before any of us knew him, and it never went away, it just learned to wait. You learn where a horse keeps it. And then you don't — " I stopped. This was where it got hard to say because it was where it got true. "You don't pull him past it. You go to the place where the worry lives, the second before it tips over, and you tell him he doesn't have to do it on his own. That's all it is. You make him a promise his body believes. *Not yet. Not while I'm here.* And he believes you, and the believing lets him do the thing his fear says he can't."

She was leaning down over Beau's shoulder, drinking it. Her hands had gone still for the first time since she'd mounted, which I noted, because it was the first thing all evening she'd done right and she'd done it by listening to me instead of riding.

"Yes," she said. "Yes. How do I make the promise."

And there it was — the catch in it, the thing she couldn't hear and I could, because I'd been hearing it about myself all summer.

"You'd have to mean it," I said.

"I do mean it. I want him to be all right."

"No." Gentle wasn't going to do it, and merciless wasn't either, so I went plain, which is the only gear I've got that doesn't lie. "Not *want him all right.* You'd have to mean it about *yourself.* You'd have to not need anything off him. The promise only works if you're not asking him to do it for you — if you're going to the worst second of him and you're staying there with nothing in your own hands. The second he feels you need him to be brave so your granny'll love you — and he will feel it, Marnie, he's feeling it now, that's why his ear won't come back to you — the second he feels the need in you, the promise turns into a demand wearing the promise's coat, and he knows the difference, they always know the difference, and then you're just another person hauling on him to get a thing you need."

She'd gone very still on him. Beau's near ear, all this while, had not once tipped back to her. It was tracking *me.* The horse in the school was listening to the boy at the rail and the girl on his back was carrying nothing, and she could feel it, I watched her feel it.

"So you're saying I can't," she said. Flat. The voice going dangerous.

"I'm saying it's not a trick I'm keeping in my pocket."

"You taught Saracen."

"I didn't *teach* him." The drip in the corner. "I gave him something. And I'll tell you what it cost, shall I, because nobody ever says this bit." I hadn't meant to say any of it. It came up out of the week, out of the fridge, out of the looking-away, and once it started I couldn't get my hand on it. "Every time he goes quiet for me, every clear round, that's not him getting braver. That's him spending. He's frightened the whole time — he's frightened in the air over the jump, the careful ones always are, that's why they're careful, they jump like that because they were hit for touching a pole — and he overrides it, he spends a thing he's got almost none of, *because I asked him to.* And the better I get at asking, the more he spends, and the more he spends the more he's worth, and the more he's worth — "

I stopped. I'd come right up to the edge of the thing I'd looked away from in a fridge and I shut my mouth on it like a gate.

But she'd heard the shape of it. She was clever, Marnie, that was the cruelty of her whole life, she was clever enough to know exactly what she didn't have.

"The more he's worth, the surer he's sold," she said.

I didn't answer that. I looked at the bucket.

"That's why you won't teach me," she said, and her voice had gone somewhere I didn't like, somewhere her grandmother went, low and certain. "It's not that you can't. It's that you know what it is and you don't want me to have it. You want to be the only one. The Sheffield boy who feels it. *Your sort always have a feel for them* — that's what my father said about you, did you know that, at the dinner — and you sat there and let him, you let him make you the magic council-estate horse boy, because being the only one who can do it is the one thing in this whole place that's yours. You haven't even got a wage. You haven't got a contract. You've got *the gift.* And you won't share it with the one person here who's drowning worse than you, because then you'd be nobody."

*

It went in clean, the way the true cruel things do. She'd reached straight past the technique and the class and the seventeen years of being taught, and she'd found the soft hungry place — *being the only one* — and she'd put her leg on it, and God help me she'd ridden me, she'd done the family thing, she was a Wexley-Tate after all, she'd found where the worry lived and gone to it.

And the worst of it was she was half right. Not the way she meant. There *was* a thing I wasn't sharing — but it wasn't a trick I was hoarding to stay special. It was that I knew what the gift did to the one going quiet, and I'd just spent a week proving I'd use it on a horse I loved for money that wasn't even mine, and I could not — I could *not* — hand that to a frightened girl and call it a kindness. You can't teach someone to override another creature's fear without teaching them, in the same breath, to override their own conscience. That's the whole thing. That's the no-line. I'd found it on a chestnut mare in June and I'd been finding it ever since, cheaper each time, and what she was asking me for, with her chin up and her whole chest, was the cheapness. She wanted me to make it easy for her. To skip the part where it costs you something to learn how to make it cost a horse something.

And I couldn't tell her that, because to tell her that I'd have had to say the rest of it out loud, the fridge, the silence, what I'd traded and who I'd traded it for — and I wasn't going to. So I let her think I was hoarding.

"Get off him," I said.

"Don't tell me what to —"

"Marnie. Get off him and let me show you one true thing. Not the gift. A real thing you can use. Then you'll see I'm not keeping anything."

She got off. She landed badly, jarred, didn't care. She handed me the reins and Beau turned his old white head to me and breathed out, long, down his nose, and his near ear came back the second I had the rein, the second the worry on his back was gone, and Marnie saw it, she saw the ear come back to me that had stayed away from her the whole evening, and something closed in her face. I'd not meant to do it. I'd meant to help. But the horse did it for me, the horse told the truth the way the honest body always tells the truth at the worst possible second, and the truth was *not her, him*, and there was no taking it back.

*

I put my hand flat on Beau's neck, where the old grey hairs were, and I didn't do anything special. I want that down. There wasn't anything special, and I'd have given a good deal not to be the one proving it in front of her. I walked him three strides and halted him off my seat and he halted square the way he always did, and I picked up a soft contact and I just — let him be carried, instead of carrying. I gave him the thing he hadn't had under her, which was a rider with nothing in her hands. And he sighed again, but it was the other sigh, the one that goes down instead of up, and his back came up under the saddle a fraction, lending it, the way the kind ones do when they decide you're worth lending to — and it was a small cruelty to make him do it in front of her, and I'd done it anyway, the way I did everything anyway by then.

"That," I said. "That's not a gift. That's just — you take the need out of your hands and he fills the space. He *wants* to. He's spent twelve years wanting to. Nobody's let him in years because everybody who sits on him needs something off him."

"I can't take the need out," she said, from the rail, very quietly now. The fight gone out of her. "I don't know how to want nothing. I've never been allowed to want nothing. There's always — the name, and Granny, and being the one who lost it, and being the disappointment, and — I can't get on a horse and want nothing, Dev. There isn't a single thing in my whole life I'm allowed to do badly. Even this. *Especially* this."

And there she was. The same frightened animal across the widest gap there is — somebody who'd been told they were special so they'd work for free, who could not get up in the morning and want nothing because their whole keeping depended on being worth something. A forty-thousand-pound surname with no money behind it, and me in half-chaps gone grey at the seams, both up to our necks in the same cold water and there wasn't enough of it for either of us.

"I know," I said. Plain. "I know you can't. That's the bit I couldn't say. It's not that I won't teach you. It's that I can't teach you the part that matters, because the part that matters is wanting nothing off the horse, and neither of us has ever in our lives been allowed to want nothing. We were both made the same way. That's why I'm the only one who can ride Saracen. Not because I'm magic. Because I know exactly what it is to be a frightened thing that has to be useful to be kept. He recognises it. He recognises *me.*"

She looked at me. Down the school the leak dripped into the bucket. Three boxes along, Saracen shifted his weight, and I heard it, and I didn't take my eyes off her, and that was its own confession.

"Then we're both going to fail the qualifier," she said.

"You might not."

"Don't." But there was no heat in it now. "Don't do the kind thing. The kind thing is the worst one, you know that. Granny does the kind thing. You just did the kind thing to a horse and it broke my heart. Don't do it to me as well."

*

She got back on him. I didn't tell her to and I didn't tell her not to. She got back on Beau because she didn't know what else to do with herself, and she picked up the reins, and her hands came up an inch, and her heels came up with them, and Beau felt the need flood back down into him through the saddle and lifted his head the inch, exactly the inch, and his near ear went up and neutral and stopped looking for her, and she rode him at a cross-pole I'd set earlier in the corner, a thing he'd have jumped in his sleep with a child on him, and three strides out I saw her decide not to, the way you always see them decide it before they do, and Beau felt her decide it, and he ran out, gently, almost apologetically, an old honest horse declining a fence he could have left up if she'd believed it — and his ear came back at the last second, came right back, looking for somebody to tell him it was all right.

Looking for me. At the rail. Not for her, on his back.

"Sit up and try again," I said. "Just be boring. Don't ask him for brave. Bore him to death. He'll jump for boring."

It was true, and it was useless, and I knew it was useless as I said it. She walked him round in the dead light to come at the cross-pole again, white round the mouth, her hands already loud, and Beau carried her by himself, the way he'd carried everyone, the way you carry a thing you can't reach, and his ear stayed tipped back to me, the boy at the rail who had nothing in his hands and couldn't get into the saddle to use it, and the bucket dripped in the corner, and neither one of them was saved.


---

# Chapter Twenty-Two — The Qualifier

Marnie left Beau's ear flicking back to her in an empty school, and three days later I loaded a different horse onto a lorry to go and win the thing I'd already paid for.

I want to be precise about the order of it, because the order is the whole of what I've got to answer for. Saracen had passed his vetting the Tuesday before. He'd passed it the way a frightened horse passes a vetting it should have failed, which is to say something had been put into him to make a body that runs from a man with a stethoscope stand still and let the man flex its hocks and listen to its heart and call it sound. I'd been in the box for it. I'd said nothing. That was the deal, and nobody had ever written the deal down, and that is how I knew it was real — the realest things in that yard were the ones nobody would put on paper. My silence kept the sale alive. The sale kept the ride alive. And the ride was the Royal International, the qualifier, 1.50m on the Hickstead grass in late July, a placing in which sent you to Birmingham in October, to the Grand Prix, to the round I'd been climbing toward since the bus put me down at a crossroads in March.

So: I'd traded a thing I couldn't see the shape of for a thing I could. I knew the price. I loaded him up at four in the morning with the diesel light already on, and Glenys handed me the flask through the cab window without a word, and we went south down the M5 in the dark, and I told myself the lie I'd got good at telling — that I'd worry about the rest of it after. There's always an after you can fold things into. That's the trick of the whole sport. You ride the next fence and you sort your soul out in the lorry park.

*

The thing about Hickstead in International week is that it stops being a horse show and becomes a small floating nation, and you arrive in it the way you'd arrive in a country you can't afford the visa for. The Derby Bank stood up green and absurd against the white stands. There were striped marquees and a man in a blazer with a list, and the lorry park was a county of chrome again, and we nosed the old box in at the cheap end between an outfit from County Cork and Tomas Berg's touring bus, which had its own awning and a generator that didn't knock and a groom power-washing a ramp you could have eaten off.

I got Saracen off. And here is the first thing I have to tell you straight, because it's the thing the chapter is about.

He came down the ramp wrong.

Not bad. Not the sideways half-ton terror of the first morning in March — I'd never see that again, I'd schooled it out of him stride by stride and given him a door every time the world tried to wall him up. He came down quiet. Too quiet. He came down the ramp the way a man walks who's had two drinks before noon, careful and a half-beat slow, his big careful feet finding the metal one at a time, his eye soft in a way his eye was never soft, the white not showing in the corner where the white always showed when there was a strange place around him.

I stood at his near shoulder with my thumb already on the scar, where my thumb went without my deciding it, and I read him the way I read everything, by the body, and the body was lying to me.

I want to say it plainly. He felt lovely. He felt like the easiest horse in the world. And that was the lie. That was exactly the lie. The thing they'd put in him to get him through the vetting hadn't worn off, or they'd topped it up for the journey, or — I didn't know, I made myself not know, the not-knowing was a muscle I'd built all season and it was strong now. What I knew was that the horse under my hand was a horse with the fear turned down at a tap somewhere I couldn't reach, and a horse with the fear turned down is a horse you cannot hear when he asks. The whole gift, the only thing I had, the thing they fed me at a table for and would not pay me for — the whole of it was *hearing him ask.* And they'd muffled the asking so he'd sell.

"He's a bit dozy off the box," I said to Glenys.

"Long way in the dark," she said. She didn't look at me when she said it. She got busy with the travel boots, undoing the velcro she'd already undone, and I understood that she knew exactly what I knew and that we were going to do the thing we both did, which was hold the knowing between us and not let it out into the air where it could be a fact.

*

I walked the course at two, jacket off, in the heat that had come up off the grass. Tomas walked it the same time and we fell in together the way you do, two strides apart, not talking, both of us counting the lines off our own legs.

The course-builder had built clever, not big. That's the cruelty of the good ones — they don't beat you with height, they beat you with a question you have to answer in the half-second between two fences. There was a treble down the far side that walked a true distance and rode short if you didn't carry your canter, and a Liverpool under an oxer at fence six that would back a horse off, and then the thing I'd come to hate before I jumped it, the thing I clocked from forty yards off and went cold about — a wall, blocks stacked to look like masonry, set on a dogleg turn into the corner where the members' enclosure pressed in close to the rope.

A wall he couldn't see past. On a turn he couldn't read. Into a corner. With a crowd. Somebody had built Saracen's exact nightmare into the qualifier without knowing his name, the way the world does, by accident, all the time.

"The wall," Tomas said. He'd stopped beside it and was looking at the turn the way I was. "On this one, you will want to be a stride sooner than feels right. The ground falls off here, see, after the bank. They will all get there long and have it down behind."

"Cheers," I said.

He looked at me a second. He had the palest eyes I've ever seen on a man and nothing at all behind them, and I'd decided weeks ago that he was the happiest person at every show because he'd had something taken out of him I still had in, a thing that hurt to keep. He nodded at the wall. "Your horse," he said. "He is very quiet today."

"He's settled."

"Mm," said Tomas Berg, and that was all, and it was worse than anything Glenys had not-said all morning, because Tomas had no reason to lie to me and no reason to spare me, and *mm* from a man like that is a verdict. He walked on and paced the treble and left me at the wall on my own, reading the ground that fell off after the bank, working out where I'd have to ask my horse for the brave thing on a day his body couldn't tell me whether the brave thing was killing him.

*

I'm not going to be clever about the warm-up. There's nothing clever in it.

I took him into the collecting ring — the chute, two stands close on either side and the crowd hung over the rail on both, his worst kind of place, the kind he'd melt down in on a normal day, the kind I'd spend twenty minutes talking him through, giving him the right rein away an inch at a time, opening doors. And he walked into it like it was a field. He walked into the chute of close crowd and standing horses and his ears stayed level and soft and the white didn't come up in his eye and a part of me that I'm ashamed of now went *thank God,* went *this is easy,* because every other time it had cost me everything I had to get him into that place still listening, and today it cost me nothing, because there was no him to talk to. The horse who'd have been frightened was somewhere under the chemical, with his hands over his ears.

I trotted him a circle and asked for the canter and he gave it like silk. I popped him over the practice cross-pole and a groom put up an oxer and I jumped that, and he jumped it careful, the front legs snapping neat up to his chin the way they always did, the apology of a horse who'd been hit for a pole once a long time ago and decided never again — and that, at least, that was still his, the carefulness was his own and not theirs, they couldn't reach down and pull that out of him, and I held onto it. He still jumped like Saracen. He just wasn't asking me anything while he did it.

Three strides off the practice oxer I watched for the flicker out of long habit. The stillness, the stopped blink, the small fast tic under the right eye — *the last second the horse is still asking for help, do I do this on my own or are you here* — I watched for it the way you'd watch for a friend's face in a crowd you'd been told he was in.

It didn't come.

He just jumped. No question came up the rein for me to answer. He went over the oxer the way a machine goes over an oxer, clean and high and dead, and landed, and I sat there in the warm-up at Hickstead with the thing I'd wanted all season, and have never in my life felt more like a thief.

Glenys was at the rail. I rode over to her.

"He's not himself," I said. Quiet. Just to her.

She put her hand up on his neck. He let her. He let everyone, today. "No," she said. And then, because she was the only one who ever told me the truth the once and never twice: "You'll ride him anyway."

"Yeah."

"Aye." She took the unlit roll-up out from behind her ear and looked at it and put it back. "Then ride him careful. Whatever's in him, it'll not tell you when he's tired. You'll have to know for him." She looked at me, the walnut face, the pity that had been in it since the first day and had cost her something to keep showing me. "You've always known for him. Today you'll have to know *harder.* That's all I'll say."

That was all she said.

*

I was drawn forty-second of fifty-eight. Long afternoon to wait. I watched the wall do exactly what Tomas said it would — horse after horse came off the bank a touch long, met the wall reaching, and had the top block off behind, the soft dead tumble of the blocks, four faults, the rider's shoulders dropping. The members' enclosure clapped politely each time, the way you clap a thing you came to watch fail.

Tomas went thirtieth. He rode the wall a stride sooner than felt right, exactly as he'd told me he would, and his horse left it up by a foot and he went clear and didn't change his face. Cost him nothing. He came out of the ring and patted his horse twice on the neck, a metronome, and that was the whole of his joy, and I watched him do it and felt the thing I'd been refusing to feel all season come a half-step closer: that this — the flat hand, the dead even face, the clear round that took nothing out of you — was the only safe way to do it, and I was a long way down the road toward it, and I hadn't packed for the journey.

Marnie went too, on Beau, in a smaller class running off the same arena earlier, and I'd watched it from the lorry because I couldn't watch it from the rail, couldn't stand where she'd see me watch. She came to the wall — they put a wall in everything, that summer, it felt like — and she went white round the mouth at it, I could see the white from sixty yards, and her hands came up, and Beau, the kindest horse in the valley, the old honest schoolmaster who'd carried a hundred children over a thousand fences, felt her decide not to and decided not to with her, and stopped. Just stopped, square in front of it, the way an honest horse stops when his rider's already left him. Four faults. The bell. She circled and got him over it the second time, both of them shaking, and finished, and you couldn't have qualified a saint off that round.

I'd stood at her rail three days before and not been able to give her the thing she asked me for. *Make him go quiet the way you do.* I couldn't. Not because I was keeping it from her, though God knows I'd kept it from her once for less reason. I couldn't because the thing she wanted wasn't a trick you teach, it was a thing you do to a body, and I'd only just found that out myself, found it out the hard way, with my own hands, and I wasn't going to be the one to put it in hers. So I'd said something true and useless at the rail and watched her not be saved, and now I watched her not be saved again, in public, and the worst of it was the thought that came up in me cold and unbidden and would not be pushed down:

*I could fix Beau in a fortnight. I could make that old horse go quiet under her and she'd qualify and her grandmother would look at her once the way she looks at me.*

And I knew, watching her circle her honest pony past the wall the second time, that I'd thought of doing it as a kindness. That's how it gets you. It always comes dressed as a kindness. It had got Iwan dressed as a kindness, *we'll just help him along, see how he goes.* It had got Caro dressed as a kindness, *there'll be a horse, you have my word.* And now it was at my own door dressed as a kindness, and I had spent the whole season learning that the kindness and the using were the same hand, and I still wanted to do it, because doing it felt like love and I have never been able to tell love from use, not in her, not in Caro, not in myself, not in anything.

I didn't go to her after. I told myself there wasn't time. There was time.

*

Then they called the forties and I got on him.

Glenys legged me up and ran her thumb down each leg one last time the way she always did, feeling for heat, for the truth in the leg that a horse can't hide and a syringe can — and there was no heat, and I didn't let myself wonder what else there was no heat to feel — and she squared my number on my back and slapped my boot, which was her whole blessing, and I rode him down to the in-gate.

The bell went. Forty-five seconds. The first fence already coming.

I'd love to tell you it was a fight. It wasn't. That's the horror I keep trying to make people understand and can't, because everyone wants the story where the doped horse fights you and you wrestle him round and it's tragic and loud. It wasn't loud. It was the quietest round I ever rode. He came off the bank and met the treble and I sat up the way Tomas said and we fitted the strides in clean and his front legs snapped neat and apologetic over every element, and the Liverpool came and the blue water sat under the oxer where it should have made him spook down, and he didn't even look at it, he jumped it flat and high and dead, and I felt the wrongness of that in my own body — *he should be frightened of that, that's water, he hates water, where's he gone* — and I rode the wrongness the way I'd learned to ride everything, by not letting it into my hands.

And then the wall, on the dogleg, off the bank, into the corner, the crowd close.

This was the place. This was always the place. Every show all season, this was the fence where I earned whatever I was. Where the real Saracen would go still and stop blinking and the tic would come under the right eye and the question would come up the rein into my hand — *that. I can't see past that. There's a corner. Are you here* — and I would give him the right rein away soft, like opening a gate I was holding for him, and I would wait the half-stride longer than his fear wanted me to wait, and he would blow out through his nose, that long low flutter, and decide to trust the next ten seconds, and *go,* careful, brave, borrowing the courage off me because he kept almost none of his own.

I came round the dogleg ready for the flicker. I had my hand soft on the right rein, ready to give it, ready to be there, ready for the question.

And the question didn't come.

He turned the corner toward the wall he couldn't see past, into the close crowd, the exact arrangement of the world that had nearly killed two pupils once and put a third off horses for life — and he felt *nothing,* or he felt it through a wall of his own, muffled, far off, a man hearing his own name shouted through a closed door and deciding not to answer. No stillness. No tic. No held breath. He just kept cantering at the wall like it was a gate left open in a field he'd known his whole life, and I had my hand ready to give him a thing he wasn't asking for, and there was nobody at the other end of the rein to give it to.

I jumped the wall on a horse who wasn't there.

It was the cleanest the wall ever jumped under me. He left it up by a hand he didn't need, careful as ever, neat front legs, the apology — and we landed, and the crowd made the warm sound a crowd makes, and there were two fences left and we jumped them, and the bell went, and the clock stopped, and the board said it.

Clear.

Clear round at Hickstead, 1.50, on a condemned horse, in the qualifier for the Grand Prix, and not one fence of it had cost me a single thing, because the horse I'd spent my whole self learning to hear had been put somewhere I couldn't hear him, so that he'd sell, and I'd ridden him round there, and I'd said *thank you* for the ride.

*

I want to tell you that the not-feeling was the thing that broke me. It wasn't, not then. That came later. What I felt in the ring, riding out through the in-gate with the qualification on the board and the announcer saying my name — D. Okafor-Lyle, Tatefield — and the polite members' clap and Caro coming down off the rail with her face lit up, was nothing where the something used to be, and worse than that, I didn't even clock the nothing. I felt it as skill. I felt good. I'd ridden the most frightening horse in England round his exact nightmare and felt good about my striding.

Caro reached me at the gate and put her hand on my boot, then high on Saracen's neck, then on my knee, and her face had the water standing in the old hard eyes, the real water, and she said, "*There* it is. That's qualified. That's the one that takes you to Birmingham," and I heard the love in it, I'd swear to you on the gold-cracked cup that the love was real, and I also heard — woven through it so you could not get a blade between the two — the other thing, the thing that wasn't *takes you* but *takes him,* the sum coming right at last, the October figure with the comma in the wrong place getting one fence closer to paid. Both. Always both, with her. I'd given up trying to pull them apart. You can't pull them apart in her because you can't pull them apart in the thing itself.

"Beautifully ridden," she said, and meant it, and it was, and it was the worst riding I've ever done in my life, and both of those are true, and that is the whole of what I learned that season and I learned it standing on a horse who wasn't there while a woman I loved told me I was clever.

Tomas qualified too. Cleanly. Cost him nothing — but for Tomas it cost nothing because there'd never been anything in him to cost, and for me it cost nothing because I'd had the thing taken out of me a coin at a time and called the taking *getting better.* Same clear round. Different empty.

*

It was past nine when the calm started to wear off him.

I was in the box, picking out the off-hind with the little ring of white round the coronet, the one foot of his four that wore the band of light, and I felt the change come up through him before I heard it. A horse tells you everything if you've a hand on him. The muscle came back into his neck first, a tightening, the slack going out of it. Then his head came up an inch off the haynet and he stopped chewing and held still, properly still, alert-still, his real stillness and not their counterfeit of it, and his eye — I came round to his head and his eye had the white back in the corner of it, the white that meant he was in the world again and the world was a strange dark place full of strange horses and he didn't know where he was.

He'd come back. Whatever it was, it was going out of him with the night, and the real horse was rising back up into his own body the way the tide comes back into a place that was dry, and he was frightened, the way he was always frightened, the way they'd paid Iwan to make him not be for one afternoon so a man could buy him for a sum that would clear a roof — and he found me. His eye went round the strange box and it found me at his near shoulder with my thumb on the scar, and he steadied on me the way he'd steadied from the first morning, and gave me his fear to hold, the way he'd always given it to me, because in the whole of the strange roaring world I was the one thing he knew was safe.

I stood there and let him give it to me. I put my hand flat on the wet warm of his neck and he leaned the weight of his head toward me the smallest amount, the way he did, asking nothing, only checking I was still the door he could put his back to.

And I'd spent his borrowed courage on a lie that afternoon, every clean careful fence of it, ridden a counterfeit of him round his nightmare to confirm the sale that was taking him away — and he stood in the dark and trusted my hands completely, the very hands, and there was nobody to tell him, and I drove the empty box back up the M5 in the dark with the diesel light on and the thing qualified that I had wanted my whole life.


---

# Chapter Twenty-Three — The Body That Lies

The chemical wore off Saracen in the box on the Sunday morning and the real horse came back into his eyes, frightened, and put his near ear back through the bars looking for me, and I went to him, because I had taught him I was a thing worth looking for, and I stood with my hand on his neck while he came back into himself and trembled at nothing, and I thought: *I did this.* Not the standing-here. The other. I'd ridden him round a 1.50m the day before with a falseness in him I could feel through the saddle like a stone in your boot you keep walking on, and we'd gone clear, and qualified, and the board said so, and the yard had eaten on it, and nobody on God's earth knew but me and the horse and a vet in Leominster who couldn't look at any of us.

So I want to be plain about what kind of morning it was before the thing happened. It was the kind where you've got everything you wanted and you can't put your tongue to a single bit of it without tasting the lie.

We were still at the show. The qualifier weekend ran a third day — Sunday classes, the smaller money, the also-rans, the horses you brought to be seen rather than to win, and a 1.30m for the made horses going down a grade to find their feet again. Caro had entered Beau in it.

She'd entered Beau because Beau had a price and Beau wasn't bringing it. The cataract in the off eye had got round the dealers the way those things do, phone to phone, and a buyer who'd been keen in May had gone quiet in July, and a grey schoolmaster of twelve who can't pass a thorough vet without a *with reservations* on him is a horse the market starts to walk past with its hands in its pockets. So you take him to the Royal International, where the right people stand at the right rails, and you put a careful child on him over a kind track, and you get him *seen* going well, and you remind the world he can still do it. You put him in the shop window one more time.

Marnie was the careful child.

She didn't want to. I knew she didn't want to before she said a word, because I'd seen her get on him in the lorry park and her hands had gone up an inch the second her seat touched the saddle, and Beau had felt it and dropped his head, kind old thing, the way he always did, taking the worry off her, doing her job for her like he'd done it for thirty children before her. He was the most honest horse in the yard and he spent half his life carrying the fear of the people on top of him so they didn't have to. That was what he was for. That was what made him a schoolmaster and that was what was going to be the death of him, though I didn't know that yet, standing at the rail with the order of go in my hand and my own horse three boxes back coming down off a drug.

"He's lame," Marnie said. She'd ridden over to me at the collecting ring. White round the mouth. "Behind. The off-fore. Something. I can feel it."

I'd schooled enough horses to know that *I can feel it, behind, the off-fore, something* is the sentence of a frightened rider looking for a door out, and I half-thought that was all it was. Half. Glenys had run the off-fore cold with the hose all spring; there'd been filling in it in June; he was twelve and stiff in the mornings and he warmed out of it, he always warmed out of it. That's a thing you say. *He'll warm out of it.* It is the most dangerous sentence in the sport and I have heard it said over a great many horses and I have said it myself, and I said it then.

"Walk him a bit more," I said. "Get him in front of your leg. He'll warm out of it."

She looked at me. There was a thing in her face I'd put there in the school three days before, when she'd asked me to give her what I had and I'd given her something true and useless instead, and she hadn't forgiven me and I hadn't asked her to. She'd asked me to make Beau go quiet for her. I'd told her you can't transfer it, which was true, and I hadn't told her the rest, which was that I wouldn't have wanted to, that I'd come to be frightened of the very thing she was begging me for. So now she was standing in front of me asking me whether her horse was sound, which is a kind of asking-me-to-go-quiet too, and I did to her what everyone did to everyone in that place. I gave her the comfortable answer because the uncomfortable one would have cost the round, and the round was the shop window, and the shop window was a buyer, and a buyer was the roof.

*He'll warm out of it.* My hands didn't even hesitate handing it over. The flinch had been gone out of me a long time by then.

Caro came up on Marnie's other side. She had her competition face on, which was her ordinary face with the warmth turned down to a working temperature. "He's stiff because he's twelve and the going's deep," she said. "Ride him forward and he'll be a different horse over the first three. Don't let him back off. You always let him back off." She put her hand flat on Beau's neck, just once, and there was love in the hand, real love, fifty years of it, this horse she'd bred or near enough, this last reliable thing — and there was also a sum being done behind her eyes, the cataract and the market and the comma in the loan letter, and you could not have got a knife between the two. I'd stopped trying. "Off you go, darling. Show them what he is."

What he was. He was an old horse who couldn't pass a vet and a frightened girl who couldn't ride and a yard that needed a number neither of them could make, and we were all about to send them into a ring to lie about all three at once.

*

Here is the thing I have never been able to make right in ten years.

I could read it. I could read the whole of it. I read the off-fore at the walk and told myself it was nerves. I read Marnie's hands and told myself she'd settle. I read Beau dropping his poll to carry her and called it a schoolmaster doing his job instead of an old soldier being marched out one more time on a leg that was telling everyone who'd listen, in the only language a horse has, which is the body. The body that never lies. He was lying to no one. He was saying it plainly, the off-fore, *not this, not today,* and three of us heard him and three of us decided, kindly, for love and for money and because the alternative was a conversation we couldn't afford, that we knew better than his body did.

And then we sent him in.

I went and stood at the rail. I didn't have to — I had Saracen to see to, the real horse coming back frightened in the box — but I went and stood at the rail because some part of me that was still nineteen and still had something left in it knew it should watch, that you don't get to set a thing in motion and then walk off to your own horse while it happens. I owed Beau that much. I'd owe him a great deal more by the end of the afternoon and never be able to pay any of it.

The bell went. Marnie circled. She crossed the start a half-beat late, fussing him, and the clock came on, and Beau picked up his careful old canter and went down to the first, a plain upright, and jumped it the way he jumped everything, neat and willing and a fraction stiff behind, and landed, and I watched the off-fore take the weight on landing and I watched something in it not come back up quite level, a hitch, the smallest thing, the thing you see in a flexion test and pretend you didn't.

Two, a brush oxer, clean. Three, the same. He was doing it. Marnie's hands had come down. She'd believed him for a second the way you do when an honest horse takes the fear off you, and I watched her start to ride, properly ride, the thing she'd wanted all season, and I had time to think *there, that's it, that's all it ever was, just believing him,* and to feel something almost like gladness for her —

and then four was the wall.

It wasn't even a big wall. A 1.30m wall, blocks, the kind Beau had jumped a thousand of. But it was on a turn off the far end, and the going was cut up to soup down there where the morning classes had churned it, and she came round the corner and the wall was a beat closer than she'd walked it, and her hands went up that inch, and Beau, honest to the last, honest in the worst possible second, did the thing he was bred and beaten and loved into doing: he took her fear and he tried anyway. He stood off it to save her a stride. He launched off the bad leg onto the soup to be brave for a frightened child the way he'd been brave for frightened children his whole life.

And the leg went.

*

There is a sound. I'd never heard it and I've never stopped hearing it. It is not loud. That's the thing nobody tells you. You think catastrophe is loud and it isn't, it's a single soft wrong note in the middle of a great deal of ordinary noise, a sound like a wet branch, there and gone, and then the noise carries on for a half-second before the silence comes down on top of it like a roof falling.

He came down on the far side of the wall. Not the clean tumble of a horse that's had a pole. He came down the way a thing comes down when the engine has gone out of it mid-air, all wrong, the great body folding where a body doesn't fold, and Marnie went off over his shoulder and rolled and was up, was up and unhurt, and I have thanked God for that one mercy every day since and I have also never quite forgiven the smallness of it against the rest.

Beau didn't get up.

He tried. That was the worst. He was an honest horse to the end of it and he tried to do the one last thing a horse believes it must do, which is get its feet under it and stand, because a horse on the ground is a dead horse in the long memory of the species, and he heaved himself half up on the front and the off-fore wasn't there to take him, wasn't anything any more, and he came back down. And he lay in the churned ground with his honest old head up and his ears going and he looked round for someone to tell him what to do, the way he'd spent thirty years telling children what to do, and there was no one. There was a ring gone silent and a fence judge running and a girl on her knees in the mud with both hands over her mouth and three thousand people who'd come to watch horses be beautiful suddenly watching the other thing, the thing underneath the beautiful, the bill.

I was over the rail before I'd decided to be. There were stewards and there were better hands than mine and none of it mattered; I got to his head and I knelt in the soup and I put my hands on his face, the off cheek, the eye with the cloud starting in it, and I did the only thing I knew how to do, the thing that was supposed to be the good thing, my whole gift, the thing they'd taken me off a council estate for. I went quiet at his head and I told him with my hands that he wasn't alone in the worst second. *I'm here. You're not on your own. I've got you.*

And it was a lie. It was the exact lie I'd built my life on. Because being there with my hands didn't fix his leg and didn't undo the morning and didn't unsay *he'll warm out of it,* and the only thing my going-quiet did, the only thing it had ever done, was keep him still and trusting and unfrightened while the people who needed something from his body decided what came next. I'd done it to Saracen at a wall to win a placing. I'd done it to Quillan at the water to close a sale. And now I was doing it to a broken old horse to keep him calm for the green screens, and it worked, it always worked, his ear came round to me and stayed, the near one, *do I have to do this on my own, or are you here,* and I said *here,* and I was, I was here, and it was the most useless and the most necessary thing in the world and I have never been able to pull those two apart either.

Iwan came across the ring at a heavy run, a man not built for running, the wax jacket dark across the shoulders as if he'd driven through his own private rain to get here. He went down on the other side of the head and his hands went over the off-fore once, quick, gentle, professional, and he didn't say anything, and his not-saying was the worst thing said all day. He looked at the leg and he looked at me across the horse's neck and his kind sunk eyes had the whole thing in them — the regime, the *helping him along,* the silence we'd all kept, the blood in the foam in the fridge in Leominster that could be drawn out and tested and would tell its own true story if anyone ever wanted it to, and nobody ever would, because the people who could ask were the people it would damn. He looked at me and I looked at him and neither of us said the thing, and that was the contract. That was the only paper any of us ever signed.

"Hold his head, son," he said, soft, Welsh, the consonants gone soft as everything in him had gone soft. "That's it. You're doing the kindest thing in the ring, you keep doing it. Keep him with you now."

So I kept him with me. While they ran the screens out on their poles — green, the screens were green, a flat municipal green, and I thought, God help me, with the part of the mind that won't stop reading even then, of the tarp on the indoor roof the first day, the blue-green plastic snapping in the wind, the bones of money and no money, and here it was again, the same colour, stretched round the bones of a body this time, the cheapest material there is put up to hide the most expensive thing a yard has, which is a horse with no more use in it. They put the screens up so the crowd can't see and the horse can't see out and goes quiet in the dark. He cornered himself one last time without meaning to, the screens his walls, and I knelt inside the green dark with him and my hand on the cloudy eye and the off cheek and I told him the lie and he believed me, he believed me right to the end, the near ear tipped back and home on the sound of my voice, because that was the thing I'd taught him to do and the thing I'd taught every horse to do and the thing that had been done to me first, before I had words for it, in a flat in Sheffield: *be still, it'll be all right, I'm here.* The love and the leverage. The same hands. The same breath. I had never in my life been able to tell which one I was giving, and I gave it to him anyway, all of it, while a drowning man did the necessary thing on the other side of his neck and the ring stayed silent and Marnie stood somewhere behind the green with her grandmother's arm around her, the two of them watching the cheapest screens in England go up around the last reliable thing the family had.

*

It was a long time before I came out from behind the screens. Nobody made me. There's a courtesy in that world for the ones who stay; they let you stay.

When I did come out the ring had been put back together — the wall rebuilt, the going raked, the order of go moving on, because the show is a clock and the clock does not stop for a body, it has never once stopped, that is the whole nature of the thing — and I walked back across the lorry park with the green still in my eyes and my hands still smelling of him and I did not feel like crying and I did not feel anything I could have put a name to, which I have since understood was its own answer. The flinch was gone. I'd let it go on purpose, a coin at a time, all season. You don't get to choose which days it stays gone.

And then I heard him.

Saracen, three boxes down the line in Tatefield's rusted old box, had got the smell of it on the air — blood, and fear, and the particular reek of a horse that's been opened up — and he was calling, the high broken thing a frightened horse does that isn't a whinny and isn't a scream but is asking the same question over and over into the empty afternoon, *where are you, where are you, are you here,* and slamming his quarters into the partition, one slow strike and a pause and another, the same patience I'd heard the first night through a caravan window, the worst patience in the world.

I went to him. Of course I went to him. I'd built my whole life out of being a thing worth going to.

He was wet through with it, sweat-dark down the near-black neck, the white of his eye showing in the corner, and when he saw me the calling stopped, and he stood there shaking and trusting me with everything he had, looking to me to make the bad smell go away the way I'd always made the bad things go away. I put my hand on the scar on his shoulder, the one nobody could read, the one fact in him I'd never been able to spin or sell or lie about, and he dropped his poll a degree and blew out, long and low.

He didn't know. That's the plain part. He didn't know I was the reason for the smell. He didn't know what my hands were. He stood in the rust and the fading light and believed the lie, and I let him, because there was nothing else to give him and because I wanted it, God forgive me, I wanted the one body in the world that didn't know what I was, and Beau was already cooling in the green dark behind us, and somewhere across the lorry park a man with a soft voice was already drawing up the form that would take this one too.

I told him he was all right.

He was the only one I never had paper on, and the only one who'd have signed.


---

# Chapter Twenty-Four — Sheffield

He called for me from the lorry that night for an hour after the screens had gone up over the other one, and I stood in the dark with my hand flat on the partition where his quarters were and let him put his nose through the gap and breathe the panic out against my fleece, the near ear searching the dark for me, finding me, tipping back, *you're here, you're here* — and I was here, and I was the thing that had done it, and the horse did not know the difference. The smell of the other horse was still on the wind. Saracen could read it the way I read hands. He didn't know what it was. I did. I stood there until he gave it up and dropped his poll and went quiet, and I told myself that going quiet under me was a mercy I gave him and not a thing I did to him, and I'd stopped believing it somewhere back down the season, and I gave it to him anyway, because it was all I had.

Then the phone went, up in the caravan, the cheap one, vibrating itself off the shelf above my bunk, and I climbed the hill in the dark to it, and the screen said *Mum*, and the time said ten past midnight, and I knew before I answered which call it was, because I'd been reading her down a phone since before I could read.

It wasn't the bright one. That was how I knew.

The bright one comes in loud, *I'm grand, love, never better, I'm having a lovely week* — the bright one she'd rung me with in May, after Yorkshire, the bright one with the ask folded inside it like a stone in a snowball. This came in quiet. A breath, and then her voice gone small and careful round the edges, the way it went when she'd been crying and was trying to climb up out of it before I could hear, and didn't make it.

"Devlin," she said.

She only called me Devlin when she was frightened or sorry. She was both. I sat down on the cold bunk with my boots still on and the partition smell still in my nose and I said, "I'm here, Mum," which is the only thing I've ever known how to say to her, the thing I learned to say at six standing in a doorway, *I'm here, it's all right, I'm here,* and I have spent my whole life saying it to frightened things and the horses believe me and she never quite has.

I won't tell you the whole of what she said. Some of it is hers and not mine to put down, and some of it I've spent ten years not repeating to anyone, and I'm not going to start now to win an argument with a dead woman about whose fault any of it was. I'll tell you what it was. It was the bad kind. The kind where the bright weeks run out and the dark comes in like water under a door, and there's no money, and there's a man involved or there isn't, it changes, and the flat's gone wrong, and the thing she takes to get through has stopped getting her through, and she's frightened of herself in a way a son should never have to hear his mother be frightened, and she's rung the one person who's good at the doorway. Which is me. I'm good at the doorway. It's the first thing I was ever good at. I read her panic down a phone three hours and a hundred and eighty miles away, by the breath, by where her voice went thin, the way I read a horse coming off a ramp, and I knew it was the kind you go to. You don't send money for this kind. You go.

"I'm coming," I said. "Tonight. Don't do owt till I'm there. Just — sit tight, Mum. I'm coming."

And then I sat in the dark caravan with the phone gone quiet in my hand and the small problem of it, which was that I was a hundred and eighty miles south with no car and no licence and eleven pound and a bit to my name, and the only horse-box on the place drank diesel I couldn't pay for and wouldn't have gone north for me anyway, because nothing on that yard went anywhere for me. I was the labour. The labour doesn't get the lorry.

*

I went down to the Hall because there was nowhere else to go and Glenys's caravan was dark and I couldn't have asked Glenys anyway, she had nothing, she had less than me, she had thirty years sunk in the place and a roll-up she'd never light. There was a light on at the one window the Hall still lit, the office end, Caro's end, the bulb she let herself for the brown envelopes and the pound-shop calculator and the ledger she kept the real sums in. It was gone half twelve. I stood on the gravel in the cold with the silver dark behind its cabinet glass and I thought, *she'll be at the figures,* the way she always was at the figures at that hour, the one time of day she let the fear sit on her face because she thought nobody was watching, and I'd watched, once, back in April, through this same window, and not been able to read it. Money doesn't have a body. I had the wrong eyes for it.

I knocked. I'd never once knocked on the Hall at night. The labour doesn't knock at night.

She came to the boot-room door in a man's dressing gown that must have been Hugo's, the cord wrapped twice round her gone-to-bone middle, her white hair down out of the scrape for once and thinner than I'd ever seen it, an old woman at her kitchen table at midnight with the heating off and the chequebook open, and she looked at me on the step and she didn't say *what is it* and she didn't say *do you know the time.* She read me. She read me the way I read horses, the way she'd read me on the gravel in March down to the half-chaps over the trainers — and whatever she saw in my face she had the door open and her hand on my arm before I'd got a word out.

"Sit down," she said. "You've gone the colour of the wall. Sit down before you go over."

"It's my mum," I said. "She's — I have to get to Sheffield. Tonight. I haven't — I've no way of —"

And here is the thing I have turned over more nights than I will ever admit to. Here is the act. I want to set it down exactly, with nothing on the scale tipped either way, because the whole of what I learned that summer is in the gap between what this looked like and what it might have been, and I have never once been able to close the gap, and I've stopped trying, and the not-closing-it is the truest thing I own.

She didn't hesitate. That's the part. There was no calculation in it that I could read, and I can read calculation, I'd watched her run the leg on a man with a chequebook and known the exact second she found his soft place — and there was none of that here, none of the move, none of the *compliment first and the other thing while you're warm from it.* She just stood up out of the chair, seventy-one years old at half-twelve at night, and she said, "Right," the way you say it when a horse is cast in its box and there's no time, and she went and got the keys to the old estate car off the hook by the boot-room door, the rotten Volvo that started one morning in three, and she said:

"I'll drive. Get your bag. Don't wake the lads, there's nothing they can do and they'll only fuss."

"You can't drive me to Sheffield," I said. "It's three hours. It's the middle of the —"

"I drove to Aachen in a four-horse lorry with a clutch going when you were a thought in nobody's head," she said. "I can drive a Volvo to Sheffield. Get your bag."

*

The Volvo started on the third try, which she took as a verdict in her favour, and we went up the black lane past the finger-post where I'd got off the bus in March, and out onto the A-road, and then the motorway, the M50 onto the M5, the same road we hauled the horses down to the shows, except going the wrong way, going north, going home, with no horse behind us and an old woman driving who'd ridden for England and was now squinting through a windscreen that wouldn't quite demist with her ruined beautiful hands at ten and two and the signet ring loose on the finger gone too thin to hold it.

She didn't talk for the first hour. She let me sit in it. That was a kindness too, or it was the right tactic, and I couldn't tell, and I was past caring which, because the dark was coming up the country toward Sheffield and my mother was at the far end of it and I was nineteen and frightened and an old rich stranger was driving me to her through the night and it was the most anyone had done for me in my life and I knew it even then. *I knew it even then.* That's what makes it impossible. You can't unfeel a thing like that afterward just because you later learn what else was going on.

Somewhere past Worcester she said, not looking over, "Is it drink, or is it something worse than drink?"

I didn't answer for a bit. Out the window the cooling towers came up, the ones that meant the Midlands, lit from underneath, steaming into the orange dark.

"It changes," I said.

"Yes." She changed up a gear, the box notchy under her hand. "It would." And then, after a while, in a voice I'd never heard her use, flatter, with the charm taken right out of it, the way you'd talk to someone in the dark when you couldn't see their face and didn't have to perform yours: "I lost my sister to something that changed. People think the cruelty of it is the big nights. It isn't. The cruelty is the good weeks. You forget. You let yourself believe it. And then." She didn't finish. She didn't need to. *And then.* I knew exactly what the *and then* was. I'd lived in the *and then* my whole childhood.

I looked at her across the dark cab. Her face in the dashboard light was just bones and the road sliding over it. I read what I could. There was no leg on. There was no move. There was a woman who'd been alone with figures at midnight and was now driving a boy she had no blood claim on through three hours of dark because his mother was drowning, and she had picked up a thing I'd said about the good weeks being the worst, and she had given me, in exchange, the one true thing I ever heard her say about herself — *I lost my sister to something that changed* — and she had given it to me in the dark where she didn't have to, with nobody watching, with nothing in it for the yard.

*

The hospital was the big one off the ring road, the one I knew, the one we'd been to before, more than once, in the *and then* years — the same revolving door, the same blue chairs, the same vending machine and the same smell, that hospital smell that's the same in Sheffield as it is anywhere, disinfectant over something it can't quite cover. I knew the way to the ward without asking. That's a thing about a certain kind of childhood. You know the way to the ward.

They'd brought her in a couple of hours before us. She was through, she was safe, she was the kind of safe that means they've made sure you can't do the thing tonight and they'll talk to someone in the morning about the rest of it. A nurse with kind tired eyes told me she was sleeping and I could sit with her but to let her sleep, and looked at Caro, this tall ramrod old woman in a man's coat thrown over a dressing gown, standing very straight under the strip lights at three in the morning, and clearly couldn't place her — too grand for a nan, too old for a mother — and Caro said, before I had to find words for it, "I'm a friend of the family. I drove him up," and the nurse nodded and let it go, and *friend of the family* went into me somewhere I didn't have a name for, because I didn't have a family that had friends, I had a mother and a doorway, and now apparently I had this.

I went in and sat with my mum.

I'm going to do this part plain. It's the only way I can. The dry gear's no good here. I keep it for the rest of the book, for the money and the well-bred and the absurd liturgy of it, I keep it for armour, because being funny is how I stop a thing reaching me — but it doesn't work on her and it doesn't work on the horse and those are the two places in this whole story where I go defenceless, and I've decided, ten years on, to let myself, the once, here.

She was asleep. She looked small. The bed made everyone small but she'd have looked small anyway. You could see the bones I got, and the watchfulness, even sleeping there was a watchfulness in her face that wouldn't put itself down. Her hands were on top of the blanket and they were still, which they weren't always, some mornings they shook and some mornings they didn't and you read the day off the hands before she was up, that was the first reading I ever did, before horses, before anything, I read my mother's hands across a kitchen to know what kind of day it was going to be, and that is where the gift came from, all of it, the whole of what I can do came out of a kid in Parson Cross learning to read a frightened thing because his safety depended on getting it right. There's no mystery in it. People want it to be a mystery. Caro wanted it to be a feel you're born with. It's not. It's a wound that learned to read.

I took one of her still hands. She didn't wake. I sat there in the dark with the obs machine ticking somewhere and the ward asleep around us and I held my mum's hand, and I didn't say *I'm here* this time, because she couldn't hear it and because for once I didn't need to be at the doorway, somebody else had got her through the door, and I could just sit, and be her son, and not her doorway, for one hour, in the dark, which is a thing I had almost never got to be.

She woke once, near four. Her eyes came open and found me and there was a second before the rest of it came back to her, a second where she was just glad, just *Devlin* in the good register, the naked one, the one that undoes me — "You came," she said, and her face did a thing, "you came, love, all that way" — and then the rest of it came back into her eyes, the shame of it, the where-she-was, and she looked away from me at the ceiling and said, in the bright one now, the lie, climbing fast back up into it, "I'm all right, you know. I am. Daft fuss. I'll be home tomorrow, you'll see, you've come all this way for nowt," and I let her have it, the bright lie, I didn't take it off her, because it was the only roof she had over the thing and you don't take a person's roof off in the rain. I said, "I know, Mum. Daft. Go back to sleep." And she did, holding my hand, the bright lie and the naked truth both in the same face in the same minute, the two registers, the two gears, hers before they were mine. I learned everything I am from this woman. The reading and the lying both. *Be useful, be needed, and maybe they'll keep you.* She never meant to teach me it. It's just the only thing she had to pass on, and she passed it on the way you pass on the colour of your eyes, without choosing.

*

I went out near five to find a machine for a coffee because I couldn't sit any more, and Caro was in the corridor.

She hadn't gone. There was a café shut behind a grille and a bank of blue plastic chairs bolted to the wall and she was sitting on one of them, this woman who'd jumped for Britain, in her dead husband's coat, under the strip light at five in the morning in a Sheffield hospital corridor, ramrod still, her hands folded in her lap and the signet ring loose on the thin finger, and she'd been there the whole time. Three hours she'd been there. She could have slept in the car. She could have driven home and let me find my own way back. She'd sat in a plastic chair all night so I wouldn't come out of that ward into an empty corridor.

I sat down next to her. The machine wasn't worth the walk. I just sat.

"How is she," Caro said.

"Asleep. They've — she's safe for tonight. There's a thing in the morning, a — they'll do an assessment, a referral, I don't — I've done this before. There's a process. It takes the shape it takes."

Caro nodded slowly. She didn't say *I'm sorry,* which I was grateful for, because *I'm sorry* is what the well-bred say to close a thing off, and she didn't try to close it off. She let it stay open in the corridor with us, the great wound of my whole life, and she sat in it with me, and after a while she said the thing, the one I've carried ten years, and I still don't know what it was.

"My son writes cheques," she said. "Julian. You've met him. He writes cheques so he never has to do — this." She moved one hand, the ruined beautiful hand, a small gesture that took in the corridor and the ward and the night and the whole of it. "He thinks the cheque is the love. He thinks if the cheque's big enough it counts as having come. It doesn't. There's only one currency in the end and it's this. It's sitting in the bad chair. Everything else is just — paper." She turned her head and looked at me, and her old hard eyes had water standing in them, real water, I'd seen it once before, at Hickstead after the clear, and I'd not been able to get a blade between the love in it and the appraisal in it, and I couldn't now either. "You're a good son to her," she said. "I want you to know I see it. Most people would have learned not to go. You still go. That's worth more than any horse, Devlin, and don't let anyone in my world tell you different, because they don't know how to do the one thing you do without thinking, which is *go.*"

And she put her hand on my shoulder. The ruined hand, the one that had held a thousand frightened mouths soft, that had held mine across a dead man's table in March, and she left it there, light, in the bad chair, at five in the morning, and I let it stay.

I want to be honest about what it did. It re-bonded me to her completely. Whatever had cracked at Hickstead — and something had cracked, I'd watched her face do the love-and-the-asset thing over the rosette and I'd felt the first cold draught of knowing what she was — this closed it. This sealed it back up. I sat in that corridor under her hand and I thought: *she came. Nobody comes. She came.* And I knew, the way you know a thing in your body and not your head, that I was not going to be able to leave her. That Raef's contract, the wages, the paper that said *I will not be able to change my mind about you on a Tuesday,* the whole honest future the only honest man in the book had laid flat in front of me — that I was never going to take it now, because how do you leave the one person who sat in the bad chair. You can't. I couldn't. The love was real. I'd swear to it in any court. There was nobody to perform it for at five in the morning in a corridor that smelled of bleach.

And the love was the leash. Both. At the same time. In the same hand on the same shoulder. That's the thing I came home with, more than I came home with anything, the thing I'd been learning all season on horses and now learned on myself, in my own body, at the worst hour of the worst night: that you cannot pull the love from the leverage, not in her, not in me, not in anyone, that the realest care I'd ever been given and the surest hold anyone ever got on me were not two things wearing each other's coat — they were one thing, with one hand, and the hand was warm, and I leaned into the warm because I was nineteen and frightened and that is what you do with a warm hand, you lean.

What I didn't know — what I couldn't have known, sitting there grateful and held — was where the rest of her was that week. I found it out later, the way I found out everything, after. That while she sat in the bad chair being the only person who'd ever mothered me, the other thing was already in motion, two hundred miles south, the thing that had no body for me to read. A date had been set for the box to come for him. The delivery papered, the figure agreed, the last service named — *he jumps Birmingham first, Mr Al-Mazroui is very pleased the horse jumps your Birmingham first.* Somewhere that week, while her hand was on my shoulder, a phone I never saw rang or didn't ring, and a thing was signed or shaken, and the sale of the horse I'd made closed over like Raef's slow certain hand closing over hers in the lorry park, and she knew it, sitting there, she knew it while she told me *go,* she carried it in her the whole drive north and never let it show, because Caro Wexley-Tate never once in her life let you see a thing fall off her.

I have spent ten years trying to tell those two looks apart on her face and I cannot do it, and the not being able is the thing I came away with, and I think now it is the thing she came away with too, that she could not tell them apart either, that she had been managing decline with perfect posture for so long she'd lost the place where the love stopped and the saving-the-yard started, the same as I'd lost it on a horse — and I'd found that out on the studs and on the gift and now I'd found it out on her, and on me, in a corridor, with the worst behind a curtain down the ward and the worst not even started yet two hundred miles south.

We sat there a long time. The light coming up grey in the high windows. My mother asleep down the ward, got through one more night by people who weren't me, which was its own strange mercy, the doorway held by somebody else for once. And Caro beside me, who'd driven through the dark, who'd lost a sister to a thing that changed, who'd sit in the bad chair all night for a boy she had no claim on, and who was, that same week, in some other room I'll never see, signing him away from me — and her hand stayed on my shoulder, and I let it stay, and I could not have told you, and cannot now, whether what was on me in that corridor was the truest love I was ever shown or the most perfect hold anyone ever got, because I have looked at it from every angle there is and they are the same hand, and it was warm, and I leaned into it.

Outside the grey came up over Sheffield. South of us, in the dark we'd have to drive back into, the horse was in his box, and I'd taught him to wait for the sound of me, and he was waiting, and that was the one thing I'd built in this whole world that did exactly what I'd built it to do.


---

# Chapter Twenty-Five — The Ride

Caro drove us back down through the night with the sun coming up grey over the Cotswolds, and somewhere past Worcester she said, "Sleep, Devlin, there's nothing left to do that being awake will fix," and put her ruined hand off the gearstick for one second onto the back of mine where it lay on my knee, dry and hard and warm, and took it away again before either of us had to do anything about it. I didn't sleep. I watched the country come back up out of the dark — the spoil heaps, then the fields, then the red earth starting, the M5 going quiet then the A-roads then the lanes — and I thought about my mother asleep down a ward in Sheffield with a drip in the back of her hand, and I thought about this woman beside me who'd driven four hours up and was driving four hours back without being asked, who'd settled a thing at a hospital desk in the small hours that wasn't hers to settle, smooth and certain, the way she did everything, while I stood there nineteen and useless and grateful past speech.

I want that on the record before the rest of it, the way I keep wanting things on the record before the rest of it. She didn't have to. There was nothing in it for the yard. Nobody was watching. She did it because — and here's the thing I can't lay flat, ten years on — she did it because she's the only one who ever mothered me like that in my life, and the someone who never had got mothered like that doesn't get to be cool about it afterward, doesn't get to call it a move. It was real. I'd swear it on her grave, which I have stood at.

It was also the week the sale went through.

I didn't know that on the motorway.
