I learned the dates afterward, the way you always do, by going back over the calendar with a colder eye — that the delivery papers, the ones with Saracen's name and a price I never let myself look at all the way and a date in October, were signed and witnessed and posted while I was holding my mother's shaking hand in a corridor and Caro was buying me a coffee out of a machine and saying the exact right plain thing about how sometimes the kindest thing you can do for a person is keep the chair next to them warm. Both. In the same week. The same woman. The love and the leash, posted second class, and I cannot pull them apart, and I have stopped trying, because the trying was eating me.

*

The yard goes on. That's the cruelty of a yard, and the mercy of it. Whatever's happened to you, the horses don't know, and the muck heap doesn't know, and the haynets want filling at six whether or not your mother's in a Sheffield ward, and there's a particular kindness in a thing that needs you exactly as much when you're broken as when you're whole, and I went back to it the morning after we got home and let it hold me up the only way it knew how, by needing me.

Saracen knew something. Not the thing — he didn't know the thing, nobody'd told the horse — but he knew I'd been gone two days and come back wrong. He met me at the door with his head low and his ears doing the careful thing, the near one swivelled to me and the off one tracking the yard, and he breathed me over, my collar, my hands, the whole length of me, reading me the way I read him, smelling two days of motorway and hospital and another county on me, and he went still for a second the way he does, not the bad still, just the listening still, and then he blew out, the long low flutter, and put his nose flat against my chest and left it there, and I stood in the cold doorway with my hand on his face and let him.

"I know," I told him. Plain. "I know. I'm back."

He didn't ask anything else. That's the difference between him and everyone. He didn't need to know where I'd been or whether I'd send him something Monday or what the night was going to be, the good kind or the other kind. He just needed me back, and I was back, and that settled it for him, and I stood there and let a half-ton animal be the simplest love I had, which it was, which it had been since March, which was the whole trouble.

*

We were going to Birmingham. That was fixed now in a way it hadn't been fixed before, fixed the way a thing is fixed when the money's been counted on both ends and there's no slack anywhere in the arithmetic.

I had it off Glenys, in the feed room, over the beet steaming in the bin. She didn't make a speech of it. She'd said her one true thing weeks back and she'd told me she wouldn't say it twice and she didn't, but she'd give me the numbers, flat, the way you'd give a man the weather, because numbers aren't a warning, numbers are just where you stand.

"October," she said. "End of October's the letter. You'll have seen it." I had. The figure with the comma in the place that made your stomach go. "The horse goes after Birmingham. They've a date. The big class at Birmingham's the — what is it — the eleventh, near enough. The money off that, if you get round, plus what they're giving for him, the two together's what clears the letter." She stirred the beet. "Just about. If you get round. If nothing breaks."

"And if I don't get round."

She looked at me, and the look was the pity-look, the one she'd had since the gravel in March, the one Caro never had. "Then they've still sold the horse, haven't they, love. They've still got that. It's just the roof goes with it instead of just the horse." She tapped the spoon on the rim. "You're the bit that's not certain. That's all you ever were. The horse is sold either way. You're the bit they're hoping comes good."

I knew all of it. I want to be clear that I knew all of it, that there wasn't a fact in there I hadn't already worked out for myself, on the motorway, in the dark, with the colder eye. The sale was done. The delivery was October, after Birmingham, because a horse that wins under the lights at the biggest show in the country flies out worth more than a horse that doesn't, and I was the only person alive who could make him win under the lights, and so I was kept on him through October not as a rider with a horse but as the last process the asset went through before it shipped — the polish, the final coat of value, the thing you do to a body to make it worth the most it'll ever be worth on the day you give it away.

I knew it. Knowing it didn't do anything. That was the lesson of the whole season and I'd had it a dozen ways and here it was again, plain as the beet: understanding the move doesn't unmake the move. I'd known that about Caro since the kitchen table in March. I knew it now about the whole machine, and I was inside the machine, and I went and got the haynets.

*

Here is the thing I have to tell you, and it is the thing I came to this chapter to tell, and I've been putting it off the way you put off the worst foot.

I schooled him in the evenings for Birmingham, and the schooling was the best work I ever did, and somewhere in it I stopped paying for it.

I don't mean the work got easier. The work was never easy — a 1.60m horse three years off being condemned doesn't get made easy, you make him a stride at a time, an evening at a time, you give and you wait and you ask and you give again, and I did all of that, I did it more carefully than I'd ever done anything. I mean the thing inside me that used to cost something when I did it. The flinch. The small true cold that opened up under the warm the first time, on Quillan, by the water, when I read her honest no and used it instead of answering it and felt it like a coin going down in the dark — that. It was gone. I went looking for it one evening in the indoor with the leak ticking into its bucket and the light going blue through the north end, and it wasn't there.

I'll tell you exactly how it went, because if I tell it general you'll let me off, and I don't want letting off.

There's a place in the school, the far end, where the door swings on a bad hinge if the wind catches it, and the door swinging is into his blind corner, his right side, the bad side, the curb side, and all spring and into the summer the swinging door was a thing we managed, a thing I rode him toward and gave him the right rein at and waited the half-stride and brought him through, and every single time it had been a conversation, a real one, *the bad thing's there, I know, I'm here, come on*, and every single time he'd asked the question — the still, the stopped blink, the flicker under the right eye — and I'd answered it, and the answering had been the cleanest thing my hands ever did, and it had cost me nothing because there was nothing to pay, it was a gift, it was love, it was two frightened things keeping each other company in the worst second.

And this evening the door swung, and I felt him gather to nap left off it, and I read the flicker start three strides out — *do I have to do this on my own, or are you here* — and I didn't answer it.

I used it.

I read exactly where his fear was and exactly how much rein he needed and exactly the half-stride he was going to need to wait, and I gave him all of it, smooth, perfect, faster than I'd ever given it, and I brought him through the corner and over the little fence past the swinging door clean and careful, his legs snapped up apologetic to his chin — and I did the whole thing the way Tomas Berg does the whole thing, surgically, from the outside, with the part of me that used to be in there with him standing off to one side now, watching, assessing, getting it right.

And it worked. Of course it worked. It always works. That was never the part that fails.

And I felt nothing.

I want to be precise about the nothing, because the nothing is the whole of it. It wasn't that I felt bad and pushed it down. Pushing it down would have been something; pushing it down is what I did on Quillan and at Hickstead with the navy groom's tape measure, and pushing it down hurts, pushing it down is a muscle you can feel ache. This was no muscle. This was the place the muscle used to be, smooth, painless, the flinch gone out of it the way feeling goes out of a scar — you can put your thumb on a scar and there's a horse there, a whole horse, a kick or a gate or a man, something that happened, and the place itself feels nothing, it's just pale and slick and quiet and tells you nothing, because the nerve died.

I rode a frightened animal past the oldest terror in his body for nobody's benefit but the practice of doing it well, and it cost me nothing, and I didn't even clock the nothing until I was halfway down the long side. I clocked it the way you clock a step that isn't there in the dark — that drop, that lurch, the foot coming down through the place where the ground should be. I felt good. That was the lurch. I'd just done the worst thing I knew how to do, the thing I'd sworn over his scar I'd never do to him, the thing I'd named out loud by the water with Quillan as *no line, there's no line, I just did the second one on purpose* — I'd done it to *him*, to Saracen, in the dark, for the practice, and I felt good, I felt skilled, I felt like a man at the top of his trade, and the feeling-good was the thing that finally went through me like cold water, because it meant the line wasn't somewhere up ahead of me anymore that I might one day cross.

It was behind me. I'd crossed it somewhere back down the season, kindly, in the dark, by the swinging door, and I hadn't even felt the day I did. There'd been no day. That's the part. There was no border post, no fence, no place where the care stopped and the other thing started. I'd gone looking for the line my whole life, this whole season, since the bolted box, and the truth was you don't cross it, it crosses you, slow, while you're being good at your job, and by the time you turn round to look for it there's just the long even ground behind you and your own boot prints going back and no line anywhere in any of it, only the distance you've come.

I pulled him up in the middle of the school. He stood, blowing soft, pleased with himself, the near ear tipped back to me, *was that all right, was I good*. And I sat there in the blue dark with my hand on his neck and could not, for a long time, make myself say the thing I always say, because for the second time that summer there was nothing true left to say to him, and this time it wasn't grief that stopped my mouth. It was that I couldn't tell, anymore, looking down at the back of his head and the ear tipped to me, whether what I felt was love or the most fluent piece of leverage I'd ever performed in my life — because I'd made them the same thing, with my own hands, an evening at a time, and now I genuinely could not get a blade between them, and neither could he, and only one of us knew there was a blade.

*

I'd like to tell you something happened after that. A reckoning. A door I walked out of, a thing I said to Caro, a horse I refused to ride.

Nothing happened. The thing was fully understood and fully felt and fully unbearable and I went on doing it anyway.

Because here's what I had, all of it bearing down at once. A horse I'd made and loved and ruined-by-making, sold out from under me on a handshake that was never paper. A vet's silence that was really my silence, the thing I saw in July and said nothing about, the saying-nothing that bought my own ride. A horse on the ground that other week, screens up, the tarp the same colour as the school roof — and I'd been there, my hands in it, in the silence if nothing else. And a mother in a Sheffield ward and a woman in this dying house who'd driven me to her through the night and held the chair warm, and who I could not leave, *could not*, because she'd mothered me the way the one who should have couldn't, and you don't leave the person who finally does the thing, even when you can see, plain as the beet, that the doing of it is exactly the leg she put on you.

Understanding was never the lock. The lock was that everything holding me there was made of the only love I'd ever learned to take, which is the love that comes in being needed, and you cannot saw through that, because the saw is made of the same stuff. I was kept because I was useful. I'd have died for the word. I more or less was.

So I went on. I mucked out and I rode the string and I schooled him in the evenings for Birmingham, surgically, costlessly, perfectly, and I got better at it every single night, and getting better was getting worse, and there was no one to tell because the only one who'd have understood it without me having to say a word was the horse, and the horse was the thing I was doing it to.

*

Glenys started getting his kit by, the way she did before a horse went anywhere it wasn't coming back from. I didn't understand it at first and then I did. She brought the good travel rugs up out of the rug room and ran a hand over them for moth and hung them on the rail in the sun. She found the spare headcollar, the soft one, our soft one, the worn brown one I'd done his first noseband up in, and she cleaned it and oiled it and laid it over the door, and I knew the new one was coming, the brass one, the colour of a thing bought that week, and that hers was the headcollar that would lead him as far as the bottom of a ramp and no further.

She didn't say anything about it. She didn't say anything about most of it, that was the deal she'd made with herself a long time ago, before me, to see all of it and stay and do the job, because she'd nowhere else to go and a caravan of her own and thirty years sunk in this drowning place, same as me only further down. She'd led horses to lorries before. She'd led horses she loved more than she loved most people to the bottoms of strangers' ramps and stood there holding the headcollar while the new owner's man backed the box up, and she'd done it kind, so the horses wouldn't be frightened, which is its own thing, being kind to a creature for the man who's taking it, being the last gentle hand. She'd told me about it, the once. *You'll have helped.* And now she was getting his rugs by and oiling our old headcollar and not saying it, and I helped her carry the rugs, and we worked side by side at it, wordless, both of us knowing, both of us doing it, the conscience and the boy, two people who could see the whole con and were each holding an end of the horse's travelling kit.

"He'll want company on the box, going out," she said once, the only thing she said about any of it, flat, practical, a groom's sentence with the whole of her grief folded up small inside it where you'd never find it without a knife. "He travels bad alone. You'll have to put Hob's old rug in with him, the smell of it. Hob's the only one he ever settled next to."

"I'll do that," I said.

"Aye," she said. "You'll do that." And she went back to the moth-checking, and that was the conversation, and it was the truest one I had with a person that whole month, two sentences about a rug.

*

I went up to him that evening and didn't ride a fence. I just rode him.

The light was going long and gold over the top field and the swallows were working the muck heap low in the warm and Hob was a brown shape down by the far hedge being no use to anyone and outliving us all out of spite, and I tacked Saracen up in the old brown headcollar's bridle and got on and walked him out round the field on the buckle, the reins loose, nothing asked, no fence, no clock, no bell anywhere, just the two of us going round in the last of a summer evening with the grass seeding silver and the wood pigeons going on in the limes.

And he was happy. That's the thing I can't get past, telling it. He was happy. He went round long and low with his neck stretched down and his back swinging and his off ear out on the world and the near ear tipped back to me the whole way, *here, are you here, this is good isn't it, we're all right* — and I gave it back to him, *here, I'm here, this is good, we're all right* — and the swallows went under us and the gold came down and a horse who'd had a hoof on a wall all night his first March, alone in the dark, doing the only thing he had left to do with no one listening, walked round a field in the evening trusting me completely, with his whole half-ton body, every soft pound of it, asking me nothing because I'd answered everything, *do I have to do this alone, or are you here*, and the answer was yes, I was here, I'd always been here, I was never going anywhere — and that was the lie, the last and biggest one, the one I rode him round on in the gold, because in October I was going to be exactly nowhere, I was going to be standing at the bottom of a ramp with a brass headcollar going past me onto a stranger's box, and he didn't know, and I did, and the only difference between us, the only thing in the whole evening that wasn't shared, was that I knew and he didn't.

He blew out, long and low and easy, the good flutter, the one he only ever gave me, and dropped his poll a degree, and walked on into the gold, trusting, not knowing.

I leaned down and put my thumb on the scar. The pale slick place on the near shoulder, the page torn out of him, the kick or the gate or the man, the one part of him I could never read, the one part of him with no feeling left in it, dead and quiet and telling me nothing. I put my thumb on it the way I always did, without deciding to, and I held it there while he walked, and I could not for the life of me, with my thumb on the one dead place in him, tell you whether the warm thing going up my arm was the truest love I'd ever had or the finest piece of work I'd ever done — because I had made them, with these hands, in this field, an evening at a time, the same thing.

I didn't say anything. There was nothing left that was both true and bearable. So I just rode him round the field in the last of the light, and let him be happy, and let myself not be, and kept my thumb on the place that couldn't feel a thing.


---

# Chapter Twenty-Six — Under Lights

The leaves had turned while I wasn't looking. That was the first thing, coming out of the lorry at five in the morning into the NEC car park with the floodlights on the tarmac and the breath standing in the air — that somewhere between the gold field and here the year had gone over, the limes at Tatefield were copper and dropping, and I'd missed it the way you miss everything in a season that's eating you, head down, one foot, one box, one evening at a time.

We'd come up overnight, Curtis driving the rotten box with the gaffer-taped wing mirror up the M5 and the M6 with his radio going through batteries, and I'd ridden in the back with the horse the last hour because he'd started to fret on the motorway, the deep slow fret, the weight going from foot to foot, and I'd sat on the upturned bucket by his head in the dark with the road coming up through my boots and talked to him plain and low about nothing, the way you'd talk to a kid who's woken up not knowing where he is. He travelled bad alone. Glenys had said it. So I went in with him, and Hob's old rug went in the corner of the box with him for the smell of home, and that was the only home he was getting where we were going.

Where we were going was Birmingham. England's biggest indoor show, the season's cathedral, the place every working kid in the country puts on a wall as the thing they want before they've worked out it'll be the thing that costs them. I want that on the record, that I wanted it. I'd wanted it since Bev's, since Cinders, since I was a kid mucking out for rides. Getting to HOYS. I'd got there. And I'd got there on a horse that was already sold, in a box that wasn't mine, to ride a round that would finish the only thing I loved, and I stood in the floodlit car park with my breath in the air and my hands going dead with cold and felt, underneath the wanting, which was real, the other thing, which was also real, and I couldn't tell you which was bigger, and I'd given up trying, the way you give up trying to read a horse that's been made to lie to you.

*

The stabling at the NEC is a hangar. That's the thing nobody tells you, that the cathedral has a back of house and the back of house is breeze block and concrete and strip light, an enormous shed of temporary boxes run up out of metal frame and ply, hundreds of them, alley after alley, and over the whole of it the lights buzz, a flat white buzzing that never goes off, day or night, so the place has no dark in it anywhere, no proper dark, just the buzzing white going on and on over the rows of horses who'd come from quiet yards in the country and now stood in the noise of it not sleeping.

I led him in off the ramp and he came down it sideways for the first three steps — not the bad sideways of March, not the half a ton going over, just the worried sideways, the off-hind feeling for ground that wasn't where his body said it should be, the white coming up in the corner of his eye — and then he was on the concrete with the lights buzzing over him and the smell of two hundred strange horses coming at him from every alley and a tannoy somewhere saying a name and a class, and he stopped dead, and he lifted his head as high as it would go, and his ears went flat back and hard to his poll, locked, and I felt the whole of him go up through the lead rope like a wire pulled tight, and I stood at his shoulder and didn't pull and didn't cluck and didn't do anything except be there, my hand flat on his neck, and let him take the measure of the worst place I'd ever asked him to stand.

"I know," I said. Plain. "I know. It's a lot."

He didn't believe me yet. That's the difference, here, that I want to be honest about — at home the *I know* worked, the *I'm here* worked, because home was a place his body had decided was safe a stride at a time over seven months, and this was not home, this was a buzzing white shed full of fear that wasn't even his, the fear coming off two hundred other horses and going into him, and the *I know* was just a sound I was making until I could make it true.

Our box was at the end of an alley, ply on three sides and bars on the front, no window, no sky, the lights overhead never going off. I got him in and he went straight to the back of it and put his quarters in the corner and turned to face the door, which everyone who didn't know him would have read as settling and which I read as the only sane thing he had — quarters to the wall, the thing in front of him where he could see it, the door that didn't shut. At home there'd been one box, one bolted box, where the dark was the only thing he didn't fight. Here there was no dark to give him. So he gave himself the wall, and I gave him the haynet and Hob's rug spread in the corner and a bucket of his own water carried from the country because he wouldn't drink strange, and I left the bottom door of the box open and stood in it where he could see me and let him understand by the long slow arithmetic of a horse that I, at least, was the one fixed thing in the buzzing white.

He blew out. Eventually. Long and low, the flutter, the one he only ever gave me, and dropped his poll a degree, and the near ear came round off the poll and tipped back toward me where I stood in the door, listening, *are you here, is this all right, are you here* — and the off ear stayed up and hard on the alley, on the noise, on the place. One ear for me and one for the thing he was frightened of. He'd gone quiet. He'd gone quiet in the worst place in England, for me, because I'd asked him to, and I stood in the open door and gave it back to him, *here, I'm here,* and did not let myself think the next part, which was that I'd be giving him this for two more days and then I'd be giving him nothing, ever, again.

*

They were all there. That was the thing about the cathedral, that it gathered everyone into one building, the whole season's apparatus under the one buzzing roof, and across the two days before the Grand Prix I saw every one of them, each going about getting exactly what they'd come for.

Tomas Berg was three alleys over, his touring rig backed up to a double bay, two grooms in matching polos doing his string the way a pit crew does a car, and he nodded to me across the concrete the morning we arrived, cordial, brief, one professional clocking another, and said, "You qualified him at Hickstead," not a question, a fact, the way he said everything, and I said yes, and he looked down the alley toward my box where Saracen's head was up over the bars watching the noise and he said, "He is a lot of horse for an indoor," and I said, "He is," and Tomas nodded once more and went back to his horses, and that was the conversation, and there was no warmth in it and no malice in it and it was more respect than half the people in that building with twice his manners ever showed me. He'd be in the Grand Prix. He'd ride a clear round, probably, the way he rode everything, clean and cold and costing him nothing, because there was nothing in him for it to cost, and I'd watch him do it and not be able to decide whether he'd solved the thing I was breaking on or just cut the part of himself out that the thing lives in.

Raef I saw the second day, in the seating along the practice area, in the understated coat that costs the most, with the stillness on him that the fully secure have, the absence of strain, the not being in any hurry about anything in the world. He saw me and came down, unhurried, and got my name right, both halves and the hyphen, the way he always did, and we stood at the rail and watched a class neither of us was in, and after a while he said, "He looks well," and he meant the horse, my horse, his horse, the horse with his name on a paper in a drawer somewhere and a date in it, and I said, "He's well," and Raef nodded, and there was a silence, and then he said the thing, the way he always said the thing, courteous and exact and devastating.

"I would like you to know," he said, "that the offer I made you has not gone anywhere. The horse is one thing. You are another. When this is over, whatever you decide, the door is open. A wage. A contract. Paper, which is a respect." He let it sit. "You have built something here that the people who made it possible will not pay you for. I would pay you for it. That is all I have ever said to you. I am sorry it is also the truth."

I read his hands on the rail while he said it, easy, still, a man with nothing falling off him, and I read the kindness in it, which was real, and the appraisal in it, which was also real, because he was offering me the same thing he'd bought in the horse — a frightened clever creature made to override its own fear for someone else's programme — and offering it honestly, with a number on it and a contract under it, which made him the cleanest dealer in the building. I could not take it. The wage felt like being told the love had been a transaction all along, and I could not survive being told that, and so I chose, the way I always chose, the leash that came wrapped as love over the contract that came wrapped as respect, and stood at the rail of the biggest show in the country turning down the one man who'd save me.

"Thank you," I said. It's my mother's word. It's Pip's word. It's the word you say when you've nothing else and no power and the other person has all of it and is being kind about it. I'd said it to the navy groom at Hickstead while my whole life went into a tape measure. I said it to Raef now. *Thank you.*

"Of course," he said, and put a hand briefly on my shoulder, and went back up to his seat, and I watched him go, the man whose name was on the horse, here to watch his horse jump, correct to the last button, getting exactly what he'd priced and never the thing he wanted, which was the thing that passed between me and the animal in the half-stride and could not be bought because it was not for sale and was, also, in the very same breath, the thing I was about to sell him.

*

Caro came up Thursday in the estate Volvo with the clutch she'd driven to Aachen on the same temper, and Glenys with her, and the two of them set up our cheap corner of the hangar the way they'd set up a hundred shows, the tack on the saddle horse, the rugs squared, the feed in the bin, the buckets labelled, the whole portable yard of a yard that couldn't afford itself, and I watched Glenys work and saw she'd brought the new headcollar. The brass one. The colour of a thing bought that week. She hung it on a nail at the end of the alley, near our box but not on it, on the bare ply where Saracen couldn't reach to mouth it, and she didn't say anything about it and neither did I, and it hung there for two days catching the buzzing light. The old brown one, our one, the one I'd done his first noseband up in, stayed on the box door where it had always been.

Glenys saw me see it.

"Don't," she said. Not unkind. "Not yet. There's a whole show to get through first. You'll think about the wrong end of it the whole way and ride like rubbish and have it down, and then they've sold the horse *and* you've ridden like rubbish, and what's the good of that to anybody." She tucked the unlit roll-up back behind her ear where it never left. "Ride the round. Think about the rug after. That's the job."

"That's the job," I said.

"Aye." She looked at me a second, the pity-look, the one she'd had since the gravel in March, and I waited for her to say the thing, the true thing, the one she'd fired the once at Hickstead and told me she'd not fire again — *they'll have the horse off you, love, and they'll have you and all, and they'll do it kind, so you'll thank them* — and she didn't say it, because she'd said it, and she keeps her word, which is more than anyone in that family ever did, and she just went back to squaring the rugs, the conscience of the place, doing the job, getting the horse ready for the ramp, with love, because that's what the job is, and the two of us worked side by side at it in the buzzing white, both of us knowing the whole of it, each of us holding an end of the kit.

Caro came and stood at the box and looked in at the horse with the back of her thin ruined hand against her mouth, the way she did when she thought no one was reading her, and I read her, because I read everyone, and I could not tell — I will never be able to tell — whether what was in her face looking at that horse was the love of fifty years of horses or the relief of a woman watching the thing that would pay the October letter stand sound and well and worth a fortune the day before she sold it. Both. Always both, with her. She'd said so herself once, in the indoor, *both, always both, with me,* the most honest sentence she ever gave me, and I'd not understood then that it was a warning.

"He's settled," she said.

"He's frightened," I said. "He just trusts me more than he's frightened. For now."

She looked at me then, properly, the old hard eyes, and something moved in them that I'd swear was real, the way I'd swear the corridor in Sheffield was real, the night she drove me up and sat the three hours in the bad chair so I'd not come out of the ward into an empty corridor, the realest thing anyone ever did for me, posted second class the same week as the delivery papers.

"That," she said, "is the only thing that's ever been worth anything in this entire business. And nobody will ever pay you a penny for it." She put her hand flat on the bars a moment, near the horse but not on him. "I'm sorry, Devlin. Some of it. Not all of it. I'd do it all again." And she took her hand off the bars and went to find Marnie, and I stood there with that, which is the most truthful and the most useless thing she ever gave me, an apology with no refund in it, love and the leash in the one sentence, *I'm sorry, some of it, not all of it, I'd do it all again,* and I have turned it over for ten years and I cannot get a blade between the two halves of it any more than I can on the horse.

*

Iwan was there. Of course Iwan was there — a show like this needs a vet on the ground, and he'd driven the grey Transit up with the knocking diesel, the *& SON* peeling worse than ever, and he was somewhere in the back of the hangar most of the two days, big and gone soft and kind-eyed and ashamed, and we passed in an alley on the second evening and he stopped and put a cracked hand on Saracen's neck through the bars, gentle, the gentlest thing about him, and the horse let him, and Iwan looked at the horse and not at me and said, in the soft trailing Welsh that never lands on the thing it's saying, "He's clean as a whistle, this one. Nothing in him at all, see. Just himself." And I understood what he was telling me, which was that whatever had been in him at Hickstead in July to make him vet sound and trot up sweet and jump the qualifier like a horse who wasn't there — *help him along, settle him, just the once* — was gone now, long gone, worn off weeks back, and that the horse who'd jump the Grand Prix would be the real one, the frightened one, the one who'd have to be talked over every fence by hand because there'd be no chemical doing it for him.

It was the kindest thing he could have told me and it was also the thing that meant the round would cost the horse everything, because a clean horse jumping the biggest fences of his life in the loudest place in England is a horse spending the whole of his borrowed courage, with nothing in him to spend it for him, on me.

And the blood was still on its shelf. I knew that too. Three tubes of it, dark glass, pencil-dated in Iwan's careful hand, sealed and cold in the steel fridge in Leominster, the body that never lies made to lie and the proof of the making kept where it would never be looked at — *mostly never gets looked at,* he'd said, *mostly* — and it sat there through the whole show, through the Grand Prix, through everything, the one gun in the story that was loaded and never went off, which I have decided is worse than if it had, because a gun that fires is a reckoning and a gun that doesn't is just everyone, all of us, every one, getting clean away with it, kindly, forever.

I looked at Iwan's hand on the horse's neck and I didn't say any of it. Neither did he. That not-saying was the whole transaction. It always had been. It was Caro's *don't insult us both by writing it down* with a needle in it instead of a handshake, and I'd signed it the same way I signed all of them, by saying nothing, by looking away, and here we both were, the vet and the boy, standing in the buzzing white with our hands near the only honest body in the building, the one we'd both quietened, each in our own way, for the man with the cheque.

*

Marnie I saw last, the second night, up at the box.

She'd come up with Caro, and she'd been in the stands all day watching the working hunters and the show ponies, the world she'd been bred into and couldn't win in, and she came down the alley at dusk — there's no dusk in the hangar, but I knew it was dusk outside, you learn the time by the tannoy quieting and the alleys emptying — and she stood at the bars and looked at Saracen in the corner with his quarters to the wall, and she was thinner than she'd been in March, white at the mouth even standing still now, even off a horse, and she bit the skin at the side of her thumbnail and looked at the horse and not at me.

We hadn't fixed anything, her and me. Since the indoor in July, since she'd stood at Beau's shoulder and asked me to make him go quiet for her so her grandmother would look at her the one time the way she looked at me, and I'd not been able to give it her and not been able to cleanly refuse it, and Beau had tipped his near ear back to me at the rail and not to her on his back, the honest body telling the truth at the worst possible second — since then, and since Beau, since the green screens the colour of the indoor roof and the wet-branch sound and the necessary thing done behind them, we'd had nothing to say that wasn't a wound, so we'd said nothing, and we said nothing now for a while, the two of us at the bars in the buzzing white.

Then she said, not looking at me, "He's going to jump it, isn't he. He's going to go clear." 

"I don't know," I said. "I hope so. He's frightened of the place."

"He'll go clear," she said. "He always goes clear for you. That's the whole —" She stopped. She bit her thumb. "Granny needs the money. The letter." She knew about the letter; she lived in the cold house; she'd grown up doing the arithmetic of decline in her head the way I'd grown up doing it on mine. "And then they take him."

"And then they take him."

She looked at me then, finally, the family bones, the strain in the face up close, the girl who was me in a forty-thousand-pound surname with no money behind it the same way I was her in half-chaps gone grey at the seams, both of us frightened things that had to be useful to be kept, and she said the thing that nobody else in that building knew to say, because she was the only one who'd ever wanted to *be* the one going quiet and understood what going quiet does to the one who goes.

"It's not fair what you've to do," she said. "You've to make him brilliant so he sells. You've to be the best you've ever been at the exact thing that loses him. And everyone's going to clap." Her voice didn't break. That was the worst of it; she'd learned, the way I'd learned, the way you learn it in houses where love arrives in bursts and leaves without warning, to say the unbearable thing flat. "I used to think I wanted what you've got. I don't. I worked it out. What you've got is you have to feel every bit of it and do it anyway. I'd rather be no good and not have to."

She wasn't wrong, and she wasn't asking for anything, and she didn't forgive me and she didn't condemn me, she just knew, which was more than anyone, and we stood at the bars a minute longer, the two frightened animals, and then she said, "Night, Dev," and went back up the alley to find her grandmother. I'd never once, the whole season, managed to do a single thing to help her, because the only thing I had to give was the thing that does the damage.

*

It got late. The alleys emptied. The grooms finished their last rounds and the tannoy went quiet and the great barn settled into what passes for night in a place with no dark, the buzzing white going on overhead, the horses shifting and dozing on their feet, two hundred of them, the country quiet of two hundred strange animals not at home, and the roar of the arena came faintly through the breeze block from the evening performance still going somewhere on the far side, the music and the announcer and the crowd, a tide of sound coming through the walls and into the box and into the horse.

He wouldn't lie down. I knew he wouldn't. He stood in his corner with Hob's rug under his feet and his quarters to the wall and his head up, not sleeping, the off ear tracking the roar through the wall, the near ear coming round to me whenever I shifted in the doorway, and every so often the roar would surge — a class won, a crowd up on its feet — and I'd watch his whole body climb, the weight going back, the white coming up in the corner of the eye, the muscle under the right eye starting, just starting, the small fast tic, there and gone, the flicker, the last second the horse is still asking — *do I have to do this on my own, or are you here* — and I'd say it, low, *here, I'm here,* and the tic would settle, and he'd blow out, and we'd wait for the next surge of the tide through the wall.

I should have gone up to the lorry and slept. There was a show to get through. Glenys had said it, *ride the round, think about the rug after, that's the job,* and she was right, the way she was right about everything, and I didn't go.

I stood in the doorway of the box a long time with my hand flat on the ply of the door, not going in, just standing there in the open frame where he could see me, the one fixed thing in the buzzing white, and watched him stand frightened in the worst place I'd ever brought him, the near ear searching the dark that wasn't dark for me, finding me, tipping back, *are you here,* and I was here, I was right here, my hand flat on his door, and tomorrow I would walk him into the chute and the lights and the roar and ask him, one more time, with everything I had, to override the oldest terror in his body and go clear for me — and the better he did it, the more brilliant I made him, the surer he was gone, sold, finished, loaded onto the brass-buckled headcollar and out of the bottom of a stranger's ramp and gone, and he didn't know, and I knew, and that was the only thing in the whole buzzing barn that wasn't shared between us.

He tipped his ear back to me in the dark that wasn't dark.

I didn't go in. Not yet. I stood with my hand on the door and let him listen for me, and saved the rest of what I had, all of it, every soft pound of it, for tomorrow — for the last time, though only one of us in that box knew it was the last time, and it wasn't him.


---

# Chapter Twenty-Seven — Walking the Course

I'd stood at the box door half the night with my hand flat on the ply and the near ear tipping back to find me in the white that never went dark, and at some point I'd gone up to the lorry and lain in the Luton over the cab and not slept, and then it was the morning of the Grand Prix and I was walking the biggest fences of my life on foot with my jacket off in the cold of the great empty arena, and the floor of it was deep and raked and printed all over with the studs of every horse that had jumped the night before, and the fences stood there under the lights, switched off now, waiting, the way a thing waits that knows you've got to come to it and it doesn't have to come to you.

They let the riders in early, before the public, while the men with the rakes were still doing the corners. The course walk is a procession and a liturgy and the only quiet you get all day. Two dozen of us out on the sand in our shirtsleeves with our numbers still in the office, pacing, heads down, counting under our breath, and nobody talking much, and the fences enormous over us in a way they never are from a horse, because from a horse you don't have time to be frightened of them and on foot you've nothing else to do.

A 1.60m oxer up close is not a fence. It's a wall of air you have to put a horse over. I stood at the first one and looked up at it and the top rail was at the height of my eye and the spread of it was the length of a man lying down, height and width, the back rail set the hand higher that invites a horse to make the shape, and I thought, the way you think it once and then never again all day because you can't afford to: *he is going to have to leave the ground a stride before that and not come down until he's past the back of it, and I am going to be on him when he does, and he is frightened of the building.* And then I put it away, the way you put everything away, and I walked the line.

*

The clock today wasn't a letter or a vetting or a man with a chequebook. The clock today was the bell, which hadn't rung yet, and the time allowed, which the course builder had set tight enough that you'd see it on the board after every round like a tax, and the forty-five seconds you'd get from the bell to the start, and then the round itself, the longest two minutes a body ever does, and somewhere under all of it the other clock that had been running since March and was nearly out — the letter, the end of October, the purse and the sale that together were meant to clear it, the whole season's arithmetic come down to whether the frightened horse I'd made could leave the ground twelve times in the right places in front of the man who already owned him.

I walked the track the way Caro taught me and the way I'd have done anyway. You don't walk where the fences are. You walk where you're going to *ride,* the line between them, the actual route your horse's feet take, and you count it in big deliberate steps, four to a stride, reading the related distances off your own legs.

The first line walked a forward six — two verticals down the long side with a flat fast gallop between them, the kind of distance that rides short if you fluff the corner and you've a pole down before you've started. Then a dogleg to the liverpool, the tray of water blue and dead under the off rail, the thing that spooks a horse down and not up, and Saracen had jumped water before and didn't love it and would jump it if I rode it like it wasn't there. Then the wall. Then the treble, A to B to C, one stride and then two, the cruellest thing on the course, because a refusal at B sends you back through A and a horse who's lost his nerve in the middle of a combination is a horse who's gone, and the distance into it was built a half-stride short, deliberately, the course builder's question of the day — *can you bring him back without killing the impulsion, can you sit him down and still send him.* And then the last line. Planks off a turn, looking like a wall, falling if a horse breathed on them. And the final oxer, square, the hardest to read, set on a related five strides from the planks down the short side, into the corner, with the wing you couldn't see past on the inside and the standing crowd that would be there tonight in a wall on the outside.

Into the corner. Off the turn. The wing on the inside, the crowd on the outside, the right rein the only way to hold the line.

I stood at the planks and walked it down to the last oxer counting under my breath and got there and turned and looked back up the five at the wing and the rail and the corner, and I knew, the way you know a thing in your stomach before your head's done the sum, that this was the fence. Every course has one. The course builder builds the whole thing to bring you, tired, with the clock on you and the horse spent, to the one place where the horse's worst thing and the round's worst thing are the same place. And here it was. A blind corner. A wing he couldn't see past. A turn that needed the right rein to hold, the right rein that was, to Saracen, not a rein but a memory of pain with no escape. The last fence of the Grand Prix was the inside of his head, built in coloured poles.

I walked the five again. I made it a quiet five — sit up off the planks, bring the canter back a breath, let him read the corner, give him the half-stride. Not a forward five with the line cut. A quiet five with the door held open. It was slower. In the first round it didn't matter; in a jump-off it would. I wasn't thinking about a jump-off. I walked it the way I'd ride it: like I had all the time in the world and the only thing on earth that mattered was that the horse believed me at the corner.

*

"You will want the inside there." 

Tomas had come down the line behind me, unhurried, his own jacket off, machine-fit and blond going grey and pacing the same five with the small economy of a man who'd walked ten thousand of these. He stopped at the planks and looked down at the last oxer the way I'd been looking at it.

"In the second round," he said. "If you are in it. The inside of the corner, off the planks, you cut the turn, you make it a four. It saves you the time and the fence is the same fence." He said it flat, technical, the truth told from one professional to another with nothing in it for him, which was the only kind of thing Tomas ever gave you and was, in that building, worth more than the warmth of people who'd never tell you anything true. "Most will not cut it because of the wing. You cannot see your fence until you are committed. But your horse" — and here he looked down the alley of his own mind toward my box, toward the animal he'd called a lot of horse for an indoor — "your horse jumps off your hand, not his eye. So you can cut it. He will go where you tell him even into the place he cannot see." 

He meant it as a compliment, a craftsman's compliment, the highest thing he had, the same as Caro's *useful,* and it went into me cold, because what he'd just described, kindly, professional to professional, in a sentence with no cruelty anywhere in it, was the whole of what I'd done to the horse all year. *He will go where you tell him even into the place he cannot see.* That was the gift. That was the thing money couldn't buy and Raef wanted to and Caro had taken off me on a handshake. A horse who would go, frightened, into the blind corner, on my word, because he'd decided my word was safe, and was wrong, because my word was leading him into the round that handed him over.

"Aye," I said. "I can cut it."

"You can win it," Tomas said, without weight, the way he'd say the going was deep, and went on down the line, and I stood at the planks and watched him go, the man who'd solved the thing I was breaking on by having no thing in him for it to cost, and I still couldn't decide, the last morning, whether what he had was the wisdom or the wound, and I'd run out of season to decide it in.

*

The studs we did in the alley, the two of us, Glenys holding and me down at the feet, an hour before they'd let us into the collecting ring.

You read the going and you choose. Indoor sand, deep and raked, soft on top with a base that grabs underneath — not the bottomless mud of a wet county show, not the iron of a dry August, but its own thing, a footing that gives and then holds, and you want grip enough that he can turn hard off it into the corners and push off it big, and not so much that he locks a foot into it landing off the oxer and twists himself. I picked the studs out of the tin Glenys had sorted years ago with a careful hand because a lost stud was money, and I chose middling squares for behind and pointed road studs in front, and I knelt in the alley with the stud-tap and the spanner and the little block of grease and I picked up the off-hind first.

Off-hind first. The white coronet there, the one bit of white on the whole near-black of him, the foot the reader of his body learns to watch. I'd done his feet off-hind first since March, since the first morning he let me pick one up without bracing, and I did it off-hind first now, in the worst place I'd ever ask him to hold ground, and I cleaned the thread out of the stud hole with the tap and I screwed the square in and torqued it down and ran my thumb over it to feel it sit, and I did the other hind, and then the fronts, and the whole time he stood for me with one near ear tipped back to where I knelt and the off ear up and hard on the buzzing alley, and Glenys held the lead loose and said nothing, and I gave him the ground.

That's what it is. That's the only way I've ever been able to say it. You give him the ground so he can hold. You put the grip into the body so it won't slip out from under him at the worst second, so when he turns into the blind corner off the planks at the end of the longest two minutes of his life his feet will be under him and not sliding, and that is care, that is nothing but care, a man on his knees in the cold giving a frightened animal a floor he can trust — and it is also the last thing I screwed into him, with my own hands, on the morning, so that he could jump well enough to be taken. Grip so he could hold. Hold so he could jump. Jump so he could go. Care and the other thing in the one turn of the spanner, off-hind first, the spanner cold in my hand.

Glenys watched me run my thumb over the last one.

"You've good hands for it," she said. Which from her was a great deal. And then, after a while, while I packed the tin and wiped the grease off on my jeans, lower, not the warning, she'd kept her word about the warning, but a cousin of it: "I've studded a lot of horses up the morning they went somewhere they weren't coming back from. You'd think it'd be the loading, the ramp, the lorry pulling out. It's not. It's this." She nodded at the tin, the tap, the foot I'd just set down. "It's the last bit of looking after them, while they don't know. You do them up nice. Last time, you do them up nicest." She took the unlit roll-up from behind her ear, looked at it, put it back. "There's lovely it is and there's no word for how cruel, and it's the same job. Go and warm him up, love. Don't think about the tin."

*

I saw Marnie once more before they called us, and it was nothing, and it was the truest thing said to me all morning.

She was at the rail of the practice area, not in the stands, down low where the horses came through to the chute, and she had a coffee going cold in both hands and she wasn't watching the horses, she was watching the chute itself, the mouth of it, the dark gap full of light where you went from the warming-up world into the ring, and I came past leading Saracen back from a leg-stretch and she fell in beside me a few steps, not asking, the way she did, the family ease that costs nothing and assumes everything.

"He's going to be in the chute soon," she said. Not a question. She knew the order; she'd memorised the order; she was the kind of frightened that memorises orders. "It's the worst bit for him, isn't it. Not the fences. The chute. The being funnelled in where he can't see out."

"It's the worst bit," I said. The chute, the wall of standing crowd either side, the place a horse can't see past, the thing he'd run from his whole life. "He'll go in if I go in front of him in his head. If I'm in there ahead of him telling him it's all right."

"And it isn't," she said. Flat. Looking at the chute. "All right."

"No."

She nodded, and drank the cold coffee, and didn't say sorry and didn't say good luck, because she knew that good luck was the cruellest thing you could wish me — that the luck I needed was the round that lost him, that to wish me clear was to wish me bereaved — and she'd worked all that out, the only one in the building who'd worked it out, because she was the only one who'd ever lain awake wanting to be the one that went quiet and understood at last what it does to the one who goes. She didn't forgive me for the thing in July, the thing with Beau, the thing I couldn't give her and couldn't refuse her. She didn't condemn me for it either. She just stood at the rail with her cold coffee and her family bones and let me see that she knew, which was the whole of what she had to give and was more than anyone else gave me that day, and then she said, "I'll be up the top. I'll watch from up the top where I can't hear him," and went, and I didn't understand that, *where I can't hear him,* until later, until the round, when I worked out she meant she'd sit where the crowd was loudest so she wouldn't have to hear the horse breathe, because she was the only person there who'd be listening for it.

*

Caro came to me in the collecting ring at the end, just before, when he was tacked and warmed and standing on a loose rein blowing while the riders ahead of me went, and she came across the churned sand in her one good coat and stood at his shoulder and put her thin ruined hand on his neck, near the scar, the hand that had held a thousand frightened mouths soft, and she didn't look at me, she looked at the horse.

"I bought the engine and never read the rest of the manual," she said, which was the thing she always said about him, the line she'd had since the sale yard years ago, and it had always been a joke, a dealer's shrug, *I bought his looks and got his fear thrown in for free* — and she said it now and it wasn't a joke, it came out of her low and plain looking at the animal she was about to sell, and I understood she was saying goodbye to him, in the only grammar she had, the grammar of horseflesh and value and the deals of fifty years, a woman who'd loved horses her whole long life and sold every one of them and called it the business and had never once been able to tell her own self where the love stopped and the sum started, which is why she could ride me, because she rode me the way she rode everything, with the soft true thing that was also the leg going on.

Then she turned and looked at me, the old hard eyes, and said the thing, and I've turned it over for ten years the way I've turned over everything she ever gave me, and it was the most loving thing anyone said to me that day and it was the round being asked for in the same breath and I cannot, still, get a blade between them.

"Don't ride it for the money," she said. "Don't you dare ride it for the letter or the man in the seats or any of it. You ride it for the horse. You go in there and you give that frightened animal the best round of his life because he deserves one good day in a ring before — " she stopped, the once, the only time I ever saw her stop on the edge of a word — "one good day. You owe him that. Not me. Him. Give him the round, Devlin. Give him the best thing you have."

And it was true. Every word of it was true and was love and was the right thing and was the only thing worth doing — *give him the round, give him the best thing you have* — and it was also, in the exact same sentence, the instruction that finished the sale, because the best round of his life was the round that proved the asset and completed the loss, and she knew that, she had to know that, she'd done the sum on a hundred horses, and she said *give him the best thing you have* and she meant it with the whole of her old loving heart and she meant it, too, as the woman who needed the purse and the man in the seats to see his new horse jump, and there was no daylight between the two, there never was with her, and she patted the horse once on the neck and walked away across the sand to find a place to watch, ramrod, gone to bone, the dynasty in a mended coat, and I stood there with the best and the worst thing anyone said to me all season warm and low where she always put it, and got ready to do exactly what she'd asked.

Iwan I didn't see. He was somewhere in the back of the building, in the green-screen world behind the show, with his bag and his shame and his careful hand, and the three tubes of dark glass were two hours south in a cold steel fridge in Leominster, sealed and dated, the body that never lies kept where it would never speak. The one loaded gun in the whole story sat on its shelf through the Grand Prix and through everything after and never went off.

*

They plaited him in the alley, Glenys and me, ten little plaits down the crest sewn tight with waxed thread, the mane I'd not let anyone hog all year laid over in a neat row, and we squared the rugs and we did the boots, brushing boots behind and tendon boots in front, the running martingale checked and the noseband checked, and I tacked him in the buzzing white with my hands doing the thing they'd done a thousand mornings while the rest of me went somewhere I couldn't follow, and we led him out toward the collecting ring through the alleys of strange horses with his off ear hard on the noise and his near ear coming round to me, and he was beautiful. I'll say it plain because there's no other way to say it. Seven months off a lorry to a sale he was never coming back from, and he came down that alley plaited and gleaming the colour of a wet conker under the lights with the white coronet flashing on the off-hind and the scar pale on the near shoulder, seventeen hands of him, the engine Caro bought, the manual nobody ever got to read, and he was the most beautiful thing in that whole cathedral of beautiful things and the only one in it that didn't know there was a price on him anywhere.

On the bare ply at the end of the alley the new brass headcollar hung where Glenys had hung it, catching the buzzing light, the colour of a thing bought that week. Our old brown one stayed on the box door, behind us now, the one I'd done his first noseband up in. I could see the brass one over his ears as I led him out. They'd hung it where I could see it. I don't think anyone meant for me to. It was just there, on the way to the ring, on the path the horse and I walked toward the thing we'd built all year, hanging on the nail catching the light, the headcollar for after.

I got him to the gate of the collecting ring and stopped and put my hand flat on his neck under the plaited crest, on the warm wet of him, and he'd not gone in yet, the bell hadn't rung, the round in front of him was still only the next thing and not the last thing, and he turned the near ear back to me, off the noise and the lights and the strange roar coming through the wall, and tipped it home, *are you here, is this all right, are you here,* and I was here, my hand flat on his wet neck, and I gave it back to him the way I'd given it back all year, *here, I'm here,* and he blew out, long and low, the flutter, the sound he only ever made for me, and dropped his poll a degree and decided to trust the next thing, the way he always decided to trust the next thing, because I'd taught him that the next thing was safe.

He didn't know. That was the only thing in the whole building that wasn't shared between us. He thought it was a fence. He didn't know it was a door.

I stood at the gate with my hand on his neck and the near ear tipped back to me, and the bell hadn't rung, not yet, and for one more breath nothing in the world had happened, and we waited.


---

# Chapter Twenty-Eight — The Bell

The bell rang for the horse ahead of me, two fences in, a pole gone at the planks with a sound like a dropped scaffold board, and the crowd made the noise a crowd makes when it's glad and sorry at once, and I felt all of that come up through the rail and into my hand on his neck, and he didn't lift his head. He stood with me at the gate with the near ear tipped back and the off ear out at the wall of light and sound where he'd shortly be asked to go, and he was frightened in a way I hadn't felt him frightened since March — frightened down where the words are, the deep old kind, the kind that's not about the fence — and he stood for it anyway, because I was there, and that was the whole of what I had to give him and the whole of what I'd taught him to take.

I want to be plain about the building, because the building was the thing.

A county show is the sky and the rain and a horse can see the whole world and run for the edge of it, which is its own terror but an honest one. This was the opposite. The NEC at night is a box. They build a ring inside a building inside the dark, and they kill the house lights and they put the ring under a white that comes from everywhere and nowhere, so there's no edge, no out, no field at the end of the bolt — just sand and a wall of standing people three and four deep on every side, and over them the dark going up into the roof, and the noise of ten thousand of them coming down off that dark and landing on the sand like weather. For a horse who'd spent his life looking for the open door, there was no door. They'd built him a box the size of a cathedral and called it the Grand Prix.

And he had to go into it through a chute.

*

The collecting ring was its own small hell, churned and floodlit and full of horses worth more than the village I was born in, circling, and the grooms with the practice poles, and the trainers in their good coats putting up a leg for the names while the names took the fence when they wanted it, and I'd waited my turn at it all season and I waited it now, last in everything, the kid from Parson Cross fetching no poles tonight because he'd a horse to keep quiet.

I kept him to the outside, off the press of them, and I worked him the only way I knew, which was off the right rein, soft, give-with-the-give, taking the contact and giving it back before he could meet it, so the right side of his mouth never once found a wall to be frightened of. I rode him forward into nothing and let him land and rode him forward again. I jumped the practice oxer twice, small, then up to a height that didn't matter, and both times three strides out he went the way he went — the stillness coming over him, the blink stopping, the muscle under the right eye starting its small bad work — and both times I waited the half-stride his fear didn't want to give and gave him mine instead, and the near ear came round to me, and he blew out, low, the flutter, and snapped his front legs up neat to his chin and was clean.

Glenys stood at the rail with the lead rope over her shoulder and the unlit roll-up behind her ear and watched us and said nothing, because there was nothing to say that we hadn't already not-said all season, and once, when I brought him back down to a walk on a loose rein to let him blow, she put her hand on his neck near the scar and left it there a second, and took it off, and that was Glenys saying goodbye to him in the only grammar she had, which was the same as Caro's, which was the same as mine.

"He's with you," she said. Not are you ready. Not good luck. "He's right with you, love. He knows it's you up there."

"He knows."

"Then go and let him be the best thing in this building," she said, which was the kindest cruellest thing anyone said to me all night and was the exact thing Caro had said in the same words an hour before, because they were the same woman in the end, all of them, every soul in that building who loved him, telling me with the whole of their hearts to do the thing that handed him over.

The man before me went clear. The crowd came up off the dark. I heard the steward say my number, and the number after it, *D. Okafor-Lyle, Saracen,* over the tannoy in a voice that made it sound like a thing that mattered, and I turned him to the chute.

*

He balked at the mouth of it.

Of course he did. The chute was the worst thing the world had ever built for him on purpose without meaning to — a funnel of crowd, a corridor with walls of people, a place you go in where you can't see out, and at the far end of it the box of light and weather. Every horse in the building hated the chute a little. Saracen hated it the way you hate the thing your whole body was taught to hate before you had words, and he stopped at the mouth of it and went up an inch in front, not a rear, just the thought of one, the question coming up the rein, *no, not in there, there's no out of there,* and the near ear flattened toward the off, going hard, going to the poll, and I felt the whole season tilt on the next two seconds.

You don't drive a horse into a chute. You can't. Drive him and he goes up and over backwards on the concrete behind the seating and that's a dead horse and a dead boy and a thing they put on the news. What you do — what Caro taught me and what I'd have done anyway because it's the only thing — is you go in first. In his head. You take the fear off him and you carry it through the gap yourself and you leave the door open behind you in his mind even though there's no door, and you make him a promise his body believes, *I'm in here ahead of you, it's all right, I've got the dark, come to me,* and it is a lie, and he comes.

I gave him the right rein away entirely, dropped it to the buckle so there was no wall on the bad side at all, and I sat very still and very soft and I let my weight tell him I wasn't going anywhere, and I felt for the half-stride, and he gave it to me — the half-second longer than his fear wanted, the half-second that was the whole of the gift, the half-second that was him deciding, against everything in him, to believe me one more time — and he dropped his poll a degree and walked into the chute on a loose rein like it was a field, the near ear come round to me, the off ear hard on the light at the end, and the crowd in the chute reached out their phones over the rope to film the famous frightened horse and didn't know they were filming the last private thing he'd ever do for free.

I rode him out of the dark and into the ring, and the white came down on us, and the roar landed on the sand, and there it all was — the biggest fences of both our lives, twelve of them, standing under the lights, waiting the way a thing waits that knows you've got to come to it.

I picked up the canter. I found my circle. The judge's box sat at the corner with the bell in it, and I rode him toward the start with my eyes already gone up the first line, and somewhere in the dark above the wall of people Caro was watching and Raef was watching his new horse and Marnie was sitting where the crowd was loudest so she wouldn't have to hear him breathe, and none of that was in the ring with us. Only the sand was in the ring with us. Only the sand and the lights and the half-ton of frightened animal who'd decided I was safe, and was wrong.

The bell went.

*

Forty-five seconds. The clock not yet on, the start line a strip of nothing between two flags, and the first fence already coming up out of the corner of my eye the way they do.

I'll tell it the way it happened, because I've ridden it ten thousand times since in the dark behind my eyes and it never gets shorter and it never gets kinder and I wouldn't have it do either.

I circled him once, sat him down a touch, felt the canter come under me — big and uphill and careful, the canter of a horse who's about to spend something he hasn't got — and I turned him for the start and crossed the line and the clock came on, and we were in it.

First line, the forward six down the long side, two big square oxers with a flat gallop between, the kind of distance that punishes a held breath. I rode the first one off a stride that came up sweet, and he left the ground a stride sooner than feels right at 1.60, the way you have to, and snapped up neat to his chin and floated the spread and came down galloping, and I sat very still and rode the six like it wasn't there, didn't fiddle, didn't count out loud, let his stride do the arithmetic, and the second oxer came up exactly where the legs said it would and he jumped it clean and careful, the back rail rocking in the cups behind us and staying, that sound, the no-sound, the held breath of the whole building.

The dogleg to the liverpool. Blue tray, dead under the rail, the thing that spooks them down. He saw it three strides out and I felt him want to back off it, the off ear coming half-round off the fence to ask, and I rode the blue like it was sky, didn't change a thing, didn't let my hand tell him there was anything to fear, and he believed my hand over his own eye, which is the gift, which is the thing, and stood off it and cleared it big and clean, a foot more than it needed, the careful horse putting daylight under himself the way a horse does who learned that touching is punished.

The wall. Light blocks, painted to look like stone, the thing he can't see past, and he jumped it the way he jumped everything tonight, careful and apologetic and clean, and I heard the crowd start to come up under us because they'd seen the round now, they'd seen what he was, and I rode him on through their noise into the treble.

The treble was the cruel one, the one I'd stood at on foot the morning before and known was a question. A to B to C, one stride and then two, the distance in built a half-stride short, the course builder asking *can you bring him back without killing the impulsion, can you sit him down and still send him.* You come at a treble at 1.60 with a horse's blood up and your job is to do the impossible double thing — hold and send in the same breath, sit him on his hocks so he can fit the short distance and not lose the engine that gets him out the far side. Get it wrong and a horse loses his nerve in the middle of a combination and a horse who's lost his nerve in the middle of a combination is a horse who's gone, and a refusal at B sends you back through A, and a second refusal anywhere ends you.

I sat up off the wall and brought the canter back a breath, just a breath, and met A out of a stride I could live with, and he jumped in careful, and I caught him in the one stride to B and let nothing change in my hands, *here, I'm here, the next one's safe,* and he jumped B off the short half-stride exactly the way it needed, sitting back on himself, neat, and then the two strides to C with the engine I'd held for him still there underneath, and he snapped up over C and we were out the far side of the treble clear, and the crowd broke over us like a wave coming off the dark, and I had four fences left and the worst of them last and I knew it.

*

I want to slow it here, the way it slowed for me, because this is the part of the round I've never been able to give anyone and have stopped trying to, the same as the gift, the same as everything I can't say about him.

Coming round the top end off the planks, into the last line, the canter blowing now, the round nearly two minutes long and his courage nearly two minutes spent, I turned him down the short side toward the final oxer — and the final oxer sat off a related five strides past the planks, into the corner, with the wing he couldn't see past on the inside and the wall of standing crowd on the outside, and the only way to hold the line into it was the right rein.

The right rein. To Saracen, not a rein. A memory of a hand that hauled and held on a curb and gave no out, pain on the right with no door, the thing somebody before Tatefield put into him so deep that Iwan said it doesn't come out, it's in there now, he'll throw his head off the right rein the rest of his life. And the course builder, who never met the horse, who built clever and not big and asked a hard question of everyone, had built the last fence of the Grand Prix in the one place in the ring that was the inside of Saracen's own head — a blind corner, a wing he couldn't see past, a turn that needed the right rein to hold, the right rein that was, to him, the whole of his fear in coloured poles.

I felt him find it three strides out.

He went still. The blink stopped. And under the right eye the muscle started its small terrible work, the flicker, the thing everyone else in his life had read as *he's about to be a sod* and I'd read, since the box in March, as the last second he was still asking — *do I have to do this on my own, or are you here.*

It came up the rein, the question, his whole body asking it, the near ear wavering between coming round to me and flattening back to the poll, his weight starting to shift onto the left where the open door was, where the run-out lived, where two pupils had gone through a wall before me — and the crowd was a roar on the outside and the wing was a blind wall on the inside and the corner was the inside of his head, and he asked me, frightened down where the words are, the deep old kind: *is it safe. is the right rein safe. are you here.*

And I answered it.

I gave him the right rein soft, soft, took the wall off the bad side, showed him the door isn't shut, and I waited the half-stride his fear didn't want to give — and he gave it to me. He waited the half-beat longer than his terror, for me, and dropped his poll, and turned into the blind corner on my word into the place he could not see, and met the oxer out of the quiet five I'd walked for him the morning before, all the time in the world, the door held open, and left the ground.

And I felt it, in the half-second he was in the air over the last fence of the Grand Prix, the thing I'd felt coming all season and never let land. His trust and my use of it were the one act, the same motion of the same hands. He was clearing the fence that finished him on the half-stride I'd taught him to give me, and it was the most beautiful thing I have ever done with my hands and the worst, and I have never since been able to get a blade between the two.

He snapped up neat to his chin, apologetic, careful, the form of a horse hit for touching poles, and put a foot of daylight he didn't need over the back rail, and came down clean on the far side of the last fence, and I sat down and rode him through the finish.

The clock stopped.

Clear.

*

The building came apart.

I've heard crowds. I'd heard Yorkshire, my first, the small good roar of a county Saturday that I thought at nineteen was the loudest love the world had. This wasn't that. This was ten thousand people in the dark coming up onto their feet at once, the noise of it landing on the sand like a roof falling in slow, and they were on their feet for him, for the frightened black horse who'd jumped the inside of his own head on the word of a kid from a council estate, and they didn't know any of that, they just knew they'd seen something true, and they were right, they had.

I rode him round on a loose rein with my hand flat on his neck under the plaited crest, on the warm wet of him going dark with sweat, and he was blowing, great long blows, the sides of him going like a bellows, and the near ear came round to me off the wall of noise — *is it over, is it all right, are you here* — and I gave it back to him the way I'd given it back all year, *over, it's over, I'm here, you were the best thing in the building,* and he believed me, and dropped his poll a degree, and walked, spent, the borrowed courage all spent now, sweat-dark from poll to flank, the colour of a wet conker gone the colour of something darker, the white coronet on the off-hind flashing once as he passed under the lights, the one bit of white on the whole near-black of him, the foot I'd done up first since March.

I looked up the once.

Caro was at the rail. She had her hands half-closed the way she always had them, the loose ring hidden, the mended coat, the spine that wouldn't bend, and her face was doing the thing her face did that I have spent ten years failing to read — the water standing in the old hard eyes, real, I'd swear it was real, the bravest riding she'd seen since she stopped being the one doing it — and under the water, woven through it so you could not pull the two apart, the other thing, the sum coming right, the asset proved, the purse and the price and the letter, end of October, the whole season's arithmetic landing clean in the same wet eyes that loved him.

And further along, in the members' end, very still, in a coat that cost nothing and meant everything, Raef Al-Mazroui watched his horse come back from the best round of its life, and he didn't cheer, and he didn't move, he just watched the way a man watches a thing he's been right about, courteous, exact, the most honest man in the building about what people are worth, and he gave me, when I came past, one nod. A man marking a thing complete.

I knew what the nod was. The clear round was the last bit of value they needed to put on him, and I'd just put it there, with my own hands, on the half-stride, in front of the man who already owned him. The crowd was on its feet because they couldn't tell the difference, and I couldn't either, not in any way that would let me stop.

*

They were still on their feet when the tannoy said it.

The voice came down through the noise, glad, official, the voice that makes a thing sound like it matters: a number of clears, the names of them, *and through to the jump-off* — and mine in among them, and Tomas Berg's, the clean professional whose round would cost him nothing, and a handful of others, the best in the building, coming back for the shortened course against the clock.

The jump-off. The bell would ring again.
