I rode him out through the chute, into the dark, into the press of crowd reaching their phones over the rope, and the moment we were out of the ring and back in the small floodlit hell of the collecting area the noise dropped behind us like a door shut on it, and it was just the two of us again, him blowing under my hand, the steam coming off him into the cold caught light, the near ear come round to me.

Glenys was at the rail with a sweat sheet over her shoulder and her face set the way she sets it. She didn't say well done. She put her hand up to his cheek, the off cheek, the frightened side, and he let her, the way he only ever let her, and she held it there.

"Clear," she said, low. Not to me. To the horse. "Clear, you daft brave bastard." And then she did look at me, and the look had the pity in it that her look had always had, the look that read me from the first day not for what I could do but for what was going to be done to me, and she said, "He's spent, love. Look at him. He's give you the lot."

He had. I could feel it under me, the engine run nearly to nothing, the courage all borrowed and all spent, two minutes of overriding the oldest thing in him, and the jump-off was the shortened raised course against the clock and the inside lines and the cut corners and the wall a second time, and to win it — to actually win it, to beat Tomas, who felt nothing and so could risk everything — I'd have to take the inside turn into that same blind corner off the planks and ask him to leave the ground a stride sooner than his fear could bear, on the right rein, in the place that was the inside of his own head, at speed, with the last of what he had left, which was almost nothing.

I'd have to ask him for the last of it.

I sat there in the steam and the caught light with my hand flat on his wet neck, and the near ear was still tipped back to me, listening, trusting, not knowing — not knowing this was a door and not a fence, not knowing the win was the loss, not knowing the brass headcollar hung where I could see it on the lorry, the colour of a thing bought that week — and somewhere above us in the dark a steward was raising the wall and cutting the course and the building was settling back into its seats to watch the best of them come back and go to war with the clock, and the floor of dread opened up under the highest moment of my life and I stood on it, and he blew out, long and low, the flutter, the sound he only ever made for me, and waited for me to tell him the next thing was safe.

The bell would ring again. And I would answer the flicker one more time, because I always had, because I'd taught him I always would, and because there was no longer anything in me that knew how not to.


---

# Chapter Twenty-Nine — The Jump-Off

I walked him slow in the collecting ring with the sweat sheet on him and the steam coming up off his quarters into the floodlight, and I let him blow, and I did the sum.

Not the money sum. The other one. The one I'd been doing all night without letting myself see the working. How much was left in him. You can't read it off a clock or a scale; you read it off the body, the way you read everything, off the depth of the blow and the give in the rein and the heat coming up through the sweat sheet into your knuckles, off whether the near ear still came round to you when a horse two boxes over let out a squeal — and it did, it still came round, but slower, and it stayed less long, and his head was lower than it should have been for a horse with another round to jump. There was almost nothing left in him. The body told me, the way it always told me, and I made myself read it.

Six of us through. Six clears come back, which in a 1.60m Grand Prix at the cathedral of the sport with the wall built into the corner is a small brave number, and meant the course builder had got it exactly right, clever and not big, asked a question only the best could answer and only six of them had. I knew most of the six off the lorry park and off the warm-up all season — a Dutchman on a chestnut machine, a girl of twenty-two on a horse her father's sponsor had bought her for the price of a house, two I'd watched and beaten and been beaten by between Hartpury and here — and Tomas Berg, who'd gone clear the way Tomas Berg went clear, which is to say without a hair out of place, without a question asked of the horse or answered, a clean professional doing a clean professional thing to a body that had nothing in it to override because he'd never once put anything in it that he'd have to.

Tomas walked his horse past me in the steam, loosening the girth a hole, and he stopped, which he didn't have to do, and looked at my horse, not at me, the way the good ones look at a horse.

"That was the best round I've watched this year," he said. No warmth in it and no lie in it either, which is the thing about Tomas, the thing I could never get a blade between. "The treble. You sat him on his hocks and still had the engine out the back. Most people grab. You didn't grab."

"He's a good horse."

"He's a frightened horse," Tomas said, mild, technical, no ego in it. "I watched him at the chute. I watched him at the last. You ride a frightened horse like it's the easiest thing in the world." He looked at me then, the once, the pale unbothered eyes that had subtracted the ache and kept the skill, the man who'd never let a horse spend its courage on *him* because he'd never asked one to. "I couldn't do what you do," he said. "I've tried. There's nothing in me it can come out of."

He said it like a man reporting the going. Soft on top, firm underneath. And he didn't mean it as a kindness and it landed as the cruellest thing anyone said to me all night, because he was telling me, accurately, professional to professional, what the gift cost and where it came from, and he was right, and he'd worked out years ago that the way to never pay was to be a colder man, and he'd done it, and he won and won and won and felt nothing in the half-stride, and standing there in the steam with my hand on a spent horse I could not tell you, then or now, whether what Tomas had done to himself was wisdom or whether it was the same thing a man does to a horse with a curb, held long enough that the flinch goes out and you call what's left calm.

He clicked his tongue and walked his horse on. His horse went with him the way a thing goes with a man it has no opinion about. They were going to be very hard to beat.

*

Glenys came to his head with the stud spanner and the look she sets her face to.

"They've cut it cruel," she said. She'd walked it, or had it walked to her — she always knew the track before I did. "Off the planks, inside turn to the wall, then the dogleg back to the last, and they've left the last where it was. In the corner. On the right rein." She didn't look up from the off-hind, working the spanner, swapping the mud studs out for the indoor going. "You don't have to take the inside, love. There's an outside line round to the wall loses you a second and a bit. You go round the long way, you give him the corner with room, you jump a clean four-fault-free careful round, you're second or third, the cheque's near enough the same off a place at this level, and he never has to go near the inside of his own head at speed."

"Tomas'll take the inside."

"Tomas's horse doesn't care," she said. "That's the whole of it, isn't it. That's why he'll beat you fair. His'll do it because nothing in it minds, and yours'll do it because you ask, and only one of those is a thing you should be allowed to spend." She set the off-hind down and stood up, slow, her wrecked back, and put the spanner in her tabard pocket and looked at me across the horse's quarters in the caught light, and the pity was in her face that had been in it since the first day, the look that read me not for what I could do but for what was being done to me. "He's give you the round of his life," she said. "He's give you the round of *anyone's* life. That's enough. You don't have to take the last of him to win a thing that was already yours the second you went clear. Let it be enough."

She'd said the true thing once, in June, on the bad day — *they'll have the horse off you, love, and they'll have you and all, and they'll do it kind, so you'll thank them* — and she'd not said it twice, because she had her own caravan and her own old age and her own thirty years sunk in this drowning place, and she handed me the muck fork every morning and mucked out beside me and was complicit in the whole con she was the only one to name. And here she was saying the second true thing of the season, the one that came after, the one that was the only mercy left in the building: *let it be enough, don't take the last of him.* And she meant it. And she knew, the same as I knew, that I wasn't going to.

Because the cheque off a place was not near enough the same, not when the letter said end of October and a number with a comma in it, and the purse off the win and what Raef was giving for him together cleared the letter and a place did not, and we both knew the arithmetic of the yard better than we knew our own birthdays. And because Tomas would take the inside and beat me by a second on a horse that didn't care, and I had spent the whole season learning that I would rather be bled white than be the one who didn't do the thing, the one who came second, the one who wasn't enough.

And because of the worst reason, the one underneath the money and the pride, the one I've had ten years to look at and still can't look at straight: that I wanted to. That somewhere down in me, in the place the gift comes out of, there was a thing that wanted to ride the inside line into the blind corner and ask him for the last of it and feel him give it, because it was the most alive I'd ever been in my life, the half-second when a frightened animal decides to trust you against everything in itself, and I was an addict of that half-second, and I had built my whole life on getting it, and I could not stop.

"I'm taking the inside," I said.

Glenys looked at me a moment longer. Then she pulled the sweat sheet off him in one motion, folded it over her arm, and ran her hand down the wet of his neck, the near side, over the scar, the hand's-width of pale hairless skin nobody could ever explain, and she said, not to me, "Go on then, Sass. Last one." And to me, flat, the roll-up behind her ear: "Mind the wall the second time. He'll know it now. He'll be looking for it before you turn."

*

There were four to jump before me. I stood him at the rail in the half-dark of the collecting end with my reins long and my hand flat on his neck and I watched two of them and then I stopped watching, because watching does nothing but put another horse's stride into your hands, and I needed only his.

The Dutchman went and had the wall — the second time round, exactly where Glenys said, the horse looking for it before the turn and backing off and catching it with a hind, the pole gone, the four faults, the crowd's soft sorry sound. The girl of twenty-two went fast and clean and put up a time. Then Tomas went.

I didn't watch and I heard it anyway, you can hear a round you can't see, you can read it off the crowd the way you read a horse off its breath — the rhythm of it, the gasps where he cut inside, the held silence over each fence and the release after, and the particular quality of the roar at the end that tells you whether it was fast. It was fast. It was very fast. The noise came up off the dark at the finish and stayed up, and the tannoy said the time, and the collecting ring did the small ugly thing collecting rings do, the grooms and the riders all hearing the same number and all doing the same sum, and the sum was: he's left it nearly impossible. Tomas had taken every inside line and left every stride out he could leave and his horse had galloped flat at 1.60 and not turned a hair, and to beat it I would have to take the inside to the wall and the inside to the last and leave a stride out somewhere a careful man fits two, on a horse with almost nothing left, into the corner that was the inside of his own head.

I gathered him up.

He came up under me off the long rein and I felt the engine, what was left of it — not much, the canter shorter than it should be, the blow not fully back in him, but there, his, the big uphill careful canter of a horse who'd spent two minutes overriding the oldest thing in himself and would, if I asked, spend the rest. The near ear came round to me as I shortened the reins and the off ear went out to the chute, and I rode him at the chute and he didn't balk this time — he'd done it once, I'd held the dark for him once, and he believed the dark was held now, which was the gift working at its most complete and its most terrible, that I'd taught him a lie so well he no longer needed me to tell it, he told it to himself in my voice — and he walked into the funnel of crowd on a loose right rein with his poll dropped and the near ear home on me, and we came out into the white.

The roar landed. The wall of standing people. The dark going up into the roof. And the shortened course laid out under the lights, the wall raised, the corner waiting, the last fence sitting where it had sat the first time, in the one place in the ring that was the inside of his head.

The judge's box at the corner. The bell in it.

I rode him round to the start with my eyes already up the first turn, and I gave him the right rein soft once, a question of my own, *are you here,* and he gave it back, the half-stride, the flutter, low, the sound he only ever made for me, and I said *here* with my hands and my weight and the whole of me, *here, last one, we go,* and I turned him for the line.

The bell went.

*

Forty-five seconds. The clock came on the instant I crossed the flags and from that instant the clock was the second opponent and the first was the corner.

I'm going to tell it the way it was, fast, because it was fast, because that's the whole of a jump-off, you spend a season's craft in under a minute and the minute is the smallest and largest thing you'll ever live inside.

First fence off a tight turn, an oxer, and I cut the corner to it harder than I'd dare on a fresh horse and met it on a half-stride short and he sat back and squeezed up over it neat, the careful horse, the apologetic snap of the front legs, clean, and we were galloping before we landed. Inside turn to the second, a vertical, and I asked him to come back and turn in the air and he turned for me, the near ear flicking, the body doing the thing I asked a beat before his fear could vote on it, and met it and cleared it, and now the wall was coming, the wall the second time, and Glenys's voice was in me, *he'll be looking for it before you turn.*

He was looking for it. I felt it come into him off the planks, two fences out, before I'd even made the turn — the head wanting to come up, the weight wanting to drift left toward the open, the wall sitting up ahead of him the way a remembered thing sits, *that, the one I can't see past, you're taking me at that again* — and the inside line meant turning him tight inside the wing and meeting the wall off a short bold stride with no time to show it to him, no half-stride to give, no field to promise, just *go,* just the boy's word and the wall and the clock.

And I had a choice, in the half-second on the turn, the choice Glenys had laid in front of me in the steam: the outside line was still there, even now, two strides of drift and I'd have him round the long way with room to see the wall and time to give him the half-stride and a clean careful four-fault-free round and a place and a horse who never had to take the wall blind at speed. The door was open. It was open the whole way down the turn. I felt it open under my left hand the way the run-out had always been open, the place he napped to, the place the fear lived, the easy door, *go round, go the long way, let it be enough.*

I shut it. I shut the easy door with my left leg and my weight and turned him inside the wing at the wall, blind, at speed, and asked him for the bold short stride into the thing he couldn't see past, and three strides out, in the place there wasn't time for it, he gave me the flicker.

*

He went still. At a gallop, into a corner, two strides from a wall, the half-ton of him going still under me the way still water goes over a weir before it falls — the blink stopping, the breath held, and under the right eye the muscle started its small terrible work, the tic, the flicker, the thing every other person who ever sat on him read as *he's about to be a sod* and I'd read since a bolted box in March as the last second the horse was still asking.

And he asked. He asked it the way he'd asked it all his frightened life, the whole body asking, the near ear wavering between coming home to me and flattening hard to the poll, the weight loading onto the left where the open door still was, the right rein live and full of the old curb and the old hand and the old pain-with-no-out, and the question came up the rein into my hand clearer than anything anyone said to me in that whole loud building, clear as a child's: *do I have to do this on my own, or are you here. is the right rein safe. is the corner safe. I can't see past it. are you here.*

And this is the part I've never given anyone, the part I stopped trying to give, the same as the gift, the same as everything I can't say about him — that I knew, in that half-second, with his fear coming up the rein into my hand and the wall two strides off and the clock running and the man who already owned him watching from the dark, I knew exactly what I was doing, and I did it anyway, and I did it perfectly.

I gave him the right rein soft. Took the wall off the bad side. Showed him the door wasn't shut even as I was the one shutting it. And I waited the half-stride — the half-stride his fear didn't want to give, the half-stride that was him overriding the oldest truest thing in him because a boy on his back had taught him the boy's word was the floor of the world — and I asked him, with the whole of the gift, soft, certain, *here, I'm here, it's safe, give it to me,* and he gave it. He gave me the half-stride. He waited the half-beat longer than his terror, *for me,* and dropped his poll, and turned into the blind corner on my word at a gallop into the place he could not see, and met the wall off the bold short stride and left the ground.

And I felt it. Not the way it landed at the first round, coming up at me out of nowhere — this time I felt it the way you feel a thing you've already decided to feel, which is worse. That his trust was real and my use of it was real and they were the one act, the same motion of the same hands, the giving and the taking the same gift, and there had never been a line, and the worst of it, the thing that finally got the blade in under everything, was that it cost me nothing. I asked the most frightened animal I have ever known to override the deepest fear in his body at a gallop into a corner he couldn't see, on the rein that was the seat of all his pain, to win a class that would hand him to another man — and my hand did not shake, and there was no cold place that opened under the warm, and no muscle in me flinched where it used to, because the flinch had gone out of me somewhere months back in the dark, the way feeling goes out of a scar, and I rode it costless and perfect and I knew it was costless and I did it anyway, and that, the costing-nothing, was the thing that I have never since been able to put down.

He cleared the wall by a foot he didn't have to give and didn't need to and gave anyway, the careful horse, the form of a horse hit for touching poles, and came down galloping on the inside line with one fence left.

*

The last.

Off the wall, the short dogleg, into the corner, the last fence on the right rein, and the clock and Tomas's time both screaming the same word, *inside, inside, leave the stride out,* and I took it. God help me, I took the inside turn to the last as tight as the wing would let me and asked him to leave the long stride out where a careful man fits two, one big bold gallop-stride into the corner that was the inside of his head, the right rein live, no time, no field, no half-stride to give, just go — and he was empty, the engine run to nothing, the courage all borrowed and all spent twice over — and I asked him anyway, for the last of it, for the very last of what was in him, and he found it. From somewhere a frightened spent horse should have had nothing left in, he found one more half-stride to wait, one more half-beat of trusting the boy over his own body, and he stood off the last on a stride that should have buried us and didn't, and threw his heart over it, and snapped his front legs up neat to his chin, apologetic, careful, the last clean fence of his life under my hands, and came down on the far side of it clear, and I sat down and rode him through the finish.

The clock stopped.

I didn't look at it. I didn't have to. I read it off the crowd the way I read everything, off the noise that came up off the dark and didn't stop, the noise that has only one shape, and I knew before the tannoy that it was faster than Tomas, that we'd beaten the clean professional and his horse that didn't care, that the kid from Parson Cross had won the Grand Prix at the Horse of the Year Show on a condemned horse three months off a lorry to a sale he was never coming back from, the dream of a wet March mile downhill made flesh under the lights, and the building came up onto its feet for him, ten thousand of them in the dark, on their feet for the frightened black horse who'd jumped the inside of his own head twice on a boy's word.

We'd won.

And winning was the loss. The clear round was the last value they needed to put on him, the proof of the asset finished, the price tag made flesh, and I'd put it there myself, with my own hands, on the half-stride, in front of the man who already owned him — every careful clean fence of the fastest round of my life raising the tag and completing the sale and spending the very last of his borrowed courage on the man taking him away. To have ridden the perfect round was to have handed him over finished. I had got the thing I came for, and getting it was losing him, the win and the loss the same motion of the same hands, both at once, and no daylight between them, and there never would be again.

I let him come back to a canter and then a walk and I put my hand flat on his neck under the plaited crest, on the warm wet of him gone dark from poll to flank, and he was blowing, great long blows, the whole body going like a bellows, the last of him spent and the last of him still listening, and the near ear came round to me off the wall of noise, *is it over, is it all right, are you here,* and I gave it back to him the way I'd given it back since March, the way I'd give it back if I had it to do again, which is the worst thing I know about myself, *over, it's over, I'm here, you were the best thing in the building,* and he believed me, and dropped his poll a degree, and walked.


---

# Chapter Thirty — Clear Round

He walked, and I rode him on a loose rein round the inside of the ring with the noise coming down off the dark like weather, and I did the thing you do at the end of the best round of your life, which is wave. I lifted my hat to the standing thousands and they answered the way crowds answer, a wall of sound that comes at you and is, for about four seconds, the most loved you will ever be by people who will not remember your name by Christmas. And under me the horse was blowing, the great long bellows-blows, sweat-dark from poll to flank gone near black, and the near ear came round to me off the noise and stayed, *is it over, are you here,* and I gave it back with my hand on his neck, and I did not, for those four seconds, think about the lorry.

Then the lorry came back.

It came back the way it always does, not as a thought but as a thing in the corner of the eye. The collecting-ring gate. The dark beyond the lights where the boxes were. A man standing just inside the gate in a stable jacket I didn't know, navy, clean, with a brass-buckled headcollar over one shoulder and a lead rope coiled in his hand like a man who has waited at a lot of ramps, and he was not looking at the crowd. He was looking at the horse. He had the patient unhurried look of a man whose employer is never in a hurry, and his employer was three rows up in the dark, and I knew without turning that Raef was on his feet like everyone else, because correct is correct, and a man stands for a horse like that, even a horse that is already his.

*

They give you a lap of honour and then they take it off you.

The steward brought me into the line-up first, on the centre line, the way they do, the winner at the head of it and the placed horses fanned out behind, the rosettes already cut and waiting in a flat box — and you'd not believe the size of the thing they hang on the winner of the Grand Prix at the Horse of the Year Show, a great rosette the breadth of a dinner plate, red and gold, the tails of it down past my boot. A girl in show kit clipped it to the browband and Saracen flinched at the rustle of it by his eye and I gave him the rein and he stood. He stood for the cameras. The lights came up white and a man with a microphone said the kid's name and where he was from, *Parson Cross, Sheffield,* into the roof, and the crowd did the thing crowds do with that, the warm roar with the knowing in it, the council-estate boy on the condemned horse, and I sat there on half a ton of spent animal with a dinner plate of ribbon at his eye and a sky of strangers loving the shape of the story, and I thought, plainly, the way you think things at the worst moments: *they have got the whole of it wrong, and they have got the whole of it right.*

Then a man in a good suit came across the sand with an envelope. Not the headcollar man. The sponsor's man, the cheque, the photograph of the cheque — they print one the size of a door, a comedy cheque, you've seen them, with the number on it in a font, and the number on it had a comma in it and then another comma, and I had spent the whole season knowing what number would clear the letter and this was bigger than that, this was the letter and the rest, and I held one end of the daft enormous board and the sponsor held the other and we smiled at the camera, and somewhere in the dark Caro Wexley-Tate was watching the exact number that meant the Hall would have its heating on this winter and the farrier would be paid and the breeze blocks could come off the tarp at last, watching the yard not die, on the body of the horse who'd earned it twice over — once by being worth a fortune to the man at the gate, and once, this minute, by jumping the inside of his own head faster than a clean professional on a horse that didn't care.

I read her face for it before I found her. You read the body, you read where the tension goes, and I found Caro at the rail by the collecting gate, brought down out of the stands by a steward because that's where the owner of the winner stands, and her shoulders had come down off her ears, all the way down, the way they had not been down since March, the fifty-year clench of a woman managing decline with perfect posture finally let off the bone — and there was joy in it, real joy, you can't fake the way the face goes when a thing you've held up for forty years stops being about to fall — and there was relief in it, the animal relief of the drowning when the water goes back down past the chin. And woven through both, so I could not pull it out, the third thing, the thing I'd been failing to pull out of her face since the gold-cracked cup: a sum coming right. The owner's relief. The asset proved at the top of the market on the very day of delivery. I stood on her winning horse and I read her face the way I read everything and the read came back what it always came back on her — joy and leverage in the one body, no daylight between them, both true, and which she felt most I could not tell you then and cannot tell you now.

She caught me reading her. She did the thing she did. She lifted her chin a degree and her ruined beautiful hand came up off the rail, the signet ring loose on the thin finger, and she gave me, across forty feet of trampled sand and ten thousand strangers, a small private nod — *well done, boy* — and I felt it go into me warm and low the way it always went, before I could vote on it, *you can feel it, the small quiet ones, you,* and I was nineteen and I was, God help me, still glad. Still glad she was pleased. After everything. That's the wound. You don't get over the wound by understanding it. I'd understood it for months. I was still glad.

*

They led the placed horses out and they did not lead Saracen out, because the winner stays for the press, and then the press were done and there was nowhere left to stand and be the winner, and the moment came that comes, the moment a yard knows in its bones and a crowd never sees, which is the quiet after, when the lights stay up but the show moves on to the next class and your great red rosette is just a thing flapping at a tired horse's eye and you are, suddenly, only a boy on a sweating animal that is no longer doing anything anyone is watching.

Glenys was at the gate.

She had the old headcollar. Not the brass one. The old soft brown leather one she'd oiled and aired the week before, and she came across the sand to his head the way she'd come across a hundred yards of yard to a hundred horses, square and slow on her wrecked back, and she did not look at the rosette and she did not look at the cheque-shaped men, she looked at his off-hind first, the way she always did, to see how he'd come out of it, whether the leg had stood the going, and then she looked at his eye, and then she put her hand flat on his neck on the wet of him over the scar and she said, low, not to me, "There's a boy. There's a good boy, then," in the soft exasperated Welsh she'd named him in for seven years, *Sass,* the name I never used, and the near ear came off me and went to her, the once, because she was the body he'd settled next to longer than he'd settled next to me, she was March and before March for him, she was the whole drowning place, and then it came back to me, the near ear, and stayed.

"Get down, love," she said. "I've got him."

I didn't want to get down.

I want to be honest about that, the way I've made myself be honest about the rest. I did not want to get down off him, because getting down was the last thing, and a body knows the last thing before the mind will say it, and my legs would not, for a second, do it. I sat on him in the emptying ring with my hand on the rein and the crowd's attention gone off us like a tide and Glenys's hand on his neck and the navy man waiting at the gate with the brass headcollar, and I did the sum one more time, the only sum I ever cared about, *how much is left in him,* and the answer came up off the depth of the blow and the heat through the wet and the slow give in the rein the way it always came, off the body, never off a clock, never off a scale — and the answer was *nothing, you've had it all, there is nothing left in him and there is nothing left in you* — and I got down.

*

You do not get a scene.

That's the thing nobody tells you. You build a whole season toward a thing and you think the end of it will be a scene, a row, a moment where somebody says the true thing and somebody answers it and the floor breaks open. There was no scene. There was a tired horse and a brass headcollar and a list of small kind competent jobs to be done in order, and the doing of them in order is how a thing like this actually ends, with everybody being very gentle and nobody saying anything, which is the cruellest way there is and the only way they know.

Glenys put the old soft headcollar on over the bridle and I took the bridle off underneath it, the way you do, and the rosette came off the browband with it and she folded the great red-and-gold thing once and put it in her tabard pocket without a word, *the yard keeps the silver,* and somewhere a long way off, behind a screen at the back of my head, I thought of a cabinet of silver behind a long window and a tarp lashed down with baler twine, and how the silver was about to get a new piece, and how I would never see it in the cabinet because I would not be there, and I put that away too, in the place I put things now, which had got very large that year.

The navy man came across the sand. He was decent. They're nearly always decent, the men who lead horses away; it's the one job in the whole concern that has no unkindness available in it, you just hold the rope and walk. He nodded to Glenys, groom to groom, and he nodded to me, and he said, "Lovely round," and meant it, and held out his hand for the lead rope of the old soft headcollar, which was, for the length of the walk to the ramp, still Glenys's rope and still the yard's horse, and would be the brass one's the second the brass one went on at the bottom of the ramp.

And here is where they were kind, because they are always kind, it's how you thank them.

"You bring him out," Glenys said to me. To me. She handed me the rope of the old brown headcollar and she stepped back, her face set to the thing she sets it to, the walnut face, the roll-up behind the ear, thirty years of leading horses to lorries they weren't coming back from, and she gave me the last of it, the walk, on purpose, as a gift, because she was the only one in the building who knew it was the last thing I'd want and the only thing I'd want, both, the way I'd wanted not to get down. She gave me the rope to lead him out myself. *They'll do it kind, so you'll thank them.* I nearly thanked her. I caught it in my teeth.

*

So I led him.

Out of the ring, into the dark of the collecting end, down the long rubber-matted walkway between the temporary boxes under the buzzing strip lights, past the other horses with their heads over the doors and the grooms with their buckets, the whole backstage machine of the show going on around the one small private ending, and I led him on a loose rope with the near ear tipped back to me the whole way, *where are we going, is it all right, are you here,* and I said *here* with the rope and my walk and didn't say it out loud because Marnie was at the corner of the walkway and Pip was beside her.

They'd come down. Of course they'd come down. Marnie in last year's good coat with her thumbnail at her mouth, bitten raw, and Pip beside her, sunburnt even in October, her broad kind face doing nothing to hide what was on it, which was that she'd loved this all season from the cheap side of it and come anyway and would carry my bucket to the lorry now if I let her and I never once, the whole year, let her know I'd seen it. Pip put her hand on the horse's shoulder, the near side, over the scar, without thinking, the way you touch a horse you've mucked out for seven months, and her chin went, and she took the hand back and wiped it on her jeans like it was wet, and said, "I'll get your stuff up to the box," and went, fast, before her face finished doing the thing, and that was Pip, that was the cleanest love in the whole building leaving so I wouldn't have to watch it, and I have thought about Pip Sallow more in ten years than she will ever know, and I never told her that either.

Marnie didn't move out of the walkway.

She stood square in it so I had to stop the horse, and she looked at him, not at me, the off ear coming round to her, the near ear staying home on me, and she said, "He's frightened." Not a question. She'd learned to read him that much, off me, off the season, the one thing I had taught her in the end without meaning to, that a horse can stand quiet and be frightened and you can tell, if you know where it lives. "He's frightened and he's standing here because you're on the end of the rope."

"He's tired," I said.

"That's not what I said." She looked at me then. White around the mouth, the family bones, seventeen and outclassed and the only person in that whole loud building who knew exactly what the round had cost, because she'd spent a whole season wanting to be the one who could make a horse go quiet and she understood now, the way I understood, what going quiet did to the one who went. "You're going to walk him onto that lorry," she said, "and he's going to walk on because you ask him to. Because you told him the dark was held." Her voice didn't rise. She'd learned that off her grandmother, the not-rising. "And then you're going to give the rope to that man and walk back here. And he won't understand why the floor went."

I didn't say anything. There wasn't a thing to say that wasn't a lie, and I'd run out, that night, of the strength to tell the kind ones.

"I'm not being cruel," she said, and her thumbnail went back up to her mouth, and I saw it for what it was, finally, the cruelty that's the truest love available to a frightened person, the same one I'd done to Pip all year by not seeing her, the same one Caro did to me with the gold-cracked cup. "I just wanted somebody else to have to know it. While it happens. So you're not the only one in here who knows." And she stepped out of the walkway, and let me pass, and I will be grateful to Marnie Wexley-Tate for the rest of my life for the worst thing she ever said to me, which was the only honest goodbye I got.

*

Iwan was by the ramp.

I hadn't expected him and I should have. He'd driven up for the horse, for the delivery, the practice vet seeing his last job through, and he stood in the dark by the lorry with his wax jacket that's never fully dry and his big soft kind ruined hands, and he could not look at me, and I could not look at him, and that was the whole transaction, the not-looking, the same not-looking we'd done at the fridge with the tube of blood in it that could end everyone in the building and never would, because the people who could ask were the people it would damn. The blood was on a shelf somewhere in Leominster, cold, dated, the one honest body in the whole arrangement, sealed up where no one would ever look. Nobody was going to be punished. Not Iwan, not Caro, not Raef, not me. We were all going to walk out into the October dark with the thing we'd come for and none of us with the thing we'd wanted. Iwan put his hand on the horse's quarters as he went past, gentle, the realest thing about him, and said, "He's a lovely mover," in the soft Welsh, to no one, and turned away into the dark, ashamed, and unpunished, and kind, all at once, the way he was the whole way through.

And then the ramp.

There was Hob's rug folded over the partition inside, the old worn New Zealand off the retired cob who'd never earned a penny and would outlive every horse in the valley out of spite, sent up in the box because Hob was the only one Saracen ever settled next to, the worthless body comforting the priced one on the way out, and the smell of it would be in the lorry the whole drive to the Gulf, and that was Glenys, that was the last thing she could do and she'd done it, and I'm not going to be clever about it.

I led him to the bottom of the ramp.

He stopped. Of course he stopped. A ramp, a chute, a dark box he couldn't see the back of — the oldest fear, the trapped-and-cornered, the thing they'd tried to load him into sideways with three men on the ropes in March, the thing that started the whole of it. He stopped at the bottom of the ramp and went still, and stopped blinking, and the weight came back onto the left where the open dark of the showground was, and under the right eye the muscle started its small terrible work, the flicker, the tic, the last second the horse was still asking, and the question came up the rope into my hand clear as a child's, clearer than anything anyone had said to me all night, *do I have to go in there on my own, or are you here. is the dark held. are you here.*

And I had it in me, one more time, the gift, the whole of it, the thing I had left, and I could have done it. I could have given him the rope soft and waited the half-stride and told him with the whole of me that the dark was held, that the box was safe, that I was here, and he'd have believed me, he'd have walked up the ramp into the lorry that was taking him to a country he'd never come back from, calm, trusting, on my word, because I'd taught him my word was the floor of the world — and it would have been the last and biggest and kindest lie I ever told him, and it would have worked, costless, perfect, the way they all worked now.

I stood at the bottom of the ramp with the flicker going under his eye and the question in my hand and I did not answer it.

Not because it was the right thing. There wasn't a right thing left; I'd used them all up. I just could not make my hands do it one more time. The one mercy I had left in me, the only one, was to not be the voice that walked him onto the lorry — to let him be frightened, truly, for the length of a ramp, rather than spend the very last of him going quiet on my word for the man taking him. I stood there and I let him be afraid and I did not gentle it, and Glenys, who'd watched the whole thing from the dark and known before I did that I'd reach the bottom of the ramp and not be able to, came up past me without a word and took the rope out of my hand, soft, *I've got him,* and she did it. She gave him the dark held. She had thirty years of it. She walked him up the ramp going quiet on her word instead of mine, *there's a boy, there's a good boy then, Sass,* and his near ear went to her and his off ear came down off the fear and he walked up the ramp into the lorry, trusting, calm, on the rope of the woman who would lead him to the bottom of a ramp and no further, and the navy man took the brass headcollar off his shoulder.

*

I stood on the sand.

They put the brass headcollar on him over Glenys's old brown one and took the old brown one off underneath it, the way you do, and the brass buckle caught the strip light, and that was the moment the horse went from being the yard's to being the man's, the second of paper made physical, a buckle changed in the dark at the top of a ramp, and nobody marked it because there was nothing to mark, it was just kit, it was just a headcollar, it was just the most ordinary thing in the world being done to the only thing I ever loved.

He was facing into the lorry, into Hob's rug and the dark, and I was on the sand at the bottom, and I went up the ramp the once, the last time, while the navy man clipped the trailer tie and the engine somewhere coughed and turned over, and I put my hand flat on his neck under the plaited crest, on the warm wet of him gone dark from poll to flank, on the scar, the hand's-width of pale hairless skin nobody could ever explain, the one part of him I could never read, the page torn out of the manual before Tatefield, before me, before any of it — and he was blowing still, the great long blows, the whole body going like a bellows, the last of him spent on the inside of his own head twice over for a boy on his back — and the near ear came round off the strange dark and the engine and the brass on his face and found me, and tipped back, and stayed.

He didn't know.

He stood in the lorry that was taking him away with my hand on his neck and the near ear tipped back to me listening for the next thing I'd ask, trusting the hands, *are you here,* and he did not know one single thing about what the brass headcollar meant or where the dark went or that the boy on the end of the trust had just won everything and lost him in the one motion of the same hands, and I took my hand off his neck, and the off-hind shifted under him, the white coronet bright the once under the strip light, and I went down the ramp, and they put it up behind me, and that was the last of it.

*

Caro Wexley-Tate was alive then, which I sometimes have to make myself believe was true.

I never rode in a Grand Prix again. People ask me why and I let them think it was the money, or the lack of a horse, or that the gift went, and it's none of those, the gift never went, that's the trouble, the gift never goes — and I have never told one of them the real reason, the same as I've never told anyone the half-stride, the same as I went plain about him the once and put the armour back on, because there are things you cannot say without pricing them, and I will not price this one. I'll only say that some mornings still, in the half-second before sleep, with no horse under me and none coming, I feel the off-hind change.
